931

932 <section id="_1">

933

934 <h2>

935General Information About Childhood Non-Hodgkin Lymphoma (NHL)</h2>

936

937 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

938<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

939<h6>

940In This Section</h6>

941<ul>

942<li>

943<a href="#_536_toc">

944Incidence </a>

945</li>

946<li>

947<a href="#_990_toc">

948Risk Factors</a>

949</li>

950<li>

951<a href="#_762_toc">

952Anatomy</a>

953</li>

954<li>

955<a href="#_764_toc">

956Diagnostic Evaluation</a>

957</li>

958<li>

959<a href="#_552_toc">

960Prognosis and Prognostic Factors for Childhood NHL<ul>

961<li>

962<a href="#_779_toc">

963Response to therapy</a>

964</li>

965<li>

966<a href="#_773_toc">

967Stage at diagnosis/minimal disseminated disease (MDD)</a>

968</li>

969<li>

970<a href="#_769_toc">

971Sites of disease at diagnosis</a>

972</li>

973<li>

974<a href="#_767_toc">

975Age</a>

976</li>

977<li>

978<a href="#_934_toc">

979Immune response to tumor</a>

980</li>

981</ul>

982</a>

983</li>

984</ul>

985</nav>

986<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

987<a href="/types/lymphoma/patient/child-nhl-treatment-pdq">

988Go to Patient Version</a>

989</div>

990<p id="_248" tabindex="-1">

991Dramatic improvements in survival have been achieved for children and adolescents with cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9921</a>

993] Between 1975 and 2020, childhood cancer mortality decreased by more than 50%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9941</a>

995-<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9963</a>

997] Between 2012 and 2018, the 5-year relative survival rate was approximately 90% for children and adolescents younger than 20 years with NHL.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9983</a>

999] In 2020, there were an estimated 30,500 survivors of childhood and adolescent NHL in the United States.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

10004</a>

1001] </p>

1002<p id="_2391" tabindex="-1">

1003Childhood and adolescent cancer survivors require close monitoring because cancer therapy side effects may persist or develop months or years after treatment. For information about the incidence, type, and monitoring of late effects in childhood and adolescent cancer survivors, see <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq">

1004Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer</a>

1005.</p>

1006<p id="_1094" tabindex="-1">

1007On the basis of immunophenotype, molecular biology, and clinical response to treatment, most NHL cases occurring in childhood and adolescence fall into three categories: </p>

1008<div class="pdq-content-list">

1009<ol id="_1095">

1010<li>

1011<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_785">

1012Aggressive mature B-cell NHL</a>

1013 (Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia, diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, and primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma).</li>

1014<li>

1015<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_322">

1016Lymphoblastic lymphoma</a>

1017.</li>

1018<li>

1019<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_330">

1020Anaplastic large cell lymphoma</a>

1021.</li>

1022</ol>

1023</div>

1024<p id="_1710" tabindex="-1">

1025Other rare types of pediatric NHL include the following:</p>

1026<div class="pdq-content-list">

1027<ul id="_1711">

1028<li>

1029<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1994">

1030Pediatric gray zone lymphoma</a>

1031.</li>

1032<li>

1033<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_855">

1034Pediatric-type follicular lymphoma</a>

1035.</li>

1036<li>

1037<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_859">

1038Marginal zone lymphoma (including mucosa-associated lymphoid tissue [MALT] lymphoma</a>

1039).</li>

1040<li>

1041<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_863">

1042Primary central nervous system (CNS) lymphoma</a>

1043.</li>

1044<li>

1045<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_867">

1046Peripheral T-cell lymphoma</a>

1047.</li>

1048<li>

1049<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_873">

1050Cutaneous T-cell lymphoma/mycosis fungoides</a>

1051.</li>

1052</ul>

1053</div>

1054<section id="_536">

1055<h3 id="_536_toc">

1056Incidence </h3>

1057<p id="_537" tabindex="-1">

1058Lymphoma (Hodgkin lymphoma and NHL) is the third most common childhood

1059malignancy, and NHL accounts for approximately 7% of

1060cancers in children younger than 20 years in high-income countries.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

10613</a>

1062]</p>

1063<p id="_726" tabindex="-1">

1064The following factors affect the incidence of NHL in children and adolescents:[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

10652</a>

1066,<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

10673</a>

1068]</p>

1069<div class="pdq-content-list">

1070<ul id="_727">

1071<li>

1072<strong>

1073Geographic location:</strong>

1074 In the United States,

1075about 1,200 new cases of NHL are diagnosed each year in children and adolescents younger than 20 years.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

10765</a>

1077] The incidence is

1078approximately 13 cases per 1 million people per year.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

10792</a>

1080,<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

10813</a>

1082] <p id="_989" tabindex="-1">

1083In sub-Saharan Africa, the incidence of Epstein-Barr virus (EBV)&#8211;induced Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia is tenfold to twentyfold higher than the incidence in the United States, resulting in a much higher incidence of NHL.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

10846</a>

1085] </p>

1086</li>

1087<li>

1088<strong>

1089Race:</strong>

1090 The incidence of NHL is higher in White people than in Black people. Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia is more common in non-Hispanic White people (3.2 cases/million person-years) than in Hispanic White people (2 cases/million person-years).[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

10917</a>

1092]</li>

1093<li>

1094<strong>

1095Age:</strong>

1096 Although there is no sharp age peak, childhood NHL

1097occurs most commonly in the second decade of life.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

10982</a>

1099,<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

11003</a>

1101] NHL occurs infrequently in children younger than 3 years [<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

11022</a>

1103,<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

11043</a>

1105] and is very rare in infants (1% in Berlin-Frankfurt-M&#252;nster [BFM] trials from 1986 to 2002).[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

11068</a>

1107] <p id="_2392" tabindex="-1">

1108The incidence of NHL is increasing overall because of a slight increase in the incidence for patients aged 15 to 19 years. Conversely, the incidence of NHL in children younger than 15 years has remained constant over the past several decades.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

11093</a>

1110]</p>

1111</li>

1112<li>

1113<strong>

1114Sex:</strong>

1115 Childhood NHL is more common in males than in females, with the exception of primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma, in which the incidence is almost the same in males and females.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

11169</a>

1117,<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

111810</a>

1119] According to data from the National Childhood Cancer Registry from 2014 to 2018, the incidence of Burkitt lymphoma was 2.4 cases per 1 million children and adolescents younger than 20 years. Males have a higher incidence of Burkitt lymphoma than females (3.9 cases vs. 0.9 cases per 1 million).[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

11203</a>

1121] The incidence of diffuse large B-cell lymphoma increases with age in both males and females. The incidence of lymphoblastic lymphoma remains relatively constant across ages for both males and females.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

11229</a>

1123]</li>

1124</ul>

1125</div>

1126<p id="_1836" tabindex="-1">

1127The incidence and age distribution of histologic types of NHL according to sex is described in <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_540">

1128Table 1</a>

1129.</p>

1130<table id="_540" class="table-default expandable-container">

1131<caption>

1291</section>

1292<section id="_990">

1293<h3 id="_990_toc">

1294Risk Factors</h3>

1295<p id="_542" tabindex="-1">

1296Relatively little data on the epidemiology of childhood NHL have been published. However, known risk factors include the following:</p>

1297<div class="pdq-content-list">

1298<ul id="_761">

1299<li>

1300<strong>

1301EBV:</strong>

1302 EBV is associated with most cases of NHL occurring in the immunodeficient population.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

13039</a>

1304] Almost all endemic Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia in Africa is associated with EBV; however, approximately 15% of cases in Europe and the United States will have EBV detectable in the tumor tissue.[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

130511</a>

1306]</li>

1307<li>

1308<strong>

1309Immunodeficiency:</strong>

1310 Immunodeficiency, both congenital and acquired (HIV or posttransplant immunodeficiency), increases the risk of NHL.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

13119</a>

1312] U.S. transplant and cancer registries show that posttransplant lymphoproliferative disease (PTLD) accounts for about 3% of all pediatric NHL diagnoses; 65% of PTLDs are diffuse large B-cell lymphoma histology, and 9% are Burkitt histology.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

131312</a>

1314]</li>

1315<li>

1316<strong>

1317DNA repair syndromes:</strong>

1318 The incidence of NHL is increased in patients with DNA repair syndromes, including ataxia-telangiectasia, Nijmegen breakage syndrome, and constitutional mismatch repair deficiency.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

131913</a>

1320] The distribution of NHL subtypes differs among the DNA repair syndromes, as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

132113</a>

1322] <div class="pdq-content-list">

1323<ul id="_2011" class="list-dash">

1324<li>

1325For patients with ataxia-telangiectasia, mature B-cell NHL accounts for most of the NHL cases.</li>

1326<li>

1327For patients with Nijmegen breakage syndrome, mature B-cell NHL is the most common lymphoma; however, T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma and peripheral T-cell lymphoma are each observed in approximately 20% of cases.</li>

1328<li>

1329For patients with constitutional mismatch repair deficiency, most cases are T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma.</li>

1330</ul>

1331</div>

1332 </li>

1333<li>

1334<strong>

1335Previous neoplasm:</strong>

1336 NHL presenting as a subsequent neoplasm is rare in pediatrics. A retrospective review of the German Childhood Cancer Registry identified 2,968 children who were newly diagnosed with cancer, 11 (0.3%) of whom were later diagnosed with NHL as a subsequent neoplasm before age 19 years.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

133714</a>

1338] In this small cohort, outcomes were similar to those of patients with de novo NHL who were treated with standard therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

133914</a>

1340]</li>

1341</ul>

1342</div>

1343</section>

1344<section id="_762">

1345<h3 id="_762_toc">

1346Anatomy</h3>

1347<p id="_763" tabindex="-1">

1348Unlike adults with NHL who present most often with nodal disease, children typically have extranodal disease involving the mediastinum, abdomen, and/or head and neck, as well as the bone marrow or CNS. In developed countries, Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia occurs in the abdomen in approximately 60% of cases, with 15% to 20% of cases arising in the head and neck.[<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

134915</a>

1350,<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

135116</a>

1352] This high incidence of extranodal disease substantiates the use of the Murphy staging system for pediatric NHL, instead of the Ann Arbor staging system.</p>

1353</section>

1354<section id="_764">

1355<h3 id="_764_toc">

1356Diagnostic Evaluation</h3>

1357<p id="_765" tabindex="-1">

1358The following tests and procedures are used to diagnose childhood NHL:</p>

1359<div class="pdq-content-list">

1360<ul id="_922">

1361<li>

1362History and physical examination.</li>

1363<li>

1364Pathologic examination of tumor cells.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1365<ul id="_923" class="list-dash">

1366<li>

1367Immunophenotyping by immunohistochemistry and/or flow cytometry.</li>

1368<li>

1369Cytogenetics and/or fluorescence <em>

1370in situ</em>

1371 hybridization (FISH).</li>

1372</ul>

1373</div>

1374</li>

1375<li>

1376Bone marrow biopsy and aspiration.</li>

1377<li>

1378Lumbar puncture with cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) cytology.</li>

1379<li>

1380Total-body imaging (e.g., computed tomography scan, positron emission tomography, and magnetic resonance imaging).</li>

1381<li>

1382Measurement of serum electrolytes, lactate dehydrogenase (LDH), uric acid, blood urea nitrogen (BUN), and creatinine.</li>

1383<li>

1384Liver function tests.</li>

1385</ul>

1386</div>

1387</section>

1388<section id="_552">

1389<h3 id="_552_toc">

1390Prognosis and Prognostic Factors for Childhood NHL</h3>

1391<p id="_553" tabindex="-1">

1392In high-income countries and with current treatments, more than 80% of children and adolescents with NHL will survive at least 5

1393years, although outcome depends on a

1394number of factors, including clinical stage and histology.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

139517</a>

1396] </p>

1397<p id="_573" tabindex="-1">

1398Prognostic factors for childhood NHL include the following:</p>

1399<div class="pdq-content-list">

1400<ul id="_766">

1401<li>

1402<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_779">

1403Response to therapy</a>

1404.</li>

1405<li>

1406<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_773">

1407Stage at diagnosis/presence of minimal disseminated disease (MDD)</a>

1408.</li>

1409<li>

1410<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_769">

1411Sites of disease at diagnosis</a>

1412.</li>

1413<li>

1414<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_767">

1415Age</a>

1416.</li>

1417<li>

1418<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_934">

1419Immune response to tumor</a>

1420.</li>

1421</ul>

1422</div>

1423<p id="_1104" tabindex="-1">

1424For more information about the tumor biology and genomic alterations associated with each type of NHL, some of which are being evaluated as potential prognostic biomarkers, see the following sections:</p>

1425<div class="pdq-content-list">

1426<ul id="_1100">

1427<li>

1428Aggressive mature B-cell lymphoma.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1429<ul id="_1101" class="list-dash">

1430<li>

1431<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_793">

1432Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia</a>

1433.</li>

1434<li>

1435<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_807">

1436Diffuse large B-cell lymphoma</a>

1437.</li>

1438<li>

1439<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_916">

1440Primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma</a>

1441.</li>

1442</ul>

1443</div>

1444</li>

1445<li>

1446<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_823">

1447Lymphoblastic lymphoma</a>

1448.</li>

1449<li>

1450<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_835">

1451Anaplastic large cell lymphoma</a>

1452.</li>

1453</ul>

1454</div>

1455<section id="_779">

1456<h4 id="_779_toc">

1457Response to therapy</h4>

1458<p id="_780" tabindex="-1">

1459Response to therapy in pediatric lymphoma is one of the most important prognostic markers. Regardless of histology, pediatric NHL that is refractory to first-line therapy has a very poor prognosis,[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

146018</a>

1461-<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

146222</a>

1463] with the exception of anaplastic large cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

146418</a>

1465,<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

146623</a>

1467]</p>

1468<div class="pdq-content-list">

1469<ul id="_782">

1470<li>

1471<strong>

1472Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia:</strong>

1473 One of the most important predictive factors is response to the initial prophase treatment; poor responders (i.e., &lt;20% resolution of disease) have an event-free survival (EFS) rate of 30%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

147424</a>

1475,<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

147625</a>

1477] However, poor response to initial treatment was not found to be an adverse prognostic factor in the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01516580">

1478Inter B-NHL Ritux 2010 (NCT01516580)</a>

1479 trial, in which patients were treated with more effective therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

148026</a>

1481]</li>

1482<li>

1483<strong>

1484Lymphoblastic lymphoma:</strong>

1485 The presence of a residual mediastinal mass at day 33 or at the end of induction was not found to be associated with a decreased survival in the BFM 90-95 studies, but all patients with less than 70% reduction at the end of induction had therapy intensified.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

148627</a>

1487]</li>

1488</ul>

1489</div>

1490<p id="_1717" tabindex="-1">

1491International pediatric NHL response criteria have been proposed but require prospective evaluation. The clinical utility of these new criteria are under investigation.[<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

149228</a>

1493]</p>

1494<p id="_925" tabindex="-1">

1495In contrast to the prognostic value of minimal residual disease (MRD) in patients with acute leukemia, the prognostic value of MRD after therapy is initiated remains uncertain and requires further investigation in pediatric patients with NHL.</p>

1496<div class="pdq-content-list">

1497<ul id="_926">

1498<li>

1499<strong>

1500Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia:</strong>

1501 One study suggested an inferior outcome for patients with Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia who had detectable MRD after induction chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

150229</a>

1503] However, other studies found that detectable MRD at the end of induction was not prognostic, possibly because of the low number of relapses in patients with disease detected in the blood or bone marrow at diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

150430</a>

1505,<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

150631</a>

1507]</li>

1508<li>

1509<strong>

1510Anaplastic large cell lymphoma:</strong>

1511 A retrospective analysis of a collaborative European study showed that after induction, MRD-negative patients had a relapse risk of approximately 20% and an overall survival (OS) rate of approximately 90%. By contrast, MRD-positive patients had a relapse risk of 81% and an OS rate of 65% (<em>

1512P</em>

1513 &lt; .001). The presence of MRD is significantly associated with uncommon histologic subtypes containing small cell and/or lymphohistiocytic components.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

151432</a>

1515][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1516Level of evidence B4</a>

1517] </li>

1518</ul>

1519</div>

1520</section>

1521<section id="_773">

1522<h4 id="_773_toc">

1523Stage at diagnosis/minimal disseminated disease (MDD)</h4>

1524<p id="_774" tabindex="-1">

1525In general, patients with low-stage disease (i.e., single extra-abdominal/extrathoracic tumor or totally resected intra-abdominal tumor) have an excellent prognosis (5-year survival rate of approximately 90%), regardless of histology.[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

152624</a>

1527,<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

152827</a>

1529,<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

153033</a>

1531-<a href="#cit/section_1.35">

153235</a>

1533] Apart from this finding, the outcome by clinical stage, using appropriate therapy on the basis of risk stratification, does not differ significantly.</p>

1534<p id="_1072" tabindex="-1">

1535A surrogate for tumor burden, specifically elevated levels of LDH, has been shown to be prognostic in many studies.[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

153624</a>

1537,<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

153833</a>

1539,<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

154036</a>

1541]</p>

1542<p id="_1075" tabindex="-1">

1543MDD is generally defined as submicroscopic bone marrow involvement that is present at diagnosis. MDD is generally detected by sensitive methods such as flow cytometry or reverse transcription&#8211;polymerase chain reaction (RT-PCR). Patients with morphologically involved bone marrow with more than 5% lymphoma cells are considered to have stage IV disease.</p>

1544<div class="pdq-content-list">

1545<ul id="_927">

1546<li>

1547<strong>

1548Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia:</strong>

1549 The role of MDD remains to be defined. One study suggested that MDD is predictive of outcome,[<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

155037</a>

1551,<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

155238</a>

1553] while another study did not.[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

155430</a>

1555]</li>

1556<li>

1557<strong>

1558T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma:</strong>

1559 A Children's Oncology Group (COG) study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00004228">

1560A5971 [NCT00004228]</a>

1561) demonstrated a 2-year EFS rate of 91% for patients who had an MDD level by flow cytometry lower than 1% (n = 73), compared with 68% if the MDD level exceeded 1% (n = 26), and 52% if the MDD was 5% and higher (n = 9).[<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

156239</a>

1563]<p id="_2393" tabindex="-1">

1564 An Associazione Italiana Ematologia Oncologia Pediatrica (AIEOP) study used an MDD cutoff level of 3% by flow cytometry. The study observed a 5-year EFS rate of 60% for patients with MDD greater than 3% versus 83% for the remaining patients (<em>

1565P</em>

1566 = .04).[<a href="#cit/section_1.40">

156740</a>

1568]</p>

1569<p id="_2394" tabindex="-1">

1570 The largest experience with MDD for T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma (n = 273) is from the COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00408005">

1571AALL0434 (NCT00408005)</a>

1572 study, in which MDD had no prognostic impact. Patients with bone marrow MDD levels of less than 1% had an EFS rate of 82.4% compared with 89.5% for those with MDD levels of 1% or higher (<em>

1573P</em>

1574 = .3084).[<a href="#cit/section_1.41">

157541</a>

1576]</p>

1577</li>

1578<li>

1579<strong>

1580Anaplastic large cell lymphoma:</strong>

1581 Multiple studies have found that the presence of MDD using molecular diagnostic methods to detect the <em class="gene-name">

1582NPM</em>

1583::<em class="gene-name">

1584ALK</em>

1585 gene transcript is associated with increased risk of treatment failure.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

158632</a>

1587][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1588Level of evidence B4</a>

1589]; [<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

159042</a>

1591-<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

159245</a>

1593] MDD is commonly quantified by normalizing the number of <em class="gene-name">

1594NPM</em>

1595::<em class="gene-name">

1596ALK</em>

1597 transcripts to 10<span class="sup">

15984</span>

1599 copies <em class="gene-name">

1600ABL1</em>

1601, with 10 normalized copy number (NCN) <em class="gene-name">

1602NPM</em>

1603::<em class="gene-name">

1604ALK</em>

1605 transcripts being the most common cut point to compare the prognostic impact of MDD.[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

160642</a>

1607-<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

160844</a>

1609] <em class="gene-name">

1610NPM</em>

1611::<em class="gene-name">

1612ALK</em>

1613 transcript levels in blood and bone marrow are comparable.[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

161442</a>

1615,<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

161645</a>

1617] Examples of studies with MDD results include the following:<div class="pdq-content-list">

1618<ul id="_2006">

1619<li>

1620Among 74 patients treated on the NHL-BFM-95 and <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006455">

1621ALCL99 (NCT00006455)</a>

1622 trials, 16 patients with 10 NCNs <em class="gene-name">

1623NPM</em>

1624::<em class="gene-name">

1625ALK</em>

1626 in bone marrow had a cumulative incidence of relapse of 71% (&#177; 14%) compared with 18% (&#177; 6%) for the 59 patients with 10 or fewer NCNs.[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

162742</a>

1628] The presence of MDD was significantly higher in patients with stages III to IV disease and in patients with small cell and other uncommon histologies.[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

162942</a>

1630]</li>

1631<li>

1632For a cohort of 420 patients treated on the ALCL99 trial, MDD results by qualitative PCR were available for 162 patients. MDD was positive in either bone marrow or blood for 54% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_1.45">

163345</a>

1634] The 10-year progression-free survival (PFS) rate was 83% for patients with negative MDD compared with 62% for patients with positive MDD. In multivariate analysis, MDD and histologic subtype (small cell/lymphohistiocytic) were the two factors significantly associated with inferior outcome.</li>

1635<li>

1636The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01979536">

1637ANHL12P1 (NCT01979536)</a>

1638 study evaluated the addition of either brentuximab vedotin or crizotinib to ALCL99 trial chemotherapy. The study confirmed the poor prognosis associated with MDD (defined as &gt;10 NCNs) in the peripheral blood at diagnosis. In each arm, approximately 40% of patients had MDD, and their 2-year EFS rate was about 60%. The remaining patients without detectable MDD had a 2-year EFS rate of approximately 85%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.46">

163946</a>

1640,<a href="#cit/section_1.47">

164147</a>

1642]</li>

1643<li>

1644The prognostic significance of MDD is modified by MRD positivity after one course of chemotherapy. In a study of 180 patients, the presence of MDD was associated with a cumulative incidence of relapse of 46%, compared with a cumulative incidence of relapse of 15% for patients with no bone marrow involvement.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

164532</a>

1646][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1647Level of evidence B4</a>

1648] Among 26 MDD-positive/MRD-positive patients, the cumulative incidence of relapse was significantly higher (81% &#177; 8%) than in the 26 MDD-positive/MRD-negative patients (31% &#177; 9%) and the 77 MDD-negative patients (15% &#177; 5%) (<em>

1649P</em>

1650 &lt; .001).</li>

1651<li>

1652Digital PCR methods have been applied to evaluating MDD for patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma to facilitate harmonization between laboratories and across studies. In a study of 91 patients, <em class="gene-name">

1653NPM</em>

1654::<em class="gene-name">

1655ALK</em>

1656 MDD levels by digital PCR correlated well with estimates by quantitative PCR.[<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

165743</a>

1658] The 3-year EFS rate was 33% (&#177; 11%) for the 18 patients with more than 10 NCN <em class="gene-name">

1659NPM</em>

1660::<em class="gene-name">

1661ALK</em>

1662 transcripts by digital PCR, compared with a 3-year EFS rate of 79% (&#177; 5%) for the 73 patients with 10 or more NCN <em class="gene-name">

1663NPM</em>

1664::<em class="gene-name">

1665ALK</em>

1666 transcripts (<em>

1667P</em>

1668 &lt; .0001).</li>

1669</ul>

1670</div>

1671<p id="_1673" tabindex="-1">

1672The presence of MDD is significantly associated with uncommon histologic subtypes containing small cell and/or lymphohistiocytic components.[<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

167342</a>

1674]</p>

1675</li>

1676</ul>

1677</div>

1678</section>

1679<section id="_769">

1680<h4 id="_769_toc">

1681Sites of disease at diagnosis</h4>

1682<p id="_770" tabindex="-1">

1683In pediatric NHL, some sites of disease appear to have prognostic value, including the following:</p>

1684<div class="pdq-content-list">

1685<ul id="_728">

1686<li>

1687<strong>

1688Bone marrow and CNS:</strong>

1689 Bone marrow and CNS involvement at diagnosis usually requires more intensive therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

169026</a>

1691,<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

169233</a>

1693,<a href="#cit/section_1.48">

169448</a>

1695,<a href="#cit/section_1.49">

169649</a>

1697] However, with appropriate risk-stratified therapy, patients with bone marrow and/or CNS involvement can achieve similar outcomes to patients without marrow and/or CNS involvement.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

169826</a>

1699]</li>

1700<li>

1701<strong>

1702Head and neck: </strong>

1703For patients with mature B-cell NHL, OS is comparable to that observed for patients with primary tumors at other sites. Head and neck primary tumors are associated with higher rates of disseminated and CNS disease and lower rates of LDH levels that were more than twofold higher than the upper limit of normal. Childhood NHL of the head and neck site was not associated with inferior OS.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

170416</a>

1705] </li>

1706<li>

1707<strong>

1708Mediastinum:</strong>

1709 Mediastinal involvement in children and adolescents with nonlymphoblastic NHL results in an inferior outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

171017</a>

1711,<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

171224</a>

1713,<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

171433</a>

1715,<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

171636</a>

1717] In children and young adults with primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma, series have reported 3-year EFS rates of 50% to 70%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

171833</a>

1719,<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

172036</a>

1721,<a href="#cit/section_1.50">

172250</a>

1723] However, studies using the dose-adjusted (DA)&#8211;EPOCH protocol (etoposide, prednisone, vincristine, and doxorubicin) with rituximab have reported EFS rates higher than 80%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.51">

172451</a>

1725,<a href="#cit/section_1.52">

172652</a>

1727]</li>

1728<li>

1729<strong>

1730Viscera:</strong>

1731 For anaplastic large cell lymphoma, a retrospective study by the European Intergroup for Childhood NHL (EICNHL) found a high-risk group of patients defined by involvement of mediastinum, skin, or viscera.[<a href="#cit/section_1.49">

173249</a>

1733] In a subsequent study analysis from EICNHL utilizing biologic risk factors, the clinical risk features were not found to be significant.[<a href="#cit/section_1.53">

173453</a>

1735] In the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002590">

1736CCG-5941 (NCT00002590)</a>

1737 study for patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma, these clinical risk factors could not be confirmed; only bone marrow involvement predicted inferior PFS.[<a href="#cit/section_1.54">

173854</a>

1739][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1740Level of evidence B4</a>

1741] </li>

1742<li>

1743<strong>

1744Bone:</strong>

1745 Although previously thought to be a poor prognostic site, patients with NHL arising in bone have an excellent prognosis, regardless of histology.[<a href="#cit/section_1.55">

174655</a>

1747,<a href="#cit/section_1.56">

174856</a>

1749] </li>

1750<li>

1751<strong>

1752Skin:</strong>

1753 The prognostic implication of skin involvement is limited to anaplastic large cell lymphoma and depends on whether the disease is localized to skin. Patients with <em class="gene-name">

1754ALK</em>

1755-negative, skin-limited anaplastic large cell lymphoma appear to have an excellent prognosis. However, studies from EICNHL and the COG have demonstrated that skin involvement in systemic anaplastic large cell lymphoma does not appear to have positive prognostic value.[<a href="#cit/section_1.53">

175653</a>

1757,<a href="#cit/section_1.54">

175854</a>

1759]</li>

1760<li>

1761<strong>

1762Testicle:</strong>

1763 Testicular involvement does not affect prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

176427</a>

1765,<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

176657</a>

1767] </li>

1768</ul>

1769</div>

1770</section>

1771<section id="_767">

1772<h4 id="_767_toc">

1773Age</h4>

1774<p id="_768" tabindex="-1">

1775 NHL in infants is rare (1% in BFM trials from 1986 to 2002).[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

17768</a>

1777] In this retrospective review, the outcome for infants was inferior compared with the outcome for older patients with NHL.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

17788</a>

1779]</p>

1780<p id="_574" tabindex="-1">

1781Adolescents have also been reported to have outcomes inferior to those of younger children.[<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

178215</a>

1783,<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

178417</a>

1785,<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

178658</a>

1787] This adverse effect of age appears to be most pronounced for adolescents with diffuse large B-cell lymphoma and, to a lesser degree, T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

178817</a>

1789,<a href="#cit/section_1.58">

179058</a>

1791] Conversely, for patients with Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia, adolescent age (&#8805;15 years) was not an independent risk factor for inferior outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

179226</a>

1793,<a href="#cit/section_1.36">

179436</a>

1795] Adolescents with mature B-cell lymphoma who are treated using pediatric protocols have a superior outcome compared with those treated with adult regimens (EFS rates, 88% vs. 66%).[<a href="#cit/section_1.59">

179659</a>

1797][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1798Level of evidence C2</a>

1799]</p>

1800</section>

1801<section id="_934">

1802<h4 id="_934_toc">

1803Immune response to tumor</h4>

1804<p id="_935" tabindex="-1">

1805An immune response against the ALK protein (i.e., anti-ALK antibody titer) appeared to correlate with lower clinical stage and predicted relapse risk but not OS.[<a href="#cit/section_1.60">

180660</a>

1807] A study by the EICNHL, which combined the level of anti-ALK antibody with MDD, demonstrated that patients with newly diagnosed anaplastic large cell lymphoma could be stratified into three risk groups, with the following PFS rates:[<a href="#cit/section_1.53">

180853</a>

1809]</p>

1810<div class="pdq-content-list">

1811<ul id="_2395">

1812<li>

1813 28% for the high-risk group (MDD positive and antibody titer &#8804;1/750). </li>

1814<li>

181568% for the intermediate-risk group (all remaining patients).</li>

1816<li>

181793% for the low-risk group (MDD negative and antibody titer &gt;1/750) (<em>

1818P</em>

1819 &lt; .0001).</li>

1820</ul>

1821</div>

1822<p id="_2396" tabindex="-1">

1823 In a cohort of Japanese patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma who were treated on the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006455">

1824ALCL99 (NCT00006455)</a>

1825 study, comparable results were obtained for a three-category risk classification algorithm.[<a href="#cit/section_1.44">

182644</a>

1827] For a cohort of 180 patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma who were treated on several European studies, low anti-ALK antibody titer retained prognostic significance in a multivariate analysis, along with MDD, MRD, and uncommon histology (small cell and others).[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

182832</a>

1829][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1830Level of evidence B4</a>

1831]</p>

1832</section>

2084 <section id="_8">

2085

2086 <h2>

2087Histopathologic and Molecular Classification of Childhood NHL</h2>

2088

2089 <div id="_section_8" class="pdq-sections">

2090<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2091<h6>

2092In This Section</h6>

2093<ul>

2094<li>

2095<a href="#_1077_toc">

2096WHO Classification for NHL</a>

2097</li>

2098</ul>

2099</nav>

2100<p id="_10" tabindex="-1">

2101In children, non-Hodgkin lymphoma (NHL) is distinct from the more common

2102forms of lymphoma observed in adults. While lymphomas in adults are more commonly

2103low or intermediate grade, almost all NHL that occurs in children is high grade.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

21041</a>

2105,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

21062</a>

2107] The World Health Organization (WHO) classifies NHL according to the following features:[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

21082</a>

2109] </p>

2110<div class="pdq-content-list">

2111<ul id="_937">

2112<li>

2113Phenotype (i.e., B-lineage, T-lineage, or natural killer [NK] cell lineage).</li>

2114<li>

2115Cell differentiation (i.e., precursor vs. mature).</li>

2116</ul>

2117</div>

2118<p id="_284" tabindex="-1">

2119On the basis of the WHO classification, most NHL cases in childhood and adolescence fall into the following three categories: </p>

2120<div class="pdq-content-list">

2121<ol id="_571">

2122<li>

2123<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_785">

2124Aggressive mature B-cell NHL</a>

2125: The most common types are Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia, diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, and primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma. Less common entities included in the WHO 2017 classification that occur in children include Burkitt-like lymphoma with 11q alteration, high-grade B-cell lymphoma&#8211;not otherwise specified, and large B-cell lymphoma with <em class="gene-name">

2126IRF4</em>

2127 rearrangement (LBCL-<em class="gene-name">

2128IRF4</em>

2129).[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

21303</a>

2131]

2132<p id="_1920" tabindex="-1">

2133Compared with treatments for adults, aggressive Burkitt regimens in pediatrics have been used to treat patients with both Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia and large B-cell histologies, resulting in no difference in outcome based on histology.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

21344</a>

2135-<a href="#cit/section_2.8">

21368</a>

2137] The exception is for patients with primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma, who have had inferior outcomes with these regimens.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

21384</a>

2139-<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

21407</a>

2141,<a href="#cit/section_2.9">

21429</a>

2143]</p>

2144<p id="_1921" tabindex="-1">

2145For patients with pediatric Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia, secondary cytogenetic abnormalities, other than <em class="gene-name">

2146MYC</em>

2147 rearrangement, have been associated with an inferior outcome,[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

214810</a>

2149,<a href="#cit/section_2.11">

215011</a>

2151] and cytogenetic abnormalities involving gain of 7q or deletion of 13q may be associated with an inferior outcome on the FAB/LMB-96 chemotherapy protocol.[<a href="#cit/section_2.11">

215211</a>

2153,<a href="#cit/section_2.12">

215412</a>

2155] For pediatric patients with diffuse large B-cell lymphoma and chromosomal rearrangement at <em class="gene-name">

2156MYC</em>

2157 (8q24), outcomes may be worse.[<a href="#cit/section_2.11">

215811</a>

2159]</p>

2160<p id="_2397" tabindex="-1">

2161Results from the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01516580">

2162Inter-B-NHL Ritux 2010 (NCT01516580)</a>

2163 phase III trial showed that the addition of rituximab to chemotherapy for patients with aggressive mature B-cell NHL improved event-free survival (EFS) rates, from 82% to 94%. The small number of treatment failures, resulting from a high EFS rate, make it challenging to confirm these previously identified candidate prognostic biomarkers.[<a href="#cit/section_2.13">

216413</a>

2165]</p>

2166<p id="_1922" tabindex="-1">

2167LBCL-<em class="gene-name">

2168IRF4</em>

2169 is included in the WHO 2017 classification as a provisional entity.[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

21703</a>

2171] LBCL-<em class="gene-name">

2172IRF4</em>

2173 cases have a translocation that juxtaposes the <em class="gene-name">

2174IRF4</em>

2175 oncogene next to one of the immunoglobulin loci and has been associated with a favorable prognosis compared with diffuse large B-cell lymphoma cases lacking this finding.[<a href="#cit/section_2.14">

217614</a>

2177,<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

217815</a>

2179]</p>

2180<p id="_2019" tabindex="-1">

2181For more information about the tumor biology and genomic alterations, see the sections on <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_793">

2182Tumor biology (Genomics of Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia)</a>

2183, <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_807">

2184Tumor biology (Genomics of diffuse large B-cell lymphoma)</a>

2185, and <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_916">

2186Tumor biology (Genomics of primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma)</a>

2187.</p>

2188</li>

2189<li>

2190<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_322">

2191Lymphoblastic lymphoma</a>

2192: This is primarily precursor T-cell lymphoma and, less frequently, precursor B-cell lymphoma. <p id="_2024" tabindex="-1">

2193For more information about the tumor biology and genomic alterations, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_823">

2194Tumor Biology (Genomics of lymphoblastic lymphoma)</a>

2195 section.</p>

2196</li>

2197<li>

2198<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_330">

2199Anaplastic large cell lymphoma</a>

2200: This type includes mature peripheral T-cell/null-cell lymphomas. The null-cell variant is considered to be the same disease in which the cells have lost most of the T-cell antigens.<p id="_1924" tabindex="-1">

2201In adults, <em class="gene-name">

2202ALK</em>

2203-negative disease has an inferior outcome; however, in children, the difference in outcome between <em class="gene-name">

2204ALK</em>

2205-positive and <em class="gene-name">

2206ALK</em>

2207-negative disease has not been demonstrated.[<a href="#cit/section_2.16">

220816</a>

2209-<a href="#cit/section_2.18">

221018</a>

2211] In a series of 375 children and adolescents with systemic <em class="gene-name">

2212ALK</em>

2213-positive anaplastic large cell lymphoma enrolled on the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006455">

2214ALCL99 (NCT00006455)</a>

2215 study, the presence of a small cell or lymphohistiocytic component

2216was observed in 32% of patients and was significantly associated with a high risk of

2217failure in the multivariate analysis, controlling for clinical characteristics.[<a href="#cit/section_2.19">

221819</a>

2219] With longer follow-up, presence of the small cell/lymphohistiocytic pattern maintained its prognostic significance on multivariate analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.20">

222020</a>

2221]</p>

2222<p id="_1925" tabindex="-1">

2223In the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00059839">

2224COG-ANHL0131</a>

2225 (NCT00059839) study, despite a different chemotherapy backbone, patients with the small cell variant of anaplastic large cell lymphoma, as well as other histologic variants, had a significantly increased risk of failure.[<a href="#cit/section_2.18">

222618</a>

2227]</p>

2228<p id="_2020" tabindex="-1">

2229For more information about the tumor biology and genomic alterations, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_835">

2230Tumor Biology (Genomics of anaplastic large cell lymphoma)</a>

2231 section.</p>

2232</li>

2233</ol>

2234</div>

2454<p id="_285" tabindex="-1">

2455Other types of lymphoma, such as the nonanaplastic large cell peripheral T-cell lymphomas (including T/NK lymphomas), cutaneous lymphomas, and indolent B-cell lymphomas (e.g., follicular lymphoma and marginal zone lymphoma), are more commonly seen in adults and rarely occur in children. The most recent WHO classification has designated pediatric-type follicular lymphoma and pediatric nodal marginal zone lymphoma as distinct entities from the counterparts observed in adults.[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

24562</a>

2457]</p>

J Clin Oncol 29 (35): 4669-76, 2011.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=22084369&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=22084369&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2560[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2561</li>

2562<li id="section_2.20">

2563Mussolin L, Le Deley MC, Carraro E, et al.: Prognostic Factors in Childhood Anaplastic Large Cell Lymphoma: Long Term Results of the International ALCL99 Trial. Cancers (Basel) 12 (10): , 2020.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=32987765&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=32987765&amp;dopt=Abstract">

2564[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

2565</li>

2566</ol>

2567</div>

2568

2569 </section>

2570

2571

2572

2573 <section id="_24">

2574

2575 <h2>

2576Stage Information for Childhood NHL</h2>

2577

2578 <div id="_section_24" class="pdq-sections">

2579<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2580<h6>

2581In This Section</h6>

2582<ul>

2583<li>

2584<a href="#_565_toc">

2585Role of Radiographic Imaging in Childhood NHL</a>

2586</li>

2587<li>

2588<a href="#_1712_toc">

2589St. Jude Children's Research Hospital (Murphy) Staging<ul>

2590<li>

2591<a href="#_26_toc">

2592Stage I childhood NHL</a>

2593</li>

2594<li>

2595<a href="#_28_toc">

2596Stage II childhood NHL</a>

2597</li>

2598<li>

2599<a href="#_30_toc">

2600Stage III childhood NHL</a>

2601</li>

2602<li>

2603<a href="#_32_toc">

2604Stage IV childhood NHL</a>

2605</li>

2606</ul>

2607</a>

2608</li>

2609</ul>

2610</nav>

2611<p id="_25" tabindex="-1">

2612The Ann Arbor staging system is used for all lymphomas in adults and for Hodgkin lymphoma in pediatrics. However, the Ann Arbor staging system has less prognostic value in pediatric non-Hodgkin lymphoma (NHL), primarily because of the high incidence of extranodal disease. Therefore, the most widely used

2613staging schema for childhood NHL is that of the St. Jude Children&#8217;s Research Hospital (Murphy Staging).[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

26141</a>

2615] A new staging system defines bone marrow and central nervous system (CNS) involvement using modern techniques to document the presence of malignant cells. However, the basic definitions of bone marrow and CNS disease are essentially the same. The clinical utility of this staging system is under investigation.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

26162</a>

2617]</p>

2618<section id="_565">

2619<h3 id="_565_toc">

2620Role of Radiographic Imaging in Childhood NHL</h3>

2621<p id="_566" tabindex="-1">

2622Radiographic imaging is essential in the staging of patients with NHL. Ultrasonography may be the preferred method for assessment of an abdominal mass, but computed tomography (CT) scan and magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) have been used for staging.</p>

2623<p id="_567" tabindex="-1">

2624The role of functional imaging in pediatric NHL is evolving and still being refined. Gallium scans have been replaced by fluorine F 18-fludeoxyglucose positron emission tomography (PET) scanning, which is now routinely performed at many centers.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

26253</a>

2626] A review of the revised International Workshop Criteria comparing CT imaging alone or CT together with PET imaging demonstrated that the combination of CT and PET imaging was more accurate than CT imaging alone.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

26274</a>

2628,<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

26295</a>

2630] </p>

2631<p id="_1674" tabindex="-1">

2632While the International Working Group (formerly called the International Harmonization Project for PET) response criteria have been attempted in adults, the prognostic value of PET scanning for staging pediatric NHL remains under investigation.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

26333</a>

2634,<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

26356</a>

2636,<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

26377</a>

2638] Data support that PET identifies more abnormalities than does CT scanning,[<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

26398</a>

2640] but it is unclear whether this should be used to upstage pediatric patients and change therapy. The International Working Group has updated their response criteria for malignant lymphoma to include PET, immunohistochemistry, and flow cytometry data.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

26415</a>

2642,<a href="#cit/section_3.9">

26439</a>

2644]</p>

2645</section>

2646<section id="_1712">

2647<h3 id="_1712_toc">

2648St. Jude Children's Research Hospital (Murphy) Staging</h3>

2649<section id="_26">

2650<h4 id="_26_toc">

2651Stage I childhood NHL</h4>

2652<p id="_27" tabindex="-1">

2653In stage I childhood NHL, a single tumor or nodal area is involved, excluding the

2654abdomen and mediastinum.

2655</p>

2656</section>

2657<section id="_28">

2658<h4 id="_28_toc">

2659Stage II childhood NHL</h4>

2660<p id="_29" tabindex="-1">

2661In stage II childhood NHL, disease extent is limited to a single tumor with

2662regional node involvement, two or more tumors or nodal areas involved on one side of the

2663diaphragm, or a primary gastrointestinal tract tumor (completely resected) with or without

2664regional node involvement.

2665</p>

2666</section>

2667<section id="_30">

2668<h4 id="_30_toc">

2669Stage III childhood NHL</h4>

2670<p id="_31" tabindex="-1">

2671In stage III childhood NHL, tumors or involved lymph node areas occur on both

2672sides of the diaphragm. Stage III NHL also includes any primary intrathoracic (mediastinal, pleural, or thymic) disease, extensive primary intra-abdominal

2673disease, or any paraspinal or epidural tumors.

2674</p>

2675</section>

2676<section id="_32">

2677<h4 id="_32_toc">

2678Stage IV childhood NHL</h4>

2679<p id="_33" tabindex="-1">

2680In stage IV childhood NHL, tumors involve the bone marrow and/or CNS, regardless of other sites of involvement. </p>

2681<p id="_305" tabindex="-1">

2682Bone marrow involvement has been defined as 5% or more malignant cells in an otherwise normal bone marrow, with normal peripheral blood counts and smears. Patients with lymphoblastic lymphoma who have more than 25% malignant cells in the bone marrow are usually considered to have leukemia and may be appropriately treated on leukemia clinical trials. </p>

2683<p id="_421" tabindex="-1">

2684 CNS disease in lymphoblastic lymphoma is defined by criteria similar to that used for acute lymphocytic leukemia (i.e., white blood cell count of at least 5/&#956;L and malignant cells in the cerebrospinal fluid [CSF]). For other types of NHL, the definition of CNS disease is any malignant cell present in the CSF regardless of cell count. The Berlin-Frankfurt-M&#252;nster group analyzed the prevalence of CNS involvement in NHL in more than 2,500 patients. Overall, CNS involvement was diagnosed in 6% of patients. CNS involvement (percentage of patients) according to NHL subtype was as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

268510</a>

2686]</p>

2687<div class="pdq-content-list">

2688<ul id="_418">

2689<li>

2690Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia: 8.8%.</li>

2691<li>

2692Precursor B-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma: 5.4%.</li>

2693<li>

2694T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma: 3.7%.</li>

2695<li>

2696Anaplastic large cell lymphoma: 3.3%.</li>

2697<li>

2698Diffuse large B-cell lymphoma: 2.6%.</li>

2699<li>

2700Primary mediastinal large B-cell lymphoma: 0%.</li>

2701</ul>

2702</div>

2703</section>

2704</section>

2754

2755 <section id="_34">

2756

2757 <h2>

2758Treatment Option Overview for Childhood NHL</h2>

2759

2760 <div id="_section_34" class="pdq-sections">

2761<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2762<h6>

2763In This Section</h6>

2764<ul>

2765<li>

2766<a href="#_643_toc">

2767Medical Emergencies<ul>

2768<li>

2769<a href="#_645_toc">

2770Mediastinal masses</a>

2771</li>

2772<li>

2773<a href="#_647_toc">

2774Tumor lysis syndrome</a>

2775</li>

2776</ul>

2777</a>

2778</li>

2779<li>

2780<a href="#_950_toc">

2781Tumor Surveillance</a>

2782</li>

2783</ul>

2784</nav>

2785<p id="_35" tabindex="-1">

2786Many of the advancements in childhood cancer survival have been made by using

2787combinations of known and/or new agents to improve the

2788best available, accepted therapy. Clinical trials in pediatrics are designed

2789to compare potentially better therapy with currently accepted

2790standard therapy. This comparison may be done in a randomized study of two treatment

2791arms or by evaluating a single new treatment and comparing the results with

2792those previously obtained with standard therapy.

2793</p>

2794<p id="_36" tabindex="-1">

2795All children with non-Hodgkin lymphoma (NHL) should be considered for entry

2796into a clinical trial. Treatment planning by a multidisciplinary team of

2797cancer specialists with experience treating tumors of childhood is strongly

2798recommended to determine, coordinate, and implement treatment to achieve

2799optimal survival. Children with NHL should be referred for treatment by a

2800multidisciplinary team of pediatric oncologists at an institution with

2801experience in treating pediatric cancers. Information about ongoing National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical

2802trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

2803NCI website</a>

2804.

2805</p>

2806<p id="_37" tabindex="-1">

2807NHL in children is generally considered to be widely disseminated at diagnosis, even when the tumor is apparently localized; as a result, combination chemotherapy

2808is recommended for most patients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

28091</a>

2810] Exceptions to this treatment strategy include the following:</p>

2811<div class="pdq-content-list">

2812<ul id="_1078">

2813<li>

2814<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_869">

2815Peripheral T-cell lymphoma that is limited to the skin, including anaplastic large cell lymphoma</a>

2816.</li>

2817<li>

2818Indolent mature B-cell lymphomas.</li>

2819<li>

2820<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_857">

2821Pediatric-type follicular lymphoma</a>

2822.</li>

2823<li>

2824<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1659">

2825Posttransplant lymphoproliferative disease</a>

2826 (when immunosuppression can be safely decreased).</li>

2827</ul>

2828</div>

2829<p id="_557" tabindex="-1">

2830In contrast to the treatment of adults with NHL, the use of radiation therapy is limited in children with NHL. Study results include the following:</p>

2831<div class="pdq-content-list">

2832<ul id="_992">

2833<li>

2834Early studies demonstrated that the routine use of radiation had no benefit for patients with low-stage (I or II) NHL.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

28352</a>

2836]</li>

2837<li>

2838Studies have demonstrated that prophylactic central nervous system (CNS) radiation can be omitted in patients with pediatric NHL.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

28393</a>

2840-<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

28416</a>

2842] </li>

2843<li>

2844For patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma and B-cell NHL who present with CNS disease, radiation can also be eliminated.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

28455</a>

2846,<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

28476</a>

2848] </li>

2849</ul>

2850</div>

2851<p id="_993" tabindex="-1">

2852Radiation therapy may have a role in treating patients who have not had a complete response to chemotherapy. Data to support limiting the use of radiation therapy in the treatment of pediatric NHL come from the Childhood Cancer Survivor Study.[<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

28537</a>

2854] This analysis demonstrated that radiation exposure was a significant risk factor for subsequent neoplasms and death in long-term survivors.</p>

2855<p id="_558" tabindex="-1">

2856The treatment of NHL in childhood and adolescence has historically been based on the histologic subtype of the disease. A study by the Children&#8217;s Cancer Group demonstrated that the outcomes for patients with lymphoblastic lymphoma were superior with longer acute lymphoblastic leukemia&#8211;like therapy, while patients with nonlymphoblastic NHL (Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia) had superior outcomes with short, intensive, pulsed therapy; the outcomes for patients with large cell lymphoma were similar with either approach.[<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

28578</a>

2858]</p>

2859<p id="_490" tabindex="-1">

2860Outcomes for children and adolescents with recurrent NHL remain very poor, with the exception of patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

28619</a>

2862-<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

286314</a>

2864] Patients or families who desire additional disease-directed therapy should consider entering trials of novel therapeutic approaches. Regardless of whether a decision is made to pursue disease-directed therapy at the time of progression, palliative care remains a central focus of management. This ensures that quality of life is maximized while attempting to reduce symptoms and stress related to the terminal illness.</p>

2865<p id="_1778" tabindex="-1">

2866<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1716">

2868 describes the treatment options for newly diagnosed and recurrent childhood NHL.</p>

3243<section id="_643">

3244<h3 id="_643_toc">

3245Medical Emergencies</h3>

3246<p id="_644" tabindex="-1">

3247The most common potentially life-threatening

3248clinical situations, seen most frequently in patients with lymphoblastic lymphoma and Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia, are the following: </p>

3249<div class="pdq-content-list">

3250<ul id="_740">

3251<li>

3252<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_645">

3253Mediastinal masses</a>

3254.</li>

3255<li>

3256<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_647">

3257Tumor lysis syndrome</a>

3258.</li>

3259</ul>

3260</div>

3261<section id="_645">

3262<h4 id="_645_toc">

3263Mediastinal masses</h4>

3264<p id="_646" tabindex="-1">

3265Patients with large mediastinal masses are at risk of tracheal compression, superior vena caval compression, large pleural and pericardial effusions, and right and left ventricular outflow compression. Thus, cardiac or respiratory arrest is a significant risk, particularly if the patient is placed in a supine position for procedures such as computed tomography (CT) scans or echocardiograms.[<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

326615</a>

3267] Most of these procedures can be performed with patients on their side or prone. </p>

3268<p id="_742" tabindex="-1">

3269Because of the risk of

3270complications from general anesthesia or heavy sedation, a careful physiologic and radiographic

3271evaluation of the patient should be completed, and the least invasive

3272procedure should be used to establish the diagnosis of lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

327316</a>

3274,<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

327517</a>

3276] The following procedures may be used: </p>

3277<div class="pdq-content-list">

3278<ul id="_994">

3279<li>

3280Bone marrow aspirate and biopsy.</li>

3281<li>

3282Thoracentesis. If a pleural or pericardial effusion is present, a cytologic diagnosis is

3283frequently possible using thoracentesis, with confirmation of the diagnosis and cell lineage by flow cytometry.</li>

3284<li>

3285Lymph node biopsy. In children who present with

3286peripheral adenopathy, a lymph node biopsy performed under local anesthesia and with the patient in an

3287upright position may be possible.[<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

328818</a>

3289] </li>

3290</ul>

3291</div>

3292<p id="_995" tabindex="-1">

3293In situations when the above

3294procedures do not yield a diagnosis, the use of a CT-guided core-needle biopsy should be considered. This procedure can frequently be performed using light sedation and local anesthesia before more

3295invasive procedures are undertaken. Care should be taken to keep patients out of a supine position. Mediastinoscopy, anterior mediastinotomy, or thoracoscopy

3296are the procedures of choice when other diagnostic modalities fail to establish

3297the diagnosis. A formal thoracotomy is rarely, if ever, indicated for the

3298diagnosis or treatment of childhood lymphoma.</p>

3299<p id="_743" tabindex="-1">

3300Occasionally, it will not be

3301possible to perform a diagnostic operative procedure because of the risk of

3302complications from general anesthesia or heavy sedation. In these situations, preoperative

3303treatment with steroids or, less commonly, localized radiation therapy should be considered.

3304Because preoperative treatment may affect the ability to obtain an accurate

3305tissue diagnosis, a diagnostic biopsy should be done as soon as the risk of

3306complications from general anesthesia or heavy sedation is reduced.</p>

3307</section>

3308<section id="_647">

3309<h4 id="_647_toc">

3310Tumor lysis syndrome</h4>

3311<p id="_648" tabindex="-1">

3312Tumor lysis syndrome results from rapid breakdown of malignant cells, causing several metabolic abnormalities, most notably hyperuricemia,

3313hyperkalemia, and hyperphosphatemia. Patients may present with tumor lysis syndrome before the start of therapy. </p>

3314<p id="_996" tabindex="-1">

3315Hyperhydration and allopurinol or

3316rasburicase (urate oxidase) are essential components of therapy in all patients, except those with the most limited disease.[<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

331719</a>

3318-<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

331924</a>

3320] In patients with G6PD deficiency, rasburicase may cause hemolysis or methemoglobinuria and should be avoided. An initial prephase consisting of low-dose

3321cyclophosphamide and vincristine does not obviate the need for

3322allopurinol or rasburicase and hydration. </p>

3323<p id="_997" tabindex="-1">

3324Hyperuricemia and tumor lysis syndrome,

3325particularly when associated with ureteral obstruction, frequently result in

3326life-threatening complications.</p>

3327</section>

3328</section>

3329<section id="_950">

3330<h3 id="_950_toc">

3331Tumor Surveillance</h3>

3332<p id="_951" tabindex="-1">

3333Although the use of positron emission tomography (PET) to assess rapidity of response to therapy appears to have prognostic value in Hodgkin lymphoma and some types of NHL observed in adult patients, it remains under investigation in pediatric NHL. To date, there are insufficient data for pediatric NHL to support a finding that early response to therapy assessed by PET has prognostic value. </p>

3334<p id="_952" tabindex="-1">

3335Diagnosing relapsed disease solely on the basis of imaging requires caution because false-positive results are common.[<a href="#cit/section_4.25">

333625</a>

3337-<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

333828</a>

3339] Data also demonstrate that PET scanning can produce false-negative results.[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

334029</a>

3341] A study of young adults with primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma demonstrated that 9 of 12 patients who had residual mediastinal masses at the end of therapy had positive PET scans. Seven of these nine patients had the masses resected, but no viable tumor was found.[<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

334230</a>

3343] Before changes in therapy are undertaken on the basis of residual masses noted by imaging, even if the PET scan is positive, a biopsy to prove residual disease is warranted.[<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

334428</a>

3345]</p>

3346</section>

Mayo Clin Proc 63 (10): 1004-11, 1988.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=3172849&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=3172849&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3477 <section id="_1780">

3478

3479 <h2>

3480Special Considerations for the Treatment of Children With Cancer</h2>

3481

3482 <div id="_section_1780" class="pdq-sections">

3483<p id="_1781" tabindex="-1">

3484Cancer in children and adolescents is rare, although the overall incidence has been slowly increasing since 1975.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

34851</a>

3486] Children and adolescents with

3487cancer should be referred to medical centers that have a multidisciplinary team

3488of cancer specialists with experience treating the cancers that occur during

3489childhood and adolescence. This multidisciplinary team approach incorporates the skills

3490of the following health care professionals and others to ensure that children receive the treatment, supportive care, and rehabilitation

3491that will achieve optimal survival and quality of life:</p>

3492<div class="pdq-content-list">

3493<ul id="_1782">

3494<li>

3495Primary care physicians.</li>

3496<li>

3497Pediatric surgeons.</li>

3498<li>

3499Radiation oncologists. </li>

3500<li>

3501Pediatric medical oncologists/hematologists.</li>

3502<li>

3503Rehabilitation specialists.</li>

3504<li>

3505Pediatric nurse specialists.</li>

3506<li>

3507Social workers.</li>

3508<li>

3509Child life professionals.</li>

3510<li>

3511Psychologists.</li>

3512</ul>

3513</div>

3514<p id="_1783" tabindex="-1">

3515For information about supportive care for children and adolescents with cancer, see the summaries on <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care">

3516Supportive and Palliative Care</a>

3517.</p>

3518<p id="_1784" tabindex="-1">

3519The American Academy of Pediatrics has outlined guidelines for

3520pediatric cancer centers and their role in the treatment of children and adolescents with

3521cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

35222</a>

3523] At these

3524pediatric cancer centers, clinical trials are available for most types of cancer that occur in children and adolescents, and the opportunity to

3525participate is offered to most patients and families. Clinical

3526trials for children and adolescents with cancer are generally designed to

3527compare potentially better therapy with current standard therapy. Most of the progress made in identifying curative therapies

3528for childhood cancers has been achieved through clinical trials. Information

3529about ongoing NCI-supported clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

3530NCI

3531 website</a>

3532.</p>

3533<h6 do-not-show="toc">

3534References</h6>

3535<ol>

3536<li id="section_5.1">

3537Smith MA, Altekruse SF, Adamson PC, et al.: Declining childhood and adolescent cancer mortality. Cancer 120 (16): 2497-506, 2014.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24853691&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24853691&amp;dopt=Abstract">

3538[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

3539</li>

3540<li id="section_5.2">

3541American Academy of Pediatrics: Standards for pediatric cancer centers. Pediatrics 134 (2): 410-4, 2014. <a href="https://pediatrics.aappublications.org/content/134/2/410" title="https://pediatrics.aappublications.org/content/134/2/410">

3542Also available online</a>

3543. Last accessed May 19, 2023.</li>

3544</ol>

3545</div>
