3665<section id="_787">

3666<h3 id="_787_toc">

3667Burkitt Lymphoma/Leukemia</h3>

3668<section id="_789">

3669<h4 id="_789_toc">

3670Incidence</h4>

3671<p id="_790" tabindex="-1">

3672In the United States, Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia accounts for about 40% of childhood non-Hodgkin lymphoma (NHL) cases and exhibits a consistent, aggressive clinical behavior.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

36731</a>

3674] The overall incidence of Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia in the United States is 2.4 cases per 1 million person-years and is higher among boys than girls (3.9 vs. 0.9).[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

36752</a>

3676,<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

36773</a>

3678] (See <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_540">

3679Table 1</a>

3680 for more information about the incidence of Burkitt lymphoma by age and sex distribution.)</p>

3681</section>

3682<section id="_791">

3683<h4 id="_791_toc">

3684Clinical presentation</h4>

3685<p id="_287" tabindex="-1">

3686 The most common primary sites of disease are the abdomen and the lymphatic tissue of Waldeyer ring.[<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

36874</a>

3688] Other sites of involvement include testes, bone, skin, bone marrow, and central nervous system (CNS). While lung involvement does not tend to occur, pleural and peritoneal spread are seen.[<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

36894</a>

3690]</p>

3691</section>

3692<section id="_793">

3693<h4 id="_793_toc">

3694Tumor biology</h4>

3695<section id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1746">

3696<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1746_toc">

3697Genomics of Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia</h5>

3698<p id="_sm_CDR0000779363_456" tabindex="-1">

3699The malignant cells of Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia show a mature B-cell phenotype and are negative for the enzyme terminal deoxynucleotidyl transferase. These malignant cells usually express surface immunoglobulin (Ig), most bearing a clonal surface IgM with either kappa or lambda light chains. A variety of additional B-cell markers (e.g., CD19, CD20, CD22) are usually present, and most childhood Burkitt lymphomas/leukemias express CD10.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

37001</a>

3701] </p>

3702<p id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1007" tabindex="-1">

3703Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia expresses a characteristic chromosomal translocation, usually t(8;14) and more rarely t(8;22) or t(2;8). Each of these translocations juxtaposes the <em class="gene-name">

3704MYC</em>

3705 oncogene and the immunoglobulin locus (IG, mostly the <em class="gene-name">

3706IGH</em>

3707 locus) regulatory elements, resulting in the inappropriate expression of <em class="gene-name">

3708MYC</em>

3709, a gene involved in cellular proliferation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

37105</a>

3711-<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

37127</a>

3713] The presence of one of the variant translocations t(2;8) or t(8;22) does not appear to affect response or outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

37148</a>

3715,<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

37169</a>

3717]</p>

3718<p id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1759" tabindex="-1">

3719Mapping of <em class="gene-name">

3720IGH</em>

3721-translocation breakpoints demonstrated that IG::<em class="gene-name">

3722MYC</em>

3723 translocations in sporadic Burkitt lymphoma most commonly occur through aberrant class-switch recombination and less commonly through somatic hypermutation. Translocations resulting from aberrant variable, diversity, and joining (VDJ) gene segment recombinations are rare.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

372410</a>

3725] These findings are consistent with a germinal center derivation of Burkitt lymphoma.</p>

3726<p id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1749" tabindex="-1">

3727While <em class="gene-name">

3728MYC</em>

3729 translocations are present in all Burkitt lymphoma, cooperating genomic alterations appear to be required for lymphoma development. Some of the more commonly observed recurring mutations that have been identified in Burkitt lymphoma in pediatric and adult cases are listed below. The clinical significance of these mutations for pediatric Burkitt lymphoma remains to be elucidated.</p>

3730<div class="pdq-content-list">

3731<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1750">

3732<li>

3733Activating mutations in the transcription factor <em class="gene-name">

3734TCF3</em>

3735 and inactivating mutations in its negative regulator <em class="gene-name">

3736ID3</em>

3737 are observed in approximately 70% of Burkitt lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

373810</a>

3739-<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

374014</a>

3741]</li>

3742<li>

3743Mutations in <em class="gene-name">

3744TP53</em>

3745 are observed in one-third to one-half of cases.[<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

374611</a>

3747,<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

374813</a>

3749]</li>

3750<li>

3751Mutations in <em class="gene-name">

3752CCND3</em>

3753 are commonly observed in sporadic Burkitt lymphoma (approximately 40% of cases) but are rare in endemic Burkitt lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

375411</a>

3755,<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

375613</a>

3757]</li>

3758<li>

3759Mutually exclusive mutations in <em class="gene-name">

3760SMARCA4</em>

3761 and <em class="gene-name">

3762ARID1A</em>

3763,[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

376410</a>

3765] components of the SWItch/Sucrose Non-Fermentable (SWI/SNF) complex, are observed in more than one-half of pediatric Burkitt lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

37669</a>

3767]</li>

3768<li>

3769Mutations in <em class="gene-name">

3770MYC</em>

3771 itself are observed in approximately one-half of Burkitt lymphoma cases and appear to enhance tumorigenesis, in part, by increasing MYC stability.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

377210</a>

3773,<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

377411</a>

3775,<a href="#cit/section_6.15">

377615</a>

3777]</li>

3778</ul>

3779</div>

3780<p id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1753" tabindex="-1">

3781A study that compared the genomic landscape of endemic Burkitt lymphoma with the genomics of sporadic Burkitt lymphoma found the expected high rate of Epstein-Barr virus (EBV) positivity in endemic cases, with much lower rates in sporadic cases. There was general similarity between the patterns of mutations for endemic and sporadic cases and for EBV-positive and EBV-negative cases. However, EBV-positive cases showed significantly lower mutation rates for selected genes/pathways, including <em class="gene-name">

3782SMARCA4</em>

3783, <em class="gene-name">

3784CCND3</em>

3785, <em class="gene-name">

3786TP53</em>

3787, and apoptosis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

37889</a>

3789]</p>

3790<p id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1008" tabindex="-1">

3791Cytogenetic evidence of <em class="gene-name">

3792MYC</em>

3793 rearrangement is the gold standard for diagnosis of Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia. For cases in which cytogenetic analysis is not available, the World Health Organization (WHO) has recommended that the Burkitt-like diagnosis be reserved for lymphoma resembling Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia or with more pleomorphism, large cells, and a proliferation fraction (i.e., MIB-1 or Ki-67 immunostaining) of 99% or greater.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

37941</a>

3795] BCL2 staining by immunohistochemistry is variable. The absence of a translocation involving the <em class="gene-name">

3796BCL2</em>

3797 gene does not preclude the diagnosis of Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia and has no clinical implications.[<a href="#cit/section_6.16">

379816</a>

3799]</p>

3800</section>

3801<section id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1769">

3802<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1769_toc">

3803Genomics of Burkitt-like lymphoma with 11q aberration</h5>

3804<p id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1754" tabindex="-1">

3805 Burkitt-like lymphoma with 11q aberration was added as a provisional entity in the 2017 revised WHO Classification of Tumours of Haematopoietic and Lymphoid Tissues.[<a href="#cit/section_6.17">

380617</a>

3807] In this entity, <em class="gene-name">

3808MYC</em>

3809 rearrangement is absent, and the characteristic chromosome 11q finding (detected cytogenetically and/or with copy-number DNA arrays) is 11q23.2-q23.3 gain/amplification and 11q24.1-qter loss.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

381018</a>

3811,<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

381219</a>

3813] </p>

3814<div class="pdq-content-list">

3815<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779363_1766">

3816<li>

3817In a study of 102 lymphomas that morphologically resembled Burkitt lymphoma, diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, and high-grade B-cell lymphoma, unclassifiable, 13 cases (13%) lacked a <em class="gene-name">

3818MYC</em>

3819 rearrangement but were positive for 11q proximal gain and telomeric loss by fluorescence <em>

3820in situ</em>

3821 hybridization.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

382220</a>

3823]</li>

3824<li>

3825Most patients with Burkitt lymphoma with 11q aberration present in the adolescent and young adult age range with localized nodal disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

382619</a>

3827,<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

382820</a>

3829] Head and neck involvement is the most common presentation, although presentation in other nodal areas, as well as in the abdomen, can occur.</li>

3830<li>

3831Cases show a very high proliferative index and can show a focal <em>

3832starry sky</em>

3833 pattern.[<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

383419</a>

3835,<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

383620</a>

3837]</li>

3838<li>

3839Outcomes appear highly favorable in the small number of cases identified.[<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

384019</a>

3841,<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

384220</a>

3843]</li>

3844<li>

3845 The mutational landscape of Burkitt-like lymphoma with 11q aberration is distinct from that of Burkitt lymphoma. Mutations commonly observed in Burkitt lymphoma (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

3846ID3</em>

3847, <em class="gene-name">

3848TCF3</em>

3849, and <em class="gene-name">

3850CCND3</em>

3851) are uncommon in Burkitt-like lymphoma with 11q aberration.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

385218</a>

3853] Conversely, mutations in <em class="gene-name">

3854GNA13</em>

3855 appear to be common (up to 50%) in patients with Burkitt-like lymphoma with 11q aberration and are less common in patients with Burkitt lymphoma.</li>

3856</ul>

3857</div>

3858</section>

3859</section>

3860<section id="_1083">

3861<h4 id="_1083_toc">

3862Prognostic factors</h4>

3863<p id="_1084" tabindex="-1">

3864For information about prognostic factors for Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_552">

3865Prognosis and Prognostic Factors for Childhood NHL</a>

3866 section.</p>

3867</section>

3868<section id="_795">

3869<h4 id="_795_toc">

3870Standard treatment options for Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia</h4>

3871<p id="_999" tabindex="-1">

3872The treatment of Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia is the same as treatment for diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, and the following discussion is pertinent to both types of childhood NHL.</p>

3873<p id="_958" tabindex="-1">

3874Unlike mature B-lineage NHL seen in adult patients, there is no difference in outcome based on histology in pediatric patients (Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia or diffuse large B-cell lymphoma). Pediatric Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia and diffuse large B-cell lymphoma are clinically very aggressive, and patients are treated with very intensive regimens.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

387521</a>

3876-<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

387726</a>

3878] </p>

3879<p id="_1675" tabindex="-1">

3880Tumor lysis syndrome is often present at diagnosis or after initiation of treatment. This emergent clinical situation should be anticipated and addressed before treatment is started. For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_647">

3881Tumor lysis syndrome</a>

3882 section.</p>

3883<p id="_361" tabindex="-1">

3884Current treatment strategies are based on risk stratification, as described in <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_483">

3885Table 4</a>

3886. Involvement of the bone marrow may lead to confusion about whether the patient has lymphoma or leukemia. Traditionally, patients with more than 25% marrow blasts are classified as having mature B-cell leukemia, and those with fewer than 25% marrow blasts are classified as having lymphoma. It is not clear whether these arbitrary definitions are biologically distinct, but there is no question that patients with Burkitt leukemia should be treated with protocols designed for Burkitt lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

388721</a>

3888,<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

388923</a>

3890,<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

389126</a>

3892] </p>

3893<table id="_483" class="table-default expandable-container">

4010<p id="_1676" tabindex="-1">

4011The following studies have contributed to the development of current treatment regimens for pediatric patients with Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia and diffuse large B-cell lymphoma.</p>

4012<p id="_1677" tabindex="-1">

4013Evidence (chemotherapy):</p>

4014<div class="pdq-content-list">

4015<ol id="_1678">

4016<li>

4017<strong>

4018Berlin-Frankfurt-M&#252;nster (BFM) studies</strong>

4019<div class="pdq-content-list">

4020<ol id="_1837" class="lower-alpha">

4021<li>

4022<strong>

4023Localized disease (R1 and R2 groups):</strong>

4024 The BFM group has treated risk group R1 patients with two cycles of multiagent chemotherapy (GER-GPOH-NHL-BFM-90 and GER-GPOH-NHL-BFM-95).[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

402521</a>

4026,<a href="#cit/section_6.28">

402728</a>

4028] R2 patients received a cytoreductive phase followed by five cycles of chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

402921</a>

4030,<a href="#cit/section_6.28">

403128</a>

4032] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4033<ul id="_1679">

4034<li>

4035Event-free survival (EFS) rates with best therapy in the NHL-BFM-95 study were higher than 95% for R1 and R2 group patients.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

403621</a>

4037]</li>

4038</ul>

4039</div>

4040</li>

4041<li>

4042<strong>

4043Advanced/disseminated disease (R3 and R4 groups):</strong>

4044 In the NHL-BFM-95 study, the EFS rate was 93% with best therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

404521</a>

4046] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4047<ul id="_1680">

4048<li>

4049Inferior outcome was observed for patients with CNS disease at presentation (3-year EFS rate, 70%).[<a href="#cit/section_6.28">

405028</a>

4051]</li>

4052</ul>

4053</div>

4054</li>

4055</ol>

4056</div>

4057</li>

4058<li>

4059<strong>

4060French Society of Pediatric Oncology Lymphome Malins de Burkitt (LMB) and French-American British (FAB) studies</strong>

4061<div class="pdq-content-list">

4062<ol id="_1838" class="lower-alpha">

4063<li>

4064<strong>

4065Localized disease (group A):</strong>

4066 Patients who received two cycles of multiagent chemotherapy, without intrathecal chemotherapy or rituximab, had excellent outcomes (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002757">

4067COG-C5961 [FAB/LMB-96]</a>

4068).[<a href="#cit/section_6.27">

406927</a>

4070][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4071Level of evidence B4</a>

4072]</li>

4073<li>

4074<strong>

4075Advanced disease (group B):</strong>

4076 <div class="pdq-content-list">

4077<ul id="_1682">

4078<li>

4079The 3-year EFS rate was 90% for stage III patients and 86% for stage IV (CNS-negative and nonleukemic) patients.</li>

4080<li>

4081Patients with a lactate dehydrogenase (LDH) level more than twice the upper limit of normal had an EFS rate of 86% compared with 96% in those with lower LDH levels.</li>

4082</ul>

4083</div>

4084</li>

4085<li>

4086<strong>

4087Disseminated disease (group C):</strong>

4088 <div class="pdq-content-list">

4089<ul id="_1683">

4090<li>

4091Patients with leukemic disease only, and no CNS disease, had a 3-year EFS rate of 90%, while patients with CNS disease at presentation had a 3-year EFS rate of 70%.</li>

4092<li>

4093Patients with combined marrow and CNS disease at diagnosis had an EFS rate of only 61%.</li>

4094<li>

4095This study identified the response to prophase reduction as the most significant prognostic factor, with poor responders (i.e., &lt;20% resolution of disease) having an EFS rate of 30%.</li>

4096</ul>

4097</div>

4098</li>

4099</ol>

4100</div>

4101</li>

4102</ol>

4103</div>

4104<p id="_1109" tabindex="-1">

4105Both the BFM and FAB/LMB studies demonstrated that omission of craniospinal irradiation, even in patients presenting with CNS disease, does not affect outcome (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002757">

4106COG-C5961 [FAB/LMB-96]</a>

4107 and NHL-BFM-90 [GER-GPOH-NHL-BFM-90]).[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

410821</a>

4109-<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

411023</a>

4111,<a href="#cit/section_6.28">

411228</a>

4113] </p>

4114<p id="_1788" tabindex="-1">

4115Evidence (rituximab):</p>

4116<div class="pdq-content-list">

4117<ol id="_1789">

4118<li>

4119In a phase II study performed by the BFM group, single-agent rituximab showed activity in pediatric patients with Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia.[<a href="#cit/section_6.29">

412029</a>

4121][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4122Level of evidence B4</a>

4123]</li>

4124<li>

4125A Children's Oncology Group (COG) pilot study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00057811">

4126COG-ANHL01P1</a>

4127) added rituximab to baseline chemotherapy with <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002757">

4128FAB/LMB-96</a>

4129 therapy in patients with stage III and stage IV B-cell NHL.[<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

413030</a>

4131]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.24">

413224</a>

4133][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4134Level of evidence C1</a>

4135] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4136<ul id="_1790">

4137<li>

4138Compared with chemotherapy-only protocols, toxicity was similar with the addition of rituximab, despite a trend toward higher peak levels of rituximab in younger patients.</li>

4139</ul>

4140</div>

4141</li>

4142<li>

4143An international randomized phase III trial (COG-ANHL1131) evaluated the benefit of adding rituximab to standard therapy for Group B patients with high levels of LDH and Group C patients. The study was closed early because of the superior outcomes observed for the patients who received rituximab.[<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

414426</a>

4145] This study led to the European Medicines Agency and U.S. Food and Drug Administration approval of rituximab for the treatment of pediatric patients with B-cell lymphoma.<div class="pdq-content-list">

4146<ul id="_1791">

4147<li>

4148For patients in the rituximab arm, the EFS rate was 94% for this high-risk group of patients (stage III with elevated LDH and stage IV) compared with 82% for patients who received standard therapy (hazard ratio, 0.32; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.15&#8211;0.66; one-sided <em>

4149P</em>

4150 = .00096). </li>

4151<li>

4152Refractory disease or relapse/progression was observed in 15% of patients who received standard therapy, compared with 3% of patients who received rituximab. </li>

4153<li>

4154 Toxic mortality occurred in 2% of patients in each arm.</li>

4155<li>

4156For patients who received rituximab, there was no difference in outcome based on age, histology (diffuse large B-cell lymphoma vs. Burkitt lymphoma), stage, and response to low-dose cyclophosphamide, vincristine, and prednisone (COP regimen).</li>

4157</ul>

4158</div>

4159</li>

4160</ol>

4161</div>

4162<p id="_1684" tabindex="-1">

4163Standard treatment options for Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia and diffuse large B-cell lymphoma are described in <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1858">

4164Table 5</a>

4165.</p>

4275<section id="_797">

4276<h4 id="_797_toc">

4277Treatment options for recurrent or refractory Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia</h4>

4278<p id="_914" tabindex="-1">

4279There is no standard treatment option for patients with recurrent or progressive disease. For patients with recurrent or refractory aggressive mature B-cell NHL, reported survival ranges between 10% to 50%; in the largest series, survival was reported to be about 20%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

428023</a>

4281,<a href="#cit/section_6.31">

428231</a>

4283,<a href="#cit/section_6.32">

428432</a>

4285]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

428633</a>

4287][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4288Level of evidence C1</a>

4289] Three large retrospective multivariable analyses identified the following prognostic factors: </p>

4290<div class="pdq-content-list">

4291<ul id="_1976">

4292<li>

4293For improved survival:<div class="pdq-content-list">

4294<ul id="_1977" class="list-dash">

4295<li>

4296Duration of complete remission of more than 6 months.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

429734</a>

4298,<a href="#cit/section_6.35">

429935</a>

4300]</li>

4301<li>

4302Normal LDH levels at initial diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

430334</a>

4304,<a href="#cit/section_6.35">

430535</a>

4306]</li>

4307<li>

4308One site of disease at relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

430934</a>

4310]</li>

4311<li>

4312No failure in bone marrow.[<a href="#cit/section_6.35">

431335</a>

4314]</li>

4315<li>

4316Diffuse large B-cell lymphoma histology.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

431734</a>

4318]</li>

4319<li>

4320Complete remission before stem cell transplantation (SCT).[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

432133</a>

4322][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4323Level of evidence C1</a>

4324]</li>

4325<li>

4326Reinduction with intensive continuous therapy before SCT.[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

432733</a>

4328][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4329Level of evidence C1</a>

4330]</li>

4331</ul>

4332</div>

4333</li>

4334<li>

4335For inferior survival:<div class="pdq-content-list">

4336<ul id="_1978" class="list-dash">

4337<li>

4338Progression during initial therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

433933</a>

4340][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4341Level of evidence C1</a>

4342]</li>

4343<li>

4344Rituximab during initial therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

434533</a>

4346][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4347Level of evidence C1</a>

4348]</li>

4349</ul>

4350</div>

4351</li>

4352</ul>

4353</div>

4354<p id="_1713" tabindex="-1">

4355Treatment options for recurrent or refractory Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia and diffuse large B-cell lymphoma include the following:</p>

4356<div class="pdq-content-list">

4357<ol id="_901">

4358<li>

4359R-ICE regimen (rituximab plus ifosfamide, carboplatin, and etoposide [ICE]).[<a href="#cit/section_6.36">

436036</a>

4361]</li>

4362<li>

4363CYVE regimen (high-dose cytarabine and etoposide) for relapsed group A and group B disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

436434</a>

4365]</li>

4366<li>

4367Allogeneic or autologous SCT.[<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

436837</a>

4369-<a href="#cit/section_6.39">

437039</a>

4371]</li>

4372<li>

4373Bispecific antibody (anti-CD20, anti-CD3).[<a href="#cit/section_6.40">

437440</a>

4375]</li>

4376<li>

4377Chimeric antigen receptor (CAR) T-cell therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.41">

437841</a>

4379]</li>

4380<li>

4381R-VICI regimen (rituximab, vincristine, idarubicin, ifosfamide, carboplatin, and dexamethasone).[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

438233</a>

4383][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4384Level of evidence C1</a>

4385]</li>

4386</ol>

4387</div>

4388<p id="_967" tabindex="-1">

4389Chemoresistance makes remission difficult to achieve.</p>

4390<p id="_1010" tabindex="-1">

4391Evidence (treatment of recurrent or refractory Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia):</p>

4392<div class="pdq-content-list">

4393<ol id="_1011">

4394<li>

4395A study from the United Kingdom for children with relapsed or refractory mature B-cell NHL and B-cell acute lymphoblastic leukemia reported the following:[<a href="#cit/section_6.42">

439642</a>

4397]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4398<ul id="_2406">

4399<li>

4400The most favorable outcomes occurred in patients who received rituximab and an autologous SCT. </li>

4401<li>

4402However, the study could not distinguish whether this relationship reflected that children who survived were those who remained well enough to tolerate chemotherapy and rituximab, achieved a response, and were eligible for transplantation.</li>

4403</ul>

4404</div>

4405</li>

4406<li>

4407In a COG study of 20 patients with relapsed/refractory B-cell NHL (Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia [n = 14] and diffuse large B-cell lymphoma) who were treated with R-ICE, the following was observed:[<a href="#cit/section_6.36">

440836</a>

4409][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4410Level of evidence C1</a>

4411] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4412<ul id="_1012">

4413<li>

4414A complete remission/partial remission rate of 60%.</li>

4415</ul>

4416</div>

4417</li>

4418<li>

4419The Japanese Pediatric Leukemia/Lymphoma Study Group performed a phase II study using R-ICE to treat 28 pediatric patients with relapsed/refractory B-cell NHL.[<a href="#cit/section_6.43">

442043</a>

4421]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4422<ul id="_1839">

4423<li>

4424The investigators observed a complete and partial response rate of 70%.</li>

4425</ul>

4426</div>

4427</li>

4428<li>

4429A retrospective review of patients with relapsed disease treated in the LMB-89, LMB-96, and LMB-2001 trials were analyzed. Group A and group B patients received the CYVE regimen as initial salvage therapy, and group C patients received ICE with or without rituximab.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

443034</a>

4431]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4432<ul id="_1840">

4433<li>

4434The complete and partial remission rate was 64%; 2 of 3 group A patients responded, 19 of 29 group B patients responded, and 3 of 5 group C patients responded.</li>

4435</ul>

4436</div>

4437</li>

4438<li>

4439A retrospective review of patients with relapsed Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia treated initially with BFM therapy noted that patients who were treated before the year 2000 had significantly inferior outcomes compared with those who were treated after 2000. The 5-year overall survival (OS) rates were 11% for those treated before the year 2000, compared with 27% for those treated after 2000. The 75 patients treated after 2000 were analyzed further, with the following results:[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

444033</a>

4441][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4442Level of evidence C1</a>

4443]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4444<ul id="_1979">

4445<li>

4446Of the 75 patients, 29 (41%) had Burkitt leukemia.</li>

4447<li>

4448Median time to relapse was 0.4 years after diagnosis; more than one-third of patients relapsed during initial treatment.</li>

4449<li>

4450Reinduction therapy was variable; 65% of patients proceeded to either autologous or allogeneic SCT.</li>

4451<li>

4452Reinduction with intensive continuous chemotherapy with R-VICI therapy before SCT improved survival (4-year OS rate, 67%).</li>

4453<li>

4454Initial disease risk category was prognostic: Low risk (R1/R2) had improved survival (4-year OS rate, 50%) compared with high risk (R3/R4; 4-year OS rate, 21%).</li>

4455<li>

4456First-line therapy was also prognostic: 25 of 28 patients with progression during first-line therapy died, and 9 of 10 patients who were treated with front-line rituximab died.</li>

4457<li>

4458Complete remission before SCT was prognostic (4-year OS rate, 63%).</li>

4459</ul>

4460</div>

4461</li>

4462<li>

4463There are limited data for CAR T-cell therapy in pediatric patients with Burkitt lymphoma and diffuse large B-cell lymphoma. A single-center experience included 23 children with relapsed or refractory Burkitt lymphoma who were treated sequentially with three CAR T cells (CD19, CD22, and CD20). Patients were followed every 2 weeks, and they received subsequent CAR T cells if CAR T cells were not detectable in the blood or there was evidence of disease progression.[<a href="#cit/section_6.41">

446441</a>

4465]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4466<ul id="_2451">

4467<li>

4468The 18-month CR and PFS rates were 78%. </li>

4469<li>

4470These results may be due to short (1 week) CAR T-cell manufacture time and/or sequential antigen targeting. </li>

4471<li>

4472 The sustained CR rates were 39% (9 of 23) for CD19 CAR T cells, 38% (5 of 13) for CD22 CAR T cells, and 50% (3 of 6) for CD20 CAR T cells.</li>

4473<li>

4474 The median follow-up was only 17 months.</li>

4475</ul>

4476</div>

4477</li>

4478</ol>

4479</div>

4480<p id="_1004" tabindex="-1">

4481If remission can be achieved, high-dose therapy plus SCT remains the best option for survival. Patients not in remission at the time of transplant fare significantly worse.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

448234</a>

4483,<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

448437</a>

4485,<a href="#cit/section_6.42">

448642</a>

4487,<a href="#cit/section_6.44">

448844</a>

4489-<a href="#cit/section_6.46">

449046</a>

4491] The very poor outcome of patients whose disease is refractory to salvage chemotherapy suggests that a nonexperimental transplant option should not be pursued in these patients.[<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

449237</a>

4493,<a href="#cit/section_6.45">

449445</a>

4495,<a href="#cit/section_6.46">

449646</a>

4497] If a complete remission was reported, survival ranges between 30% to 75%, albeit all series have a small number of patients (i.e., fewer than 40).[<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

449837</a>

4499,<a href="#cit/section_6.42">

450042</a>

4501,<a href="#cit/section_6.43">

450243</a>

4503,<a href="#cit/section_6.45">

450445</a>

4505,<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

450647</a>

4507] The benefit of autologous versus allogeneic SCT remains unclear.[<a href="#cit/section_6.32">

450832</a>

4509,<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

451037</a>

4511,<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

451247</a>

4513,<a href="#cit/section_6.48">

451448</a>

4515]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.44">

451644</a>

4517][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4518Level of evidence B4</a>

4519]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.49">

452049</a>

4521][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4522Level of evidence C2</a>

4523] </p>

4524<p id="_1005" tabindex="-1">

4525For more information about transplantation, see <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/hp-stem-cell-transplant/autologous">

4526Pediatric Autologous Hematopoietic Stem Cell Transplantation</a>

4527, <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/hp-stem-cell-transplant/allogeneic">

4528Pediatric Allogeneic Hematopoietic Stem Cell Transplantation</a>

4529, and <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/hp-stem-cell-transplant">

4530Pediatric Hematopoietic Stem Cell Transplantation and Cellular Therapy for Cancer</a>

4531.</p>

4532<p id="_1006" tabindex="-1">

4533Evidence (SCT therapy):</p>

4534<div class="pdq-content-list">

4535<ol id="_1013">

4536<li>

4537An analysis of data from the Center for International

4538Blood and Marrow Transplant Research demonstrated the following:[<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

453937</a>

4540]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4541<ul id="_1015">

4542<li>

4543No difference in outcome using either autologous or allogeneic donor stem cell sources.</li>

4544<li>

4545The 2-year EFS rates were 50% for patients with diffuse large B-cell lymphoma and 30% for patients with Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia who survived to undergo a transplant. </li>

4546<li>

4547Some graft-versus-lymphoma effect has been implied by the lower relapse rate in the allogeneic SCT patients; however, that was balanced by the higher treatment-related mortality.</li>

4548</ul>

4549</div>

4550</li>

4551<li>

4552A small, single-center, prospective study used autologous transplantation followed by reduced-intensity allogeneic SCT to treat patients with relapsed NHL.[<a href="#cit/section_6.38">

455338</a>

4554]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4555<ul id="_1016">

4556<li>

4557The study reported an EFS rate of 60%.</li>

4558</ul>

4559</div>

4560</li>

4561</ol>

4562</div>

4563</section>

4564<section id="_1792">

4565<h4 id="_1792_toc">

4566Treatment options under clinical evaluation for Burkitt leukemia/lymphoma</h4>

4567<p id="_1793" tabindex="-1">

4568Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

4569NCI website</a>

4570. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

4571ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4572.</p>

4573<p id="_1824" tabindex="-1">

4574The following is an example of a national and/or institutional clinical trial that is currently being conducted:</p>

4575<div class="pdq-content-list">

4576<ul id="_1806">

4577<li>

4578<strong>

4579<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03155620">

4580APEC1621 (NCT03155620)</a>

4581</strong>

4582 (Pediatric MATCH: Targeted Therapy Directed by Genetic Testing in Treating Pediatric Patients with Relapsed or Refractory Advanced Solid Tumors, Non-Hodgkin Lymphomas, or Histiocytic Disorders)<strong>

4583:</strong>

4584 NCI-COG Pediatric Molecular Analysis for Therapeutic Choice (MATCH), referred to as Pediatric MATCH, will match targeted agents with specific molecular changes identified in a patient's tumor (refractory or recurrent). Children and adolescents aged 1 to 21 years are eligible for the trial.<p id="_1795" tabindex="-1">

4585Patients with tumors that have molecular variants addressed by treatment arms included in the trial will be offered treatment on Pediatric MATCH. Additional information can be obtained on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match">

4586NCI website</a>

4587 and <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620">

4588ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4589.</p>

4590

4591</li>

4592</ul>

4600 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. 4602 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4605</section>

4606<section id="_801">

4607<h3 id="_801_toc">

4608Diffuse Large B-cell Lymphoma</h3>

4609<p id="_1110" tabindex="-1">

4610Primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma, previously considered a subtype of diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, is now a separate entity in the most recent WHO classification. For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_815">

4611Primary Mediastinal B-cell Lymphoma</a>

4612 section.</p>

4613<section id="_803">

4614<h4 id="_803_toc">

4615Incidence</h4>

4616<p id="_804" tabindex="-1">

4617Diffuse large B-cell lymphoma is an aggressive mature B-cell neoplasm that represents 10% to 20% of pediatric NHL cases.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

461850</a>

4619,<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

462051</a>

4621] Diffuse large B-cell lymphoma occurs more frequently during the second decade of life than during the first decade.[<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

462251</a>

4623,<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

462452</a>

4625] For more information on the incidence of diffuse large B-cell lymphoma by age and sex distribution, see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_540">

4626Table 1</a>

4627.</p>

4628</section>

4629<section id="_805">

4630<h4 id="_805_toc">

4631Clinical presentation</h4>

4632<p id="_291" tabindex="-1">

4633 Pediatric diffuse large B-cell lymphoma may present in a manner clinically similar to that of Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia, although more often it is localized, and less often it involves the bone marrow or CNS.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

463450</a>

4635,<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

463652</a>

4637] For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_791">

4638Clinical presentation</a>

4639 section in the Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia section.</p>

4640</section>

4641<section id="_807">

4642<h4 id="_807_toc">

4643Tumor biology</h4>

4644<section id="_sm_CDR0000779364_1747">

4645<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779364_1747_toc">

4646Genomics of diffuse large B-cell lymphoma</h5>

4647<p id="_sm_CDR0000779364_915" tabindex="-1">

4648The World Health Organization (WHO) classification system categorizes diffuse large B-cell lymphoma on the basis of molecular characteristics into the germinal center B-cell subtype and the activated B-cell subtype, with the remaining types being classified as diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, not otherwise specified (NOS).[<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

464953</a>

4650]</p>

4651<p id="_sm_CDR0000779364_350" tabindex="-1">

4652Diffuse large B-cell lymphoma in children and adolescents differs biologically from diffuse large B-cell lymphoma in adults in the following ways:</p>

4653<div class="pdq-content-list">

4654<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779364_1714">

4655<li>

4656Most pediatric diffuse large B-cell lymphoma cases have a germinal center B-cell phenotype, as assessed by immunohistochemical analysis of selected proteins found in normal germinal center B cells, such as the <em class="gene-name">

4657BCL6</em>

4658 gene product and CD10.[<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

46598</a>

4660,<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

466154</a>

4662-<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

466356</a>

4664] The age at which the favorable germinal center subtype changes to the less favorable nongerminal center subtype was shown to be a continuous variable.[<a href="#cit/section_6.57">

466557</a>

4666] </li>

4667<li>

4668Pediatric diffuse large B-cell lymphoma rarely demonstrates the t(14;18) translocation involving the <em class="gene-name">

4669IGH</em>

4670 gene and the <em class="gene-name">

4671BCL2</em>

4672 gene that is seen in adults.[<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

467354</a>

4674]</li>

4675<li>

4676As many as 30% of patients younger than 14 years with diffuse large B-cell lymphoma will have a gene signature similar to Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia.[<a href="#cit/section_6.58">

467758</a>

4678,<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

467959</a>

4680]</li>

4681<li>

4682In contrast to adult diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, pediatric cases show a high frequency of abnormalities at the <em class="gene-name">

4683MYC</em>

4684 locus (chromosome 8q24), with approximately one-third of pediatric cases showing <em class="gene-name">

4685MYC</em>

4686 rearrangement and with approximately one-half of the nonrearranged cases showing <em class="gene-name">

4687MYC</em>

4688 gain or amplification.[<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

468959</a>

4690,<a href="#cit/section_6.60">

469160</a>

4692]</li>

4693<li>

4694In a report that included 31 pediatric patients with diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, NOS, most patients (n = 21) showed a germinal center phenotype, and the genomic alterations resembled those of adult germinal center B-cell diffuse large B-cell lymphoma (GCB-DLBCL) (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

4695SOCS1</em>

4696 and <em class="gene-name">

4697KMT2D</em>

4698 mutations). Among this group of patients, <em class="gene-name">

4699MYC</em>

4700 rearrangements were detected in 3 patients, and 5 of 25 cases were EBV positive (4 with the activated B-cell phenotype).[<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

470156</a>

4702]</li>

4703</ul>

4704</div>

4705<p id="_sm_CDR0000779364_1751" tabindex="-1">

4706Large B-cell lymphoma with <em class="gene-name">

4707IRF4</em>

4708 rearrangement (LBCL-<em class="gene-name">

4709IRF4</em>

4710) was added as a provisional entity in the 2017 revision of the WHO classification of lymphoid neoplasms.[<a href="#cit/section_6.61">

471161</a>

4712]</p>

4713<div class="pdq-content-list">

4714<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779364_1752">

4715<li>

4716LBCL-<em class="gene-name">

4717IRF4</em>

4718 cases have a translocation that juxtaposes the <em class="gene-name">

4719IRF4</em>

4720 oncogene next to one of the immunoglobulin loci.</li>

4721<li>

4722 In one report, diffuse large B-cell lymphoma cases with an <em class="gene-name">

4723IRF4</em>

4724 translocation were significantly more frequent in children than in adults with diffuse large B-cell lymphoma or follicular lymphoma (15% vs. 2%). One study of 32 pediatric cases of diffuse large B-cell lymphoma or follicular lymphoma found 2 (6%) with <em class="gene-name">

4725IRF4</em>

4726 translocations.[<a href="#cit/section_6.62">

472762</a>

4728] A second study of 34 cases of pediatric follicular lymphoma or diffuse large B-cell lymphoma found 7 cases (21%) with <em class="gene-name">

4729IRF</em>

4730 translocations; most of these cases occurred in the adolescent age range.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

473120</a>

4732]</li>

4733<li>

4734LBCL-<em class="gene-name">

4735IRF4</em>

4736 cases are primarily germinal center&#8211;derived B-cell lymphomas, and commonly present with nodal involvement of the head and neck (particularly the Waldeyer ring) and less commonly in the gastrointestinal tract.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

473720</a>

4738,<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

473956</a>

4740,<a href="#cit/section_6.63">

474163</a>

4742,<a href="#cit/section_6.64">

474364</a>

4744]</li>

4745<li>

4746LBCL-<em class="gene-name">

4747IRF4</em>

4748 shows strong IRF4/MUM1 expression, and in a study of 17 cases, the most frequently mutated genes were <em class="gene-name">

4749CARD11</em>

4750 (35%) and <em class="gene-name">

4751CCND3</em>

4752 (24%).</li>

4753<li>

4754LBCL-<em class="gene-name">

4755IRF4</em>

4756 appears to be a low stage at diagnosis and is associated with a favorable prognosis compared with diffuse large B-cell lymphoma cases lacking this abnormality.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

475720</a>

4758,<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

475956</a>

4760,<a href="#cit/section_6.63">

476163</a>

4762]</li>

4763</ul>

4764</div>

4765<p id="_sm_CDR0000779364_1753" tabindex="-1">

4766High-grade B-cell lymphoma, NOS is defined as a clinically aggressive B-cell lymphoma that lacks <em class="gene-name">

4767MYC</em>

4768 plus <em class="gene-name">

4769BCL2</em>

4770 and/or <em class="gene-name">

4771BCL6</em>

4772 rearrangements and that does not meet criteria for diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, NOS or Burkitt lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.65">

477365</a>

4774]</p>

4775<div class="pdq-content-list">

4776<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779364_1754">

4777<li>

4778High-grade B-cell lymphoma, NOS is a biologically heterogeneous disease. In a study of eight pediatric high-grade B-cell lymphoma, NOS cases, four had mutational profiles similar to that of Burkitt lymphoma (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

4779MYC</em>

4780 rearrangements and mutations in <em class="gene-name">

4781CCND3</em>

4782, <em class="gene-name">

4783ID3</em>

4784, and <em class="gene-name">

4785DDX3X</em>

4786).[<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

478756</a>

4788] The remaining cases lacked <em class="gene-name">

4789MYC</em>

4790 rearrangements and had mutational profiles closer to GCB-DLBCL (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

4791TNFRSF14</em>

4792, <em class="gene-name">

4793CARD11</em>

4794 and <em class="gene-name">

4795EZH2</em>

4796 mutations), and lacked <em class="gene-name">

4797MYC</em>

4798 translocations.</li>

4799</ul>

4800</div>

4801</section>

4802</section>

4803<section id="_1096">

4804<h4 id="_1096_toc">

4805Prognostic factors</h4>

4806<p id="_1097" tabindex="-1">

4807For information about prognostic factors for diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_552">

4808Prognosis and Prognostic Factors for Childhood NHL</a>

4809 section.</p>

4810</section>

4811<section id="_809">

4812<h4 id="_809_toc">

4813Treatment options for diffuse large B-cell lymphoma</h4>

4814<p id="_880" tabindex="-1">

4815As with Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia, current treatment strategies are based on risk stratification, as described in <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1858">

4816Table 5</a>

4817. The treatment of diffuse large B-cell lymphoma is the same as the treatment of Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia. For information about the treatment of diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_795">

4818Standard treatment options for Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia</a>

4819 section.</p>

4820</section>

4821<section id="_811">

4822<h4 id="_811_toc">

4823Treatment options for recurrent or refractory diffuse large B-cell lymphoma</h4>

4824<p id="_798" tabindex="-1">

4825The treatment of recurrent diffuse large B-cell lymphoma is the same as the treatment of recurrent Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia. For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_797">

4826Treatment options for recurrent or refractory Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia</a>

4827 section.</p>

4828</section>

4829<section id="_1796">

4830<h4 id="_1796_toc">

4831Treatment options under clinical evaluation for diffuse large B-cell lymphoma</h4>

4832<p id="_1825" tabindex="-1">

4833Information about NCI-supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

4834NCI website</a>

4835. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

4836ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4837.</p>

4838<p id="_1826" tabindex="-1">

4839The following is an example of a national and/or institutional clinical trial that is currently being conducted:</p>

4840<div class="pdq-content-list">

4841<ul id="_1798">

4842<li>

4843<strong>

4844<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03155620">

4845APEC1621 (NCT03155620)</a>

4846</strong>

4847 (Pediatric MATCH: Targeted Therapy Directed by Genetic Testing in Treating Pediatric Patients with Relapsed or Refractory Advanced Solid Tumors, Non-Hodgkin Lymphomas, or Histiocytic Disorders)<strong>

4848:</strong>

4849 NCI-COG Pediatric Molecular Analysis for Therapeutic Choice (MATCH), referred to as Pediatric MATCH, will match targeted agents with specific molecular changes identified in a patient's tumor (refractory or recurrent). Children and adolescents aged 1 to 21 years are eligible for the trial.<p id="_1820" tabindex="-1">

4850Patients with tumors that have molecular variants addressed by treatment arms included in the trial will be offered treatment on Pediatric MATCH. Additional information can be obtained on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match">

4851NCI website</a>

4852 and <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620">

4853ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4854.</p>

4855

4856</li>

4857</ul>

4858</div>

4859<section id="_TrialSearch_1796_sid_10">

4860<h5 id="_TrialSearch_1796_sid_10_toc">

4861Current Clinical Trials</h5>

4862<p id="_TrialSearch_1796_22" tabindex="-1">

4863Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

4864advanced clinical trial search</a>

4865 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4866General information</a>

4867 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4868</section>

4869</section>

4870</section>

4871<section id="_815">

4872<h3 id="_815_toc">

4873Primary Mediastinal B-cell Lymphoma</h3>

4874<section id="_972">

4875<h4 id="_972_toc">

4876Incidence</h4>

4877<p id="_973" tabindex="-1">

4878In the pediatric population, primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma is predominantly seen in older adolescents, accounting for 1% to 2% of all pediatric NHL cases.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

487952</a>

4880,<a href="#cit/section_6.66">

488166</a>

4882-<a href="#cit/section_6.68">

488368</a>

4884]</p>

4885</section>

4886<section id="_974">

4887<h4 id="_974_toc">

4888Clinical presentation</h4>

4889<p id="_975" tabindex="-1">

4890As the name suggests, primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma occurs in the mediastinum. The tumor can be locally invasive (e.g., pericardial and lung extension) and can be associated with superior vena cava syndrome. The tumor can disseminate outside the thoracic cavity with nodal and extranodal involvement, with predilection to the kidneys; however, CNS and marrow involvement are exceedingly rare.[<a href="#cit/section_6.69">

489169</a>

4892]</p>

4893</section>

4894<section id="_916">

4895<h4 id="_916_toc">

4896Tumor biology</h4>

4897<section id="_sm_CDR0000779482_1">

4898<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000779482_1_toc">

4899Genomics of primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma</h5>

4900<p id="_sm_CDR0000779482_976" tabindex="-1">

4901Primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma was previously considered a subtype of diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, but is now a separate entity in the most recent World Health Organization (WHO) classification.[<a href="#cit/section_6.70">

490270</a>

4903] These tumors arise in the mediastinum from thymic B-cells and show a diffuse large cell proliferation with sclerosis that compartmentalizes neoplastic cells. </p>

4904<p id="_sm_CDR0000779482_1685" tabindex="-1">

4905Primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma can be very difficult to distinguish morphologically from the following types of lymphoma:</p>

4906<div class="pdq-content-list">

4907<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779482_1686">

4908<li>

4909Diffuse large B-cell lymphoma: Cell surface markers in primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma are similar to the ones seen in diffuse large B-cell lymphoma (i.e., CD19, CD20, CD22, CD79a, and PAX-5). However, primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma may display cytoplasmic immunoglobulins, and CD30 expression is commonly present.[<a href="#cit/section_6.70">

491070</a>

4911]</li>

4912<li>

4913Hodgkin lymphoma: Primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma may be difficult to distinguish from Hodgkin lymphoma clinically and morphologically, especially with small mediastinal biopsies because of extensive sclerosis and necrosis.</li>

4914</ul>

4915</div>

4916<p id="_sm_CDR0000779482_917" tabindex="-1">

4917Primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma has distinctive gene expression and mutation profiles compared with diffuse large B-cell lymphoma. However, its gene expression and mutation profiles have features similar to those seen in Hodgkin lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.71">

491871</a>

4919-<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

492073</a>

4921] Primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma is also associated with a distinctive constellation of chromosomal aberrations compared with other NHL subtypes. Because primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma is primarily a cancer of adolescents and young adults, the genomic findings are presented without regard to age.</p>

4922<div class="pdq-content-list">

4923<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779482_1692">

4924<li>

4925Multiple genomic alterations contribute to immune evasion in primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma:<div class="pdq-content-list">

4926<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779482_1688" class="list-dash">

4927<li>

4928Structural rearrangements and copy number gains at chromosome 9p24 are common in primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma. This region encodes the immune checkpoint genes <em class="gene-name">

4929CD274</em>

4930 (<em class="gene-name">

4931PDL1</em>

4932) and <em class="gene-name">

4933PDCD1LG2</em>

4934, and the genomic alterations lead to increased expression of these checkpoint proteins.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

493573</a>

4936-<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

493777</a>

4938]</li>

4939<li>

4940Genomic alterations in <em class="gene-name">

4941CIITA</em>

4942, which is the master transcriptional regulator of major histocompatibility complex (MHC) class II expression, are common in primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma, and they lead to loss of MHC class II expression.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

494373</a>

4944,<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

494577</a>

4946,<a href="#cit/section_6.78">

494778</a>

4948]</li>

4949<li>

4950Approximately 50% of primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma cases show mutations or focal copy number losses in <em class="gene-name">

4951B2M</em>

4952, the gene that encodes beta-2-microglobulin (the invariant chain of the MHC class I), which leads to reduced expression of MHC class I.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

495373</a>

4954,<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

495577</a>

4956]</li>

4957</ul>

4958</div>

4959</li>

4960<li>

4961Genomic alterations involving genes of the JAK-STAT pathway are observed in most cases of primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.79">

496279</a>

4963]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4964<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779482_1689" class="list-dash">

4965<li>

4966<em class="gene-name">

4967STAT6</em>

4968 is mutated in approximately 40% of primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

496973</a>

4970,<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

497177</a>

4972] </li>

4973<li>

4974The chromosome 9p region that shows gains and amplification in primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma encodes <em class="gene-name">

4975JAK2</em>

4976, which activates the STAT pathway.[<a href="#cit/section_6.67">

497767</a>

4978,<a href="#cit/section_6.68">

497968</a>

4980]</li>

4981<li>

4982<em class="gene-name">

4983SOCS1</em>

4984, a negative regulator of JAK-STAT signaling, is inactivated in approximately 50% to 60% of primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma cases by either mutation or gene deletion.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

498573</a>

4986,<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

498777</a>

4988,<a href="#cit/section_6.80">

498980</a>

4990,<a href="#cit/section_6.81">

499181</a>

4992] </li>

4993<li>

4994The <em class="gene-name">

4995IL4R</em>

4996 gene shows activating mutations in approximately 20% to 30% of primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma cases, and IL4R activation leads to increased JAK-STAT pathway activity.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

499773</a>

4998,<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

499977</a>

5000,<a href="#cit/section_6.79">

500179</a>

5002]</li>

5003</ul>

5004</div>

5005</li>

5006<li>

5007Genomic alterations leading to NF-&#312;B activation are also common in primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma. These include copy number gains and amplifications at 2p16.1, a region that encodes <em class="gene-name">

5008BCL11A</em>

5009 and <em class="gene-name">

5010REL</em>

5011.[<a href="#cit/section_6.67">

501267</a>

5013,<a href="#cit/section_6.68">

501468</a>

5015,<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

501673</a>

5017,<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

501877</a>

5019] Genes encoding negative regulators of NF-kB signaling (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

5020TNFAIP3</em>

5021 and <em class="gene-name">

5022NFKBIE</em>

5023) show inactivating mutations in primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

502473</a>

5025,<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

502677</a>

5027]</li>

5028<li>

5029Other genes that are mutated in primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma include <em class="gene-name">

5030ZNF217</em>

5031, <em class="gene-name">

5032XPO1</em>

5033, and <em class="gene-name">

5034EZH2</em>

5035.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

503673</a>

5037,<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

503877</a>

5039]</li>

5040</ul>

5041</div>

5042</section>

5043</section>

5044<section id="_1098">

5045<h4 id="_1098_toc">

5046Prognostic factors</h4>

5047<p id="_1099" tabindex="-1">

5048For information on prognostic factors for primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_552">

5049Prognosis and Prognostic Factors for Childhood NHL</a>

5050 section.</p>

5051</section>

5052<section id="_817">

5053<h4 id="_817_toc">

5054Treatment options for primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma</h4>

5055<p id="_978" tabindex="-1">

5056Treatment options for primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma include the following:</p>

5057<div class="pdq-content-list">

5058<ol id="_979">

5059<li>

5060Dose-adjusted etoposide, doxorubicin, cyclophosphamide, vincristine, prednisone, and rituximab (DA-EPOCH-R).</li>

5061<li>

5062Lymphomes Malins B (LMB)&#8211;based chemotherapy plus rituximab.</li>

5063</ol>

5064</div>

5065<p id="_748" tabindex="-1">

5066Pediatric and adolescent patients with stage III primary mediastinal large B-cell lymphoma fared significantly worse on the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002757">

5067FAB/LMB-96 (NCT00002757)</a>

5068 study, with a 5-year EFS rate of 66%, compared with 85% for adolescents with nonmediastinal diffuse large B-cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.82">

506982</a>

5070][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5071Level of evidence B4</a>

5072] Similarly, in the NHL-BFM-95 trial, patients with primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma had an EFS rate of 50% at 3 years.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

507321</a>

5074] However, a study of young adults treated with DA-EPOCH-R showed excellent disease-free survival rates.[<a href="#cit/section_6.83">

507583</a>

5076]</p>

5077<p id="_1687" tabindex="-1">

5078Evidence (DA-EPOCH-R):</p>

5079<div class="pdq-content-list">

5080<ol id="_1688">

5081<li>

5082A single-arm study in young adults utilized the DA-EPOCH-R regimen (usually six cycles) with filgrastim and no radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.83">

508383</a>

5084][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5085Level of evidence B4</a>

5086] <div class="pdq-content-list">

5087<ul id="_1689">

5088<li>

5089The 5-year EFS rate was 93%, and the OS rate was 97%.</li>

5090<li>

5091At short-term follow-up, there was no evidence of cardiac toxicity, despite a high cumulative dose of doxorubicin for those who went through most of the anthracycline-dose escalations.</li>

5092<li>

5093An important finding in this study was the prognostic value of end-of-therapy imaging. Nine of 12 patients who had residual mediastinal masses at the end of therapy had positive positron emission tomography scans. Seven of these nine patients had the masses resected, but no viable tumor was found.</li>

5094<li>

5095A concern for using this regimen is the significantly higher cumulative doses of alkylating agents and anthracyclines administered than those used in previous regimens. </li>

5096</ul>

5097</div>

5098</li>

5099<li>

5100A multicenter, retrospective study of 38 pediatric patients (aged &lt;21 years) and 118 adult patients treated with DA-EPOCH-R observed the following:[<a href="#cit/section_6.84">

510184</a>

5102]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5103<ul id="_1841">

5104<li>

5105 Pediatric patients had a 3-year EFS rate of 81% and a 3-year OS rate of 91%. These results were not significantly different from the results observed in adults.</li>

5106</ul>

5107</div>

5108</li>

5109<li>

5110A prospective, international study included 46 patients (aged &lt;18 years) who were treated with DA-EPOCH-R. The study demonstrated the following results:[<a href="#cit/section_6.85">

511185</a>

5112]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5113<ul id="_2426">

5114<li>

5115The 4-year EFS rate was 70%, and the 3-year OS rate was 85%.</li>

5116<li>

5117These outcomes were lower than those in other studies that treated patients with DA-EPOCH-R and were not statistically different from the results of the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002757">

5118FAB/LMB-96 (NCT00002757)</a>

5119 trial, when a Burkitt lymphoma therapy was used. </li>

5120<li>

5121The ability to dose escalate and the adverse events were similar to what has been reported in adult patients.</li>

5122</ul>

5123</div>

5124</li>

5125</ol>

5126</div>

5127<p id="_2481" tabindex="-1">

5128Evidence (LMB-based chemotherapy plus rituximab):</p>

5129<div class="pdq-content-list">

5130<ol id="_2482">

5131<li>

5132The French prospective LMB2001 study reported the outcomes of patients with primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma who were treated with LMB-based chemotherapy without radiation therapy. There were 773 patients with B-cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma, including 42 patients with primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma, treated between 2001 and 2012. In 2008, the investigators recommended treating all patients with primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma with rituximab on day 1 of each course. Additionally, patients with bulky mediastinal adenopathy (&gt;10 cm) and/or high LDH serum level (&gt;2N on the Institution upper limit value), and/or lomboaortic nodes were assigned to Group C1 therapy. After 2010, patients with primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma were treated with a hybrid Group B/C therapy. In total, 21 of 42 patients received rituximab. Nineteen patients were treated with Group B therapy, 18 with Group C therapy, and 5 with Group B/C therapy. The median follow-up was 7.1 years for the entire cohort, 10.6 years for patients who did not receive rituximab, and 6.4 years for patients treated with rituximab.[<a href="#cit/section_6.86">

513386</a>

5134]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5135<ul id="_2483">

5136<li>

5137The 5-year EFS rate was 88.1% (95% CI, 75%&#8211;94.8%) for the whole cohort.<div class="pdq-content-list">

5138<ul id="_2484">

5139<li>

5140The 5-year EFS rate was 81% (95% CI, 60%&#8211;92.3%) for patients who did not receive rituximab.</li>

5141<li>

5142The 5-year EFS rate was 95.2% (95% CI, 77.3%&#8211;99.2%) for patients who received rituximab.</li>

5143</ul>

5144</div>

5145</li>

5146<li>

5147The 5-year OS rate was 95.2% for the whole cohort.<div class="pdq-content-list">

5148<ul id="_2485">

5149<li>

5150The 5-year OS rate was 90.5% for patients who did not receive rituximab.</li>

5151<li>

5152The 5-year OS rate was 100% for patients who received rituximab.</li>

5153</ul>

5154</div>

5155</li>

5156</ul>

5157</div>

5158</li>

5159</ol>

5160</div>

5161</section>

5162<section id="_1950">

5163<h4 id="_1950_toc">

5164Treatment options for recurrent or refractory primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma</h4>

5165<p id="_1951" tabindex="-1">

5166The U.S. Food and Drug Administration granted accelerated approval of pembrolizumab for the treatment of adult and pediatric patients with refractory primary mediastinal large B-cell lymphoma or who have relapsed after two or more previous lines of therapy. The approval was based on data from 53 patients (median age, 33 years; range, 20&#8211;61 years). The overall response rate was 41%, which included 12% complete responses and 29% partial responses.[<a href="#cit/section_6.87">

516787</a>

5168]</p>

5169</section>

5170<section id="_1800">

5171<h4 id="_1800_toc">

5172Treatment options under clinical evaluation for primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma</h4>

5173<p id="_1801" tabindex="-1">

5174Information about NCI-supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

5175NCI website</a>

5176. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

5177ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

5178.</p>

5179<p id="_1827" tabindex="-1">

5180The following are examples of national and/or institutional clinical trials that are currently being conducted:</p>

5181<div class="pdq-content-list">

5182<ul id="_1802">

5183<li>

5186APEC1621 (NCT03155620)</a>

5187</strong>

5188 (Pediatric MATCH: Targeted Therapy Directed by Genetic Testing in Treating Pediatric Patients with Relapsed or Refractory Advanced Solid Tumors, Non-Hodgkin Lymphomas, or Histiocytic Disorders)<strong>

5189:</strong>

5190 NCI-COG Pediatric Molecular Analysis for Therapeutic Choice (MATCH), referred to as Pediatric MATCH, will match targeted agents with specific molecular changes identified in a patient's tumor (refractory or recurrent). Children and adolescents aged 1 to 21 years are eligible for the trial.<p id="_1821" tabindex="-1">

Additional information can be obtained on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match">

5192NCI website</a>

5193 and <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620">

5196

5197</li>

5198<li>

5199<strong>

5202</strong>

5204:</strong>

5205 This phase III trial compares the effects of nivolumab combined with chemo-immunotherapy versus chemo-immunotherapy alone in treating patients with newly diagnosed primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma.</li>

Blood 130 (3): 267-270, 2017.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=28490569&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=28490569&amp;dopt=Abstract">

5545[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

5546</li>

5547</ol>

5548</div>

5549

5550 </section>
