5606<section id="_819">

5607<h3 id="_819_toc">

5608Incidence</h3>

5609<p id="_820" tabindex="-1">

5610Lymphoblastic lymphoma comprises approximately 20% of childhood non-Hodgkin lymphoma (NHL) cases.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

56111</a>

5612,<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

56132</a>

5614] For more information about the incidence of lymphoblastic lymphoma by age and sex distribution, see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_540">

5615Table 1</a>

5616.</p>

5617</section>

5618<section id="_821">

5619<h3 id="_821_toc">

5620Clinical Presentation</h3>

5621<p id="_294" tabindex="-1">

5622As many as 75% of patients with T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma will present with an anterior mediastinal mass, which may manifest as dyspnea, wheezing, stridor, dysphagia, or swelling of the head and neck.</p>

5623<p id="_1021" tabindex="-1">

5624Pleural and/or pericardial effusions may be present, and the involvement of lymph nodes, usually above the diaphragm, may be a prominent feature. There may also be involvement of bone, skin, bone marrow, central nervous system (CNS), abdominal organs (but rarely bowel), and, occasionally, other sites such as lymphoid tissue of Waldeyer ring, testes, or subcutaneous tissue. Abdominal involvement is less common in T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma than in Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia.</p>

5625<p id="_295" tabindex="-1">

5626Involvement of the bone marrow may lead to confusion about whether the patient has lymphoma with bone marrow involvement or leukemia with extramedullary disease. Traditionally, patients with more than 25% marrow blasts are considered to have T-cell acute lymphoblastic leukemia (ALL), and those with fewer than 25% marrow blasts are considered to have stage IV T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma. The World Health Organization (WHO) classifies lymphoblastic lymphoma as the same disease as ALL.[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

56273</a>

5628] The debate centers on whether they truly represent the same disease.[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

56294</a>

5630] It is not yet clear whether these arbitrary definitions are biologically distinct or relevant for treatment design.</p>

5631</section>

5632<section id="_823">

5633<h3 id="_823_toc">

5634Tumor Biology</h3>

5635<section id="_sm_CDR0000779369_1746">

5636<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779369_1746_toc">

5637Genomics of lymphoblastic lymphoma</h4>

5638<p id="_sm_CDR0000779369_293" tabindex="-1">

5639 Lymphoblastic lymphomas are usually positive for terminal deoxynucleotidyl transferase, with more than 75% of cases having a T-cell immunophenotype and the remaining cases having a precursor B-cell phenotype.[<a href="#cit/section_7.5">

56405</a>

5641] </p>

5642<p id="_sm_CDR0000779369_980" tabindex="-1">

5643As opposed to pediatric T-cell acute lymphoblastic leukemia (T-ALL), chromosomal abnormalities and the molecular biology of pediatric lymphoblastic lymphoma are not as well characterized. Many genomic alterations that occur in T-ALL also occur in T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma. Examples include the following:</p>

5644<div class="pdq-content-list">

5645<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779369_1750">

5646<li>

5647<em class="gene-name">

5648NOTCH1</em>

5649 and <em class="gene-name">

5650FBXW7</em>

5651 mutations (which also induce NOTCH pathway signaling) are common in T-ALL;[<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

56526</a>

5653] <em class="gene-name">

5654NOTCH1</em>

5655 and <em class="gene-name">

5656FBXW7</em>

5657 mutations are also observed in approximately 60% to 65% and 15% to 25% of T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma cases, respectively.[<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

56587</a>

5659-<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

566010</a>

5661]</li>

5662<li>

5663<em class="gene-name">

5664CDKN2A</em>

5665 at chromosome 9p21 is commonly altered in both T-ALL and in T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma, with approximately three-fourths of each showing deletions of this gene locus.[<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

56666</a>

5667,<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

566810</a>

5669]</li>

5670<li>

5671Loss of heterozygosity at chromosome 6q is observed in approximately 15% of T-ALL cases.[<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

567210</a>

5673]</li>

5674<li>

5675<em class="gene-name">

5676PTEN</em>

5677 mutations are observed in approximately 15% of T-ALL cases and in a comparable percentage of T-lymphoblastic lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

56786</a>

5679,<a href="#cit/section_7.9">

56809</a>

5681,<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

568210</a>

5683]</li>

5684<li>

5685Mutations in <em class="gene-name">

5686KMT2D</em>

5687 are observed in approximately 10% of T-lymphoblastic lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

568810</a>

5689] Other genes associated with epigenetics that are mutated in T-ALL include <em class="gene-name">

5690PHF6</em>

5691 and <em class="gene-name">

5692KMT2C</em>

5693.</li>

5694</ul>

5695</div>

5696<p id="_sm_CDR0000779369_1751" tabindex="-1">

5697For the genomic alterations described above, <em class="gene-name">

5698NOTCH1</em>

5699 and <em class="gene-name">

5700FBXW7</em>

5701 mutations may confer a more favorable prognosis for patients with T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma, while loss of heterozygosity at chromosome 6q, <em class="gene-name">

5702PTEN</em>

5703 mutations, and <em class="gene-name">

5704KMT2D</em>

5705 mutations may be associated with an inferior prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

57067</a>

5707-<a href="#cit/section_7.11">

570811</a>

5709] For example, one study noted that the presence of a <em class="gene-name">

5710KMT2D</em>

5711 and/or <em class="gene-name">

5712PTEN</em>

5713 mutation was associated with a high risk of relapse in patients with wild-type <em class="gene-name">

5714NOTCH1</em>

5715 or <em class="gene-name">

5716FBXW7</em>

5717, but these mutations were not associated with an increased risk of relapse in patients with mutations in <em class="gene-name">

5718NOTCH1</em>

5719 or <em class="gene-name">

5720FBXW7</em>

5721.[<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

572210</a>

5723] Studies with larger numbers of patients are needed to better define the critical genomic determinants of outcome for patients with T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma. </p>

5724<p id="_sm_CDR0000779369_1752" tabindex="-1">

5725There have been few studies of the genomic characteristics of B-lymphoblastic lymphoma. A report describing copy number alterations for pediatric B-lymphoblastic lymphoma cases noted that some gene deletions that are common in B-ALL (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

5726CDKN2A</em>

5727, <em class="gene-name">

5728IKZF1</em>

5729, and <em class="gene-name">

5730PAX5</em>

5731) appeared to occur with appreciable frequency in B-lymphoblastic lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

57324</a>

5733]</p>

5734</section>

5735</section>

5736<section id="_1088">

5737<h3 id="_1088_toc">

5738Prognostic Factors</h3>

5739<p id="_1089" tabindex="-1">

5740For information about prognostic factors for lymphoblastic lymphoma, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_552">

5741Prognosis and Prognostic Factors for Childhood NHL</a>

5742 section.</p>

5743</section>

5744<section id="_825">

5745<h3 id="_825_toc">

5746Standard Treatment Options for Lymphoblastic Lymphoma</h3>

5747<p id="_309" tabindex="-1">

5748Low-stage (stage I or stage II) lymphoblastic lymphoma is primarily a B-cell disease. Treatment with short, pulsed chemotherapy (i.e., doxorubicin, cyclophosphamide, vincristine, and prednisone [CHOP]), followed by 6 months of maintenance therapy, produces a disease-free survival (DFS) rate of about 60% and an overall survival (OS) rate exceeding 90%.[<a href="#cit/section_7.12">

574912</a>

5750,<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

575113</a>

5752] However, the use of an ALL treatment approach, consisting of induction, consolidation, and maintenance therapy for a total of 24 months, has produced DFS rates higher than 90% in children with low-stage lymphoblastic lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

575314</a>

5754-<a href="#cit/section_7.16">

575516</a>

5756] </p>

5757<p id="_476" tabindex="-1">

5758Patients with high-stage (stage III or stage IV) lymphoblastic lymphoma have DFS rates higher than 80%.[<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

575914</a>

5760,<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

576115</a>

5762,<a href="#cit/section_7.17">

576317</a>

5764] Mediastinal involvement is common, but radiation therapy is not necessary for patients with mediastinal masses, except in the emergency treatment of symptomatic superior vena cava obstruction or airway obstruction. In these cases, either corticosteroid therapy or low-dose radiation therapy is usually employed. For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_645">

5765Mediastinal masses</a>

5766 section. </p>

5767<p id="_2047" tabindex="-1">

5768The following studies have contributed to the development of current treatment regimens for pediatric patients with lymphoblastic lymphoma.</p>

5769<p id="_612" tabindex="-1">

5770The Pediatric Oncology Group conducted a trial to test the effectiveness of high-dose methotrexate in the treatment of patients with T-cell ALL and T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma. In the lymphoma patients (n = 66), high-dose methotrexate did not demonstrate a benefit, with a 5-year event-free survival (EFS) rate of 88%.[<a href="#cit/section_7.18">

577118</a>

5772][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5773Level of evidence A1</a>

5774] Of note, all of these patients received prophylactic cranial radiation therapy, even though other studies have shown that it is not required for patients with T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

577515</a>

5776,<a href="#cit/section_7.17">

577717</a>

5778] In this study, the benefit of adding the cardioprotectant dexrazoxane was tested in a randomized fashion. The addition of dexrazoxane did not affect patient outcomes, and it provided cardioprotective benefits, as demonstrated by echocardiographic and laboratory assessments.[<a href="#cit/section_7.19">

577919</a>

5780][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5781Level of evidence B4</a>

5782]</p>

5783<p id="_1996" tabindex="-1">

5784In the NHL-BFM-90 study, the 5-year DFS rate was 90%, and there was no difference in outcome between patients with stage III and stage IV disease.[<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

578514</a>

5786] Patients with precursor B-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma appeared to have similar results using the same therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

57872</a>

5788] All patients received prophylactic cranial radiation therapy. In the NHL-BFM-95 study, the amount of daunorubicin and asparaginase in induction was reduced and patients did not receive prophylactic cranial radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

578915</a>

5790] The DFS rate in this study was similar to the rate in the NHL-BFM-90 study; however, the EFS rate was lower, at 82%, because of a higher incidence of subsequent neoplasms.[<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

579115</a>

5792] A single-center study reported that patients treated for lymphoblastic lymphoma had a higher incidence of subsequent neoplasms than did patients treated for other pediatric NHL.[<a href="#cit/section_7.20">

579320</a>

5794] However, studies from the Children's Oncology Group (COG) and the Childhood Cancer Survivor Study Group did not support this finding.[<a href="#cit/section_7.17">

579517</a>

5796,<a href="#cit/section_7.21">

579721</a>

5798-<a href="#cit/section_7.23">

579923</a>

5800]</p>

5801<p id="_2048" tabindex="-1">

5802Evidence for chemotherapy (low-stage treatment regimens for lymphoblastic lymphoma):</p>

5803<div class="pdq-content-list">

5804<ol id="_2049">

5805<li>

5806<strong>

5807<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00004228">

5808COG-A5971 (NCT00004228)</a>

5809</strong>

5810<strong>

5811:</strong>

5812 Stage I or stage II patients (arm A0; localized disease) received a modified Children's Cancer Group (CCG) BFM regimen and a reduced number of intrathecal treatments during the maintenance phase.[<a href="#cit/section_7.16">

581316</a>

5814] <div class="pdq-content-list">

5815<ul id="_2050">

5816<li>

5817In 56 patients, the 5-year EFS rate was 90%, and the OS rate was 96%.</li>

5818</ul>

5819</div>

5820</li>

5821</ol>

5822</div>

5823<p id="_2051" tabindex="-1">

5824Evidence for chemotherapy (high-stage treatment regimens for lymphoblastic lymphoma):</p>

5825<div class="pdq-content-list">

5826<ol id="_885">

5827<li>

5828<strong>

5829GER-GPOH-NHL-BFM-95:</strong>

5830 Only CNS-positive patients received CNS radiation therapy. The treatment duration for patients with T-cell and B-cell precursor lymphoblastic lymphoma was 24 months.[<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

583114</a>

5832,<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

583315</a>

5834]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5835<ul id="_2421">

5836<li>

5837The 5-year DFS rate was 88%, and the OS rate was 85%.</li>

5838</ul>

5839</div>

5840</li>

5841<li>

5842<strong>

5843<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00004228">

5844COG-A5971 (NCT00004228)</a>

5845</strong>

5846<strong>

5847:</strong>

5848 This trial evaluated two strategies for CNS prophylaxis, without the use of CNS irradiation, for patients with stage III and stage IV lymphoblastic lymphoma. Patients were randomly assigned to receive either high-dose methotrexate during the interim maintenance phase (BFM-95) or intrathecal chemotherapy throughout the maintenance phase (CCG-BFM).[<a href="#cit/section_7.17">

584917</a>

5850][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5851Level of evidence A1</a>

5852]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5853<ul id="_2052">

5854<li>

5855First randomization:<div class="pdq-content-list">

5856<ul id="_2424">

5857<li>

5858Arm A1 (disseminated disease, no CNS disease): Modified CCG-BFM regimen without intensification. No high-dose methotrexate was administered during the interim maintenance phase, but intrathecal therapy was administered throughout the maintenance phase.</li>

5859<li>

5860Arm B1 (disseminated disease, no CNS disease): GER-GPOH-NHL-BFM-95 regimen without intensification and without intrathecal therapy during the maintenance phase.</li>

5861</ul>

5862</div>

5863</li>

5864<li>

5865Second randomization:<div class="pdq-content-list">

5866<ul id="_2425">

5867<li>

5868Arm A2 (disseminated disease, no CNS disease): Modified CCG-BFM regimen (arm A1) with intensified induction and delayed intensification.</li>

5869<li>

5870Arm B2 (disseminated disease, no CNS disease): GER-GPOH-NHL-BFM-95 regimen (arm B1) with intensified induction and delayed intensification. Patients with CNS disease were nonrandomly treated on arm B2 with the addition of radiation therapy.</li>

5871</ul>

5872</div>

5873</li>

5874</ul>

5875</div>

5876<p id="_2054" tabindex="-1">

5877Equivalent outcomes were observed for patients treated on arms A1, B1, A2, and B2. The 5-year EFS rates were 81%, 80%, 84%, and 80%, respectively. The OS rates were 84%, 88%, 85%, and 85%, respectively. Patients with CNS disease at diagnosis had a 5-year EFS rate of 63% and an OS rate of 81%.</p>

5878</li>

5879<li>

5880<strong>

5881COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00408005">

5882AALL0434 (NCT00408005)</a>

5883:</strong>

5884 In this trial, patients with stages II to IV T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma received COG-augmented, BFM-backbone therapy with Capizzi methotrexate and intrathecal chemotherapy through the maintenance phase. No patients with CNS disease received cranial radiation therapy because patients with CNS3 disease were not eligible. Patients with less than 1% minimal disseminated disease (MDD) in their bone marrow at diagnosis, assessed by flow cytometry, were nonrandomly assigned to treatment without nelarabine. Patients with greater than 1% MDD were randomly assigned to receive treatment with or without nelarabine.[<a href="#cit/section_7.23">

588523</a>

5886]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5887<ul id="_2002">

5888<li>

5889The overall 4-year DFS rate was 85%, and the OS rate was 89%.</li>

5890<li>

5891There was no difference in DFS observed between standard-risk and high-risk patients. Disease stage and MDD status at diagnosis also did not demonstrate differences in EFS. </li>

5892<li>

5893Although nelarabine was beneficial in patients with T-ALL, there was no statistical difference in outcome for patients with NHL who did or did not receive nelarabine. This result may be explained by small numbers of randomized NHL patients or lower CNS relapse rates in NHL patients (i.e., 1.4%).</li>

5894</ul>

5895</div>

5896 </li>

5897<li>

5898<strong>

5899COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT02112916">

5900AALL1231 (NCT02112916)</a>

5901:</strong>

5902 Patients who were newly diagnosed with T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma were treated with the AALL0434 backbone, with some treatment modifications. Dexamethasone was given instead of prednisone in the induction and maintenance phases, and two additional pegaspargase doses were given during the induction and delayed intensification phases. Patients were randomly assigned to receive treatment with or without bortezomib (four doses in induction and four doses in delayed intensification).[<a href="#cit/section_7.24">

590324</a>

5904]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5905<ul id="_2452">

5906<li>

5907There was an increased rate of toxic deaths in the AALL1231 trial (4%), compared with the AALL0434 trial (2%). </li>

5908<li>

5909Patients who received bortezomib had statistically significantly better 4-year EFS rates than those who did not receive bortezomib (EFS rates, 86% vs. 76%; <em>

5910P</em>

5911 = .04). However, patients in the bortezomib arm had EFS and OS rates similar to patients in both arms of the AALL0434 trial, who were treated with and without nelarabine. </li>

5912<li>

5913The poorer-than-expected outcome for patients treated without bortezomib cannot be explained solely by an increased toxic death rate. Despite more intensive therapy in the AALL1231 trial than in the AALL0434 trial, more relapses were observed in AALL1231. </li>

5914</ul>

5915</div>

5916</li>

5917</ol>

5918</div>

5919</section>

5920<section id="_827">

5921<h3 id="_827_toc">

5922Treatment Options for Recurrent or Refractory Lymphoblastic Lymphoma</h3>

5923<p id="_491" tabindex="-1">

5924For patients with recurrent or refractory lymphoblastic lymphoma, reports of survival range from 10% to 40%.[<a href="#cit/section_7.21">

592521</a>

5926,<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

592725</a>

5928]; [<a href="#cit/section_7.26">

592926</a>

5930][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5931Level of evidence B4</a>

5932]; [<a href="#cit/section_7.27">

593327</a>

5934,<a href="#cit/section_7.28">

593528</a>

5936][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5937Level of evidence C1</a>

5938] As in patients with Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia, chemoresistant disease is common. </p>

5939<p id="_904" tabindex="-1">

5940There are no standard treatment options for patients with recurrent or refractory disease. </p>

5941<p id="_1691" tabindex="-1">

5942Treatment options for recurrent or refractory lymphoblastic lymphoma include the following:</p>

5943<div class="pdq-content-list">

5944<ol id="_905">

5945<li>

5946Nelarabine or nelarabine-containing chemotherapy regimens (nelarabine, cyclophosphamide, and etoposide).[<a href="#cit/section_7.29">

594729</a>

5948-<a href="#cit/section_7.31">

594931</a>

5950]</li>

5951<li>

5952ICE regimen (ifosfamide, carboplatin, and etoposide).[<a href="#cit/section_7.32">

595332</a>

5954]</li>

5955<li>

5956Bortezomib with block 1 (four-drug induction), block 2 (cyclophosphamide, etoposide, and high-dose methotrexate), and block 3 (high-dose cytarabine and PEG-asparaginase).[<a href="#cit/section_7.33">

595733</a>

5958]</li>

5959<li>

5960Allogeneic stem cell transplantation (SCT).[<a href="#cit/section_7.34">

596134</a>

5962,<a href="#cit/section_7.35">

596335</a>

5964]</li>

5965</ol>

5966</div>

5967<p id="_1028" tabindex="-1">

5968Evidence (treatment of recurrent or refractory lymphoblastic lymphoma):</p>

5969<div class="pdq-content-list">

5970<ol id="_1029">

5971<li>

5972A COG phase II study of nelarabine (compound 506U78) as a single agent demonstrated a response rate of 40%.[<a href="#cit/section_7.29">

597329</a>

5974]</li>

5975<li>

5976A phase IV multicenter study of patients with recurrent or refractory T-cell leukemia/lymphoma (n = 28, 11 lymphoma) were treated with single-agent nelarabine.[<a href="#cit/section_7.30">

597730</a>

5978]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5979<ul id="_1842">

5980<li>

5981A complete response rate of 36% was observed. </li>

5982</ul>

5983</div>

5984</li>

5985<li>

5986Three small series have treated patients with recurrent or refractory T-cell leukemia/lymphoma using nelarabine, cyclophosphamide, and etoposide.[<a href="#cit/section_7.31">

598731</a>

5988,<a href="#cit/section_7.36">

598936</a>

5990,<a href="#cit/section_7.37">

599137</a>

5992]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5993<ol id="_1912" class="lower-alpha">

5994<li>

5995One study treated 27 patients.[<a href="#cit/section_7.31">

599631</a>

5997]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5998<ul id="_1843">

5999<li>

6000A partial/complete response rate of 85% was observed.</li>

6001<li>

6002However, 13% of patients developed greater than grade 3 neurotoxicity, and three patients died of neurotoxicity.</li>

6003<li>

6004Of the four lymphoma patients, one patient achieved a partial remission, but all patients eventually had disease progression.</li>

6005</ul>

6006</div>

6007</li>

6008<li>

6009The second study treated seven patients.[<a href="#cit/section_7.36">

601036</a>

6011]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6012<ul id="_1913">

6013<li>

6014The partial/complete response rate was 100%.</li>

6015<li>

6016Of the two lymphoma patients, both achieved partial responses but later progressed.</li>

6017</ul>

6018</div>

6019</li>

6020<li>

6021The third study treated ten patients.[<a href="#cit/section_7.37">

602237</a>

6023]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6024<ul id="_2480">

6025<li>

6026Forty-four percent of the patients had any response (complete response, complete response with incomplete platelet recovery, or partial response), and the complete response rate was 33%.</li>

6027<li>

6028Two of ten patients had severe peripheral neuropathy.</li>

6029</ul>

6030</div>

6031</li>

6032</ol>

6033</div>

6034</li>

6035<li>

6036On the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00873093">

6037AALL07P1 (NCT00873093)</a>

6038 trial, ten patients with T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma in first relapse were treated with bortezomib added to a four-drug induction regimen.[<a href="#cit/section_7.33">

603933</a>

6040] <div class="pdq-content-list">

6041<ul id="_2003">

6042<li>

6043Seven patients had a response; one patient had a complete response, two patients had unconfirmed complete responses, and four patients had partial responses.</li>

6044</ul>

6045</div>

6046</li>

6047<li>

6048 A BFM study showed an OS rate of 14% for patients relapsing after BFM front-line therapy; all patients who survived had undergone an allogeneic SCT.[<a href="#cit/section_7.28">

604928</a>

6050]</li>

6051<li>

6052A Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research analysis demonstrated that EFS was significantly worse when an autologous (4%) versus allogeneic (40%) donor stem cell source was used, with all failures resulting from progressive disease.[<a href="#cit/section_7.34">

605334</a>

6054]</li>

6055</ol>

6056</div>

6057</section>

6058<section id="_829">

6059<h3 id="_829_toc">

6060Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation for Lymphoblastic Lymphoma</h3>

6061<p id="_1068" tabindex="-1">

6062Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

6063NCI website</a>

6064. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

6065ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

6066.</p>

6067<p id="_1828" tabindex="-1">

6068The following is an example of a national and/or institutional clinical trial that is currently being conducted:</p>

6069<div class="pdq-content-list">

6070<ul id="_702">

6071<li>

6072<strong>

6073<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03155620">

6074APEC1621 (NCT03155620)</a>

6075</strong>

6076 (Pediatric MATCH: Targeted Therapy Directed by Genetic Testing in Treating Pediatric Patients with Relapsed or Refractory Advanced Solid Tumors, Non-Hodgkin Lymphomas, or Histiocytic Disorders)<strong>

6077:</strong>

6078 NCI-COG Pediatric Molecular Analysis for Therapeutic Choice (MATCH), referred to as Pediatric MATCH, will match targeted agents with specific molecular changes identified in a patient's tumor (refractory or recurrent). Children and adolescents aged 1 to 21 years are eligible for the trial.<p id="_1822" tabindex="-1">

6079Patients with tumors that have molecular variants addressed by treatment arms included in the trial will be offered treatment on Pediatric MATCH. Additional information can be obtained on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match">

6080NCI website</a>

6081 and <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620">

6082ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

6083.</p>

6084

6085</li>

6086</ul>

6087</div>

6093advanced clinical trial search</a>

6094 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

6095General information</a>

6096 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

Br J Haematol 179 (2): 272-283, 2017.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=28771662&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=28771662&amp;dopt=Abstract">

6224[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

6225</li>

6240[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

Pediatr Blood Cancer 69 (11): e29901, 2022.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=35989458&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=35989458&amp;dopt=Abstract">

6309<section id="_831">

6310<h3 id="_831_toc">

6311Incidence</h3>

6312<p id="_832" tabindex="-1">

6313Anaplastic large cell lymphoma accounts for approximately 10% of childhood non-Hodgkin lymphoma (NHL) cases.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

63141</a>

6315] For more information about the incidence of anaplastic large cell lymphoma by age and sex distribution, see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_540">

6316Table 1</a>

6317.</p>

6318</section>

6319<section id="_833">

6320<h3 id="_833_toc">

6321Clinical Presentation</h3>

6322<p id="_484" tabindex="-1">

6323Clinically, systemic anaplastic large cell lymphoma has a broad range of presentations. These include involvement of lymph nodes and a variety of extranodal sites, particularly skin and bone and, less often, gastrointestinal tract, lung, pleura, and muscle. Involvement of the central nervous system (CNS) and bone marrow is uncommon. </p>

6324<p id="_1030" tabindex="-1">

6325Anaplastic large cell lymphoma is often associated with systemic symptoms (e.g., fever, weight loss) and a prolonged waxing and waning course, making diagnosis difficult and often delayed. Patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma may present with signs and symptoms consistent with hemophagocytic lymphohistiocytosis.[<a href="#cit/section_8.2">

63262</a>

6327]</p>

6328<p id="_1031" tabindex="-1">

6329There is a subgroup of anaplastic large cell lymphoma patients who have leukemic peripheral blood involvement. These patients usually exhibit significant respiratory distress with diffuse lung infiltrates or pleural effusions and have hepatosplenomegaly.[<a href="#cit/section_8.3">

63303</a>

6331,<a href="#cit/section_8.4">

63324</a>

6333]</p>

6334</section>

6335<section id="_835">

6336<h3 id="_835_toc">

6337Tumor Biology</h3>

6338<section id="_sm_CDR0000779370_1746">

6339<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779370_1746_toc">

6340Genomics of anaplastic large cell lymphoma</h4>

6341<p id="_sm_CDR0000779370_297" tabindex="-1">

6342 While the predominant immunophenotype of anaplastic large cell lymphoma is mature T cell, null-cell disease (i.e., no T-cell, B-cell, or natural killer-cell surface antigen expression) does occur. The World Health Organization (WHO) classifies anaplastic large cell lymphoma as a subtype of peripheral T-cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_8.5">

63435</a>

6344] </p>

6345<p id="_sm_CDR0000779370_1032" tabindex="-1">

6346All anaplastic large cell lymphoma cases are CD30-positive. More than 90% of pediatric anaplastic large cell lymphoma cases have a chromosomal rearrangement involving the <em class="gene-name">

6347ALK</em>

6348 gene. About 85% of these chromosomal rearrangements will be t(2;5)(p23;q35), leading to the expression of the fusion protein NPM::ALK. The other 15% of cases are composed of variant <em class="gene-name">

6349ALK</em>

6350 translocations.[<a href="#cit/section_8.6">

63516</a>

6352] Anti-ALK immunohistochemical staining pattern is quite specific for the type of <em class="gene-name">

6353ALK</em>

6354 translocation. Cytoplasm and nuclear ALK staining is associated with NPM::ALK fusion protein, whereas cytoplasmic staining of ALK is only associated with the variant <em class="gene-name">

6355ALK</em>

6356 translocations, as shown in Table 6.[<a href="#cit/section_8.7">

63577</a>

6358]</p>

6359<table id="_sm_CDR0000779370_1749" class="table-default expandable-container">

6360<caption>

6362ALK</em>

64843q12.2 </td>

6485<td>

6486Rare</td>

6487</tr>

6489<td>

6490<em class="gene-name">

6491TPM4</em>

6493ALK</em>

649619p13 </td>

6502<em class="gene-name">

6505ALK</em>

6514<p id="_sm_CDR0000779370_736" tabindex="-1">

6515In adults, <em class="gene-name">

6516ALK</em>

6517-positive anaplastic large cell lymphoma is viewed differently from other peripheral T-cell lymphomas because prognosis tends to be superior.[<a href="#cit/section_8.8">

65188</a>

6519] Also, adult patients with <em class="gene-name">

6520ALK</em>

6521-negative anaplastic large cell lymphoma have an inferior outcome compared with patients who have <em class="gene-name">

6522ALK</em>

6523-positive disease.[<a href="#cit/section_8.9">

65249</a>

6525] In children, however, this difference in outcome between <em class="gene-name">

6526ALK</em>

6527-positive and <em class="gene-name">

6528ALK</em>

6529-negative disease has not been demonstrated. In addition, no correlation has been found between outcome and the specific <em class="gene-name">

6530ALK</em>

6531-translocation type.[<a href="#cit/section_8.10">

653210</a>

6533-<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

653412</a>

6535]</p>

6536<p id="_sm_CDR0000779370_737" tabindex="-1">

6537In a European series of 375 children and adolescents with systemic <em class="gene-name">

6538ALK</em>

6539-positive anaplastic large cell lymphoma, the presence of a small cell or lymphohistiocytic component was observed in 32% of patients and was significantly associated with a high risk of failure in the multivariate analysis, controlling for clinical characteristics (hazard ratio, 2.0; <em>

6540P</em>

6541 = .002).[<a href="#cit/section_8.11">

654211</a>

6543] The prognostic implication of the small cell variant of anaplastic large cell lymphoma was also shown in the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00059839">

6544COG-ANHL0131</a>

6545 (NCT00059839) study, despite a different chemotherapy backbone.[<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

654612</a>

6547]</p>

6548</section>

6549</section>

6550<section id="_1092">

6551<h3 id="_1092_toc">

6552Prognostic Factors</h3>

6553<p id="_1093" tabindex="-1">

6554For information on prognostic factors for anaplastic large cell lymphoma, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_552">

6555Prognosis and Prognostic Factors for Childhood NHL</a>

6556 section.</p>

6557</section>

6558<section id="_837">

6559<h3 id="_837_toc">

6560Standard Treatment Options for Anaplastic Large Cell Lymphoma</h3>

6561<p id="_886" tabindex="-1">

6562Children and adolescents with high-stage (stage III or IV) anaplastic large cell lymphoma have a disease-free survival rate of approximately 60% to 75%.[<a href="#cit/section_8.13">

656313</a>

6564-<a href="#cit/section_8.18">

656518</a>

6566] </p>

6567<p id="_1715" tabindex="-1">

6568It is unclear which treatment strategy is best for patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma. Current data do not suggest superiority of one treatment regimen over another for these standard treatment options. </p>

6569<p id="_1650" tabindex="-1">

6570Commonly used treatment regimens include the following:</p>

6571<div class="pdq-content-list">

6572<ol id="_887">

6573<li>

6574<strong>

6575POG-8314/POG-8719/POG 9219:</strong>

6576 Three cycles of chemotherapy (no radiation or maintenance therapy) for stage I and stage II disease.[<a href="#cit/section_8.19">

657719</a>

6578]</li>

6579<li>

6580<strong>

6581GER-GPOH-NHL-BFM-90:</strong>

6582 Prephase plus three cycles of chemotherapy (only for completely resected disease).[<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

658314</a>

6584]</li>

6585<li>

6586<strong>

6587APO regimen:</strong>

6588 Doxorubicin, prednisone, and vincristine.[<a href="#cit/section_8.15">

658915</a>

6590] This regimen can be administered in the outpatient setting. The duration of therapy is 52 weeks, and the cumulative dose of doxorubicin is 300 mg/m<span class="sup">

65912</span>

6592. No alkylator therapy is given.</li>

6593<li>

6594<strong>

6595<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006455">

6596FRE-IGR-ALCL99</a>

6597:</strong>

6598 Dexamethasone, cyclophosphamide, ifosfamide, etoposide, doxorubicin, intravenous (IV) methotrexate (3 g/m<span class="sup">

65992</span>

6600 in one study arm), cytarabine, prednisolone, and vinblastine.[<a href="#cit/section_8.20">

660120</a>

6602] This regimen usually requires hospitalization for administration. The total duration of therapy is 5 months, and the cumulative dose of doxorubicin is 150 mg/m<span class="sup">

66032</span>

6604.</li>

6605</ol>

6606</div>

6607<p id="_1033" tabindex="-1">

6608Evidence (treatment of anaplastic large cell lymphoma):</p>

6609<div class="pdq-content-list">

6610<ol id="_1034">

6611<li>

6612The POG-9219 study for patients with low-stage lymphoma used three cycles of doxorubicin, cyclophosphamide, vincristine, and prednisone (CHOP).[<a href="#cit/section_8.19">

661319</a>

6614]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6615<ul id="_1035">

6616<li>

6617The 5-year event-free survival (EFS) rate was 88% for patients with large cell lymphoma (anaplastic large cell lymphoma and diffuse large B-cell lymphoma). </li>

6618</ul>

6619</div>

6620</li>

6621<li>

6622The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006455">

6623FRE-IGR-ALCL99</a>

6624 trial used three cycles of chemotherapy after cytoreductive prophase for patients with stage I, completely resected disease. The therapy for patients without complete resection was the same as the therapy for patients with disseminated disease.[<a href="#cit/section_8.21">

662521</a>

6626][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6627Level of evidence B4</a>

6628] <div class="pdq-content-list">

6629<ul id="_1036">

6630<li>

6631Only 6 of 36 patients with stage I disease had complete resections; no treatment failures were reported for these 6 patients.</li>

6632<li>

6633The 3-year EFS (77%) and overall survival (OS) (97%) rates for patients without complete resection were not statistically different from the outcomes for patients with higher-stage disease.</li>

6634</ul>

6635</div>

6636</li>

6637<li>

6638The German Berlin-Frankfurt-M&#252;nster (BFM) group used six cycles of intensive pulsed therapy, similar to their B-cell NHL therapy (GER-GPOH-NHL-BFM-90 [NHL-BFM-90]).[<a href="#cit/section_8.14">

663914</a>

6640,<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

664122</a>

6642,<a href="#cit/section_8.23">

664323</a>

6644]; [<a href="#cit/section_8.20">

664520</a>

6646][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6647Level of evidence A1</a>

6648] Building on these results, the European Intergroup for Childhood NHL group conducted the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006455">

6649FRE-IGR-ALCL99</a>

6650 study (based on the GER-GPOH-NHL-BFM-90 regimen). <div class="pdq-content-list">

6651<ul id="_1666">

6652<li>

6653First, this randomized study demonstrated that methotrexate 1 g/m<span class="sup">

66542</span>

6655 infused over 24 hours plus intrathecal methotrexate and methotrexate 3 g/m<span class="sup">

66562</span>

6657 infused over 3 hours without intrathecal methotrexate yielded similar outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

665822</a>

6659][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810021&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810021&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6660Level of evidence A3</a>

6661] However, methotrexate 3 g/m<span class="sup">

66622</span>

6663 over 3 hours had less toxicity than methotrexate 1 g/m<span class="sup">

66642</span>

6665 over 24 hours.[<a href="#cit/section_8.22">

666622</a>

6667]; [<a href="#cit/section_8.20">

666820</a>

6669][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6670Level of evidence B1</a>

6671] </li>

6672<li>

6673Second, patients in the FRE-IGR-ALCL99 trial were randomly assigned to receive either limited vinblastine or prolonged (1 year) vinblastine exposure. Patients who received the vinblastine-plus-chemotherapy regimen had a better EFS rate in the first year after therapy (91%) than did those who did not receive vinblastine (74%); however, after 2 years of follow-up, the EFS rate was 73% for both groups.[<a href="#cit/section_8.23">

667423</a>

6675][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6676Level of evidence B1</a>

6677] This suggests that the longer therapy in the vinblastine group delayed, but did not prevent, relapse. </li>

6678</ul>

6679</div>

6680</li>

6681<li>

6682The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00059839">

6683COG-ANHL0131 (NCT00059839)</a>

6684 trial showed that the addition of vinblastine to the doxorubicin, prednisone, and vincristine (APO) regimen increased toxicity, but did not improve the survival of patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_8.12">

668512</a>

6686]</li>

6687<li>

6688The earlier Pediatric Oncology Group (POG) trial (POG-9317) demonstrated no benefit of adding methotrexate and high-dose cytarabine to 52 weeks of the APO regimen.[<a href="#cit/section_8.15">

668915</a>

6690]</li>

6691<li>

6692The Italian Association of Pediatric Hematology/Oncology group used a leukemia-like regimen for 24 months in the LNH-92 trial, with results similar to those of other regimens, although the duration of first remission was prolonged by the longer therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.16">

669316</a>

6694]</li>

6695<li>

6696The <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002590">

6697CCG-5941</a>

6698 study tested an approach similar to that used in the LNH-92 trial, with more intensive induction and consolidation with maintenance for a total duration of therapy of 1 year. Similar outcomes and similar significant increase in hematologic toxicity were observed.[<a href="#cit/section_8.17">

669917</a>

6700][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6701Level of evidence B4</a>

6702] </li>

6703<li>

6704One arm of the COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01979536">

6705ANHL12P1 (NCT01979536)</a>

6706 study added brentuximab vedotin to the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006455">

6707ALCL99</a>

6708 trial chemotherapy backbone, and 68 patients were enrolled.[<a href="#cit/section_8.24">

670924</a>

6710]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6711<ul id="_2427">

6712<li>

6713Patients who received the brentuximab vedotin&#8211;containing regimen had a 2-year EFS rate of 79% (95% confidence interval [CI], 67%&#8211;87%) and an OS rate of 97% (95% CI, 88%&#8211;99%). Patients who received the ALCL99 trial therapy without brentuximab vedotin had an estimated 5-year progression-free survival (PFS) rate of approximately 70%.</li>

6714<li>

6715All events occurred after completion of therapy, with median time from diagnosis to relapse of 7.5 months (range, 5.5&#8211;22.0 months).</li>

6716<li>

6717The addition of brentuximab vedotin to ALCL99 trial chemotherapy produced toxicity similar to what was observed in the ALCL99 trial.</li>

6718<li>

6719This study confirmed the poor prognosis associated with minimal disseminated disease (MDD) in the peripheral blood at diagnosis. Patients with MDD (n = 22; 37%) had a 5-year EFS rate of 57.8%, compared with patients without MDD (n = 37; 63%) who had a 5-year EFS rate of 84.9%.</li>

6720</ul>

6721</div>

6722 </li>

6723<li>

6724The second arm of the COG ANHL12P1 study added crizotinib to the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00006455">

6725ALCL99</a>

6726 trial chemotherapy backbone, and 66 patients were enrolled.[<a href="#cit/section_8.25">

672725</a>

6728]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6729<ul id="_2488">

6730<li>

6731Patients who received the crizotinib-containing regimen had a 2-year EFS rate of 76.8% (95% CI, 68.5%&#8211;88.1%) and a 2-year OS rate of 95.2% (95% CI, 85.7%&#8211;98.4%). Patients who received ALCL99 trial therapy without crizotinib had an estimated 5-year PFS rate of approximately 70%.</li>

6732<li>

6733All events occurred after completion of therapy, with median time from diagnosis to relapse of 7.4 months (range, 4.2&#8211;28.9 months).</li>

6734<li>

6735The addition of crizotinib produced an unexpectedly high rate of thromboembolic events. Eleven of the first 41 patients (26.8%; 95% CI, 14.2%&#8211;42.9%) experienced grade 2 or higher thromboembolic adverse events. Among 25 patients enrolled after instituting mandatory prophylactic anticoagulation, 2 patients (8%) experienced grade 2 or higher thromboembolic events. Given the high rate of thromboembolic events, the authors recommend not using crizotinib with ALCL99 trial chemotherapy.</li>

6736<li>

6737Patients with MDD in the peripheral blood at diagnosis (n = 20) had a 5-year EFS rate of 58.1%, while patients without MDD (n = 37) had a 5-year EFS rate of 85.6%.</li>

6738</ul>

6739</div>

6740</li>

6741</ol>

6742</div>

6743<p id="_699" tabindex="-1">

6744CNS involvement in patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma is rare at diagnosis. In an international study of systemic childhood anaplastic large cell lymphoma, 12 of 463 patients (2.6%) had CNS involvement, 3 of whom had isolated CNS disease (primary CNS lymphoma). For the CNS-positive group who received multiagent chemotherapy, including high-dose methotrexate, cytarabine,

6745and intrathecal treatment, the EFS rate was 50% (95% CI, 25%&#8211;75%), and the OS rate was 74% (95% CI, 45%&#8211;91%) at a median follow-up of 4.1 years. The role of cranial radiation therapy has been difficult to assess.[<a href="#cit/section_8.26">

674626</a>

6747]</p>

6748</section>

6749<section id="_839">

6750<h3 id="_839_toc">

6751Treatment Options for Recurrent or Refractory Anaplastic Large Cell Lymphoma</h3>

6752<p id="_983" tabindex="-1">

6753Unlike mature B-cell or lymphoblastic lymphoma, the survival rates for patients with recurrent or refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma is 40% to 60%.[<a href="#cit/section_8.27">

675427</a>

6755-<a href="#cit/section_8.30">

675630</a>

6757] </p>

6758<p id="_903" tabindex="-1">

6759There is no standard approach for the treatment of recurrent or refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma. </p>

6760<p id="_1692" tabindex="-1">

6761Treatment options for recurrent or refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma include the following:</p>

6762<div class="pdq-content-list">

6763<ol id="_906">

6764<li>

6765ICE regimen (ifosfamide, carboplatin, and etoposide).[<a href="#cit/section_8.31">

676631</a>

6767]</li>

6768<li>

6769Vinblastine.[<a href="#cit/section_8.32">

677032</a>

6771]</li>

6772<li>

6773Brentuximab vedotin.[<a href="#cit/section_8.33">

677433</a>

6775]; [<a href="#cit/section_8.34">

677634</a>

6777][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6778Level of evidence B4</a>

6779]</li>

6780<li>

6781Crizotinib [<a href="#cit/section_8.35">

678235</a>

6783] and other ALK inhibitors (e.g., alectinib and ceritinib).[<a href="#cit/section_8.36">

678436</a>

6785,<a href="#cit/section_8.37">

678637</a>

6787]</li>

6788<li>

6789Allogeneic or autologous stem cell transplantation (SCT).[<a href="#cit/section_8.38">

679038</a>

6791-<a href="#cit/section_8.40">

679240</a>

6793] </li>

6794</ol>

6795</div>

6796<p id="_1953" tabindex="-1">

6797Although remissions can be achieved with single-agent therapy (e.g., vinblastine, brentuximab vedotin, or crizotinib), CNS progressions after therapy have been observed in patients with recurrent anaplastic large cell lymphoma. A large retrospective review of a study database found that the incidence of CNS involvement at relapse is about 4%. The median time to relapse with CNS involvement was 8 months for 26 patients. The 3-year OS rate after relapse was about 50%.[<a href="#cit/section_8.41">

679841</a>

6799]</p>

6800<p id="_485" tabindex="-1">

6801Chemotherapy, followed by autologous SCT or allogeneic SCT, if remission can be achieved, has been employed in this setting.[<a href="#cit/section_8.28">

680228</a>

6803,<a href="#cit/section_8.29">

680429</a>

6805,<a href="#cit/section_8.38">

680638</a>

6807,<a href="#cit/section_8.39">

680839</a>

6809,<a href="#cit/section_8.42">

681042</a>

6811]</p>

6812<p id="_1844" tabindex="-1">

6813Evidence (chemotherapy and targeted therapy):</p>

6814<div class="pdq-content-list">

6815<ol id="_1845">

6816<li>

6817Vinblastine is active as a single agent in patients with recurrent or refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma.<div class="pdq-content-list">

6818<ol id="_2012" class="lower-alpha">

6819<li>

6820In one study, patients with recurrent or refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma were treated with vinblastine alone, and the following was observed:[<a href="#cit/section_8.32">

682132</a>

6822][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6823Level of evidence C1</a>

6824]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6825<ul id="_1847">

6826<li>

6827Vinblastine induced complete remission in 25 of 30 evaluable patients (83%).</li>

6828<li>

6829Nine of these 25 patients remained in complete remission, with a median follow-up of 7 years from the end of treatment.</li>

6830</ul>

6831</div>

6832</li>

6833</ol>

6834</div>

6835</li>

6836<li>

6837ALK kinase inhibitors are highly active in patients with recurrent anaplastic large cell lymphoma that express the NPM::ALK fusion protein.<div class="pdq-content-list">

6838<ol id="_2013" class="lower-alpha">

6839<li>

6840Crizotinib, a kinase inhibitor that blocks the activity of the NPM::ALK fusion protein, has been evaluated in children and adults with relapsed/refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_8.43">

684143</a>

6842]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6843<ul id="_1848">

6844<li>

6845Of 26 patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma who were treated with crizotinib on a pediatric phase I study with a phase II extension, 21 patients achieved complete responses.[<a href="#cit/section_8.35">

684635</a>

6847,<a href="#cit/section_8.44">

684844</a>

6849][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6850Level of evidence B4</a>

6851]</li>

6852<li>

6853Although complete responses are common, the duration of therapy remains unclear.[<a href="#cit/section_8.45">

685445</a>

6855][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6856Level of evidence C2</a>

6857]</li>

6858<li>

6859The most common adverse event was neutropenia.[<a href="#cit/section_8.44">

686044</a>

6861]</li>

6862</ul>

6863</div>

6864</li>

6865<li>

6866Alectinib is a second-generation ALK inhibitor that showed superiority over crizotinib in phase III studies for patients with <em class="gene-name">

6867ALK</em>

6868-mutant non-small cell lung cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_8.46">

686946</a>

6870] <div class="pdq-content-list">

6871<ul id="_2014">

6872<li>

6873In a study of ten patients with relapsed/refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma, eight achieved objective responses, with six complete responses. Five patients achieved durable remissions (397&#8211;925 days at the time of the report) to alectinib as a single agent.[<a href="#cit/section_8.36">

687436</a>

6875]</li>

6876</ul>

6877</div>

6878</li>

6879<li>

6880Ceritinib, an orally administered, second-generation ALK inhibitor, has been shown to be safe and effective in pediatric patients with <em class="gene-name">

6881ALK</em>

6882-positive tumors whose cancer relapsed. In a phase I dose-finding study, eight patients with anaplastic large cell lymphoma were evaluable for response.[<a href="#cit/section_8.37">

688337</a>

6884]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6885<ul id="_2428">

6886<li>

6887 Twenty-five percent of the patients achieved a complete response, and 50% achieved a partial response.</li>

6888</ul>

6889</div>

6890</li>

6891</ol>

6892</div>

6893</li>

6894<li>

6895Brentuximab vedotin has been evaluated in adults with anaplastic large cell lymphoma.<div class="pdq-content-list">

6896<ol id="_2015" class="lower-alpha">

6897<li>

6898 In a phase II study of adults and adolescents with CD30-positive cancers, patients were administered a dose of 1.8 mg/kg of brentuximab vedotin every 3 weeks for approximately 1 year. The median age of patients was 52 years (range, 14&#8211;76 years). Sixteen of 58 patients (28%) had <em class="gene-name">

6899ALK</em>

6900-positive anaplastic large cell lymphoma, and 42 of 58 patients (72%) had <em class="gene-name">

6901ALK</em>

6902-negative anaplastic large cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_8.33">

690333</a>

6904]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6905<ul id="_1850">

6906<li>

6907 Complete remission rates of approximately 55% to 60% and partial remission rates of 29% were observed.[<a href="#cit/section_8.33">

690833</a>

6909]</li>

6910<li>

6911For the 38 patients who achieved a complete remission (28 <em class="gene-name">

6912ALK</em>

6913-negative patients, 10 <em class="gene-name">

6914ALK</em>

6915-positive patients), the 5-year PFS rate was 79%, and the OS rate was 57%. PFS was similar for <em class="gene-name">

6916ALK</em>

6917-positive and <em class="gene-name">

6918ALK</em>

6919-negative patients.[<a href="#cit/section_8.47">

692047</a>

6921]</li>

6922<li>

6923Sixteen patients (11 <em class="gene-name">

6924ALK</em>

6925-negative patients, 5 <em class="gene-name">

6926ALK</em>

6927-positive patients) remained in remission without the start of new therapy other than consolidative SCT at 5 or more years from the end of treatment with brentuximab vedotin. Of the five <em class="gene-name">

6928ALK</em>

6929-positive patients who remained in remission, four received an allogeneic SCT, and one received no therapy other than brentuximab vedotin.[<a href="#cit/section_8.47">

693047</a>

6931]</li>

6932</ul>

6933</div>

6934</li>

6935</ol>

6936</div>

6937</li>

6938<li>

6939Brentuximab vedotin has been evaluated in children with recurrent or refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma.<div class="pdq-content-list">

6940<ol id="_2017" class="lower-alpha">

6941<li>

6942 In a phase II study, 17 patients (median age, 11 years) were treated with brentuximab vedotin at a dose of 1.8 mg/kg every 21 days. Twelve patients were <em class="gene-name">

6943ALK</em>

6944 positive and five were <em class="gene-name">

6945ALK</em>

6946 negative. After treatment with brentuximab vedotin, nine patients then received either autologous or allogeneic transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_8.34">

694734</a>

6948][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6949Level of evidence B4</a>

6950]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6951<ul id="_2018">

6952<li>

6953The overall response rate was 53% (9 of 17 patients), with a complete response rate of 41% (7 of

695417 patients), a partial response rate of 12% (2 of 17 patients), a stable disease rate of 29% (5 of 17 patients), and a progressive disease rate of 18% (3 of 17 patients). </li>

6955<li>

6956The median EFS was 4.8 months.</li>

6957<li>

6958The OS rate was 93.3% at 24 months.</li>

6959</ul>

6960</div>

6961</li>

6962</ol>

6963</div>

6964</li>

6965</ol>

6966</div>

6967<p id="_1042" tabindex="-1">

6968Evidence (autologous vs. allogeneic SCT):</p>

6969<div class="pdq-content-list">

6970<ol id="_1043">

6971<li>

6972A retrospective study of relapsed or refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma in patients who received BFM-type first-line therapy, reinduction chemotherapy, followed by autologous SCT reported the following:[<a href="#cit/section_8.29">

697329</a>

6974][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6975Level of evidence B4</a>

6976] <div class="pdq-content-list">

6977<ul id="_1044">

6978<li>

6979A 5-year EFS rate of 59% and an OS rate of 77%. However, outcome of patients with bone marrow or CNS involvement, relapse during first-line therapy, or CD3-positive anaplastic large cell lymphoma was poor. These patients may benefit from allogeneic transplantation. </li>

6980</ul>

6981</div>

6982</li>

6983<li>

6984In a European prospective study of patients with relapsed or refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma, primary refractory patients received aggressive multiagent chemotherapy and underwent allogeneic SCT. Relapsed patients with CD3-positive disease received less-intense multiagent chemotherapy and allogeneic SCT (recommended therapy) or autologous SCT if no donors were available. Relapsed patients with CD3-negative disease (&lt;1 year from diagnosis) received less-aggressive chemotherapy and autologous SCT. Relapsed patients with CD3-negative disease (&gt;1 year from diagnosis) received single-agent vinblastine for 2 years.[<a href="#cit/section_8.30">

698530</a>

6986] The study demonstrated the following results:<div class="pdq-content-list">

6987<ul id="_2004">

6988<li>

6989For the entire cohort, the 5-year EFS rate was approximately 50%, and the OS rate was 77%.</li>

6990<li>

6991For patients with refractory disease or early relapse, reinduction with multiagent chemotherapy followed by allogeneic SCT achieved the best results (i.e., long-term EFS rate, 65%). </li>

6992<li>

6993Patients who received autologous SCT fared much worse (i.e., long-term EFS rate, 25%).</li>

6994<li>

6995 Patients who had a late relapse (&gt;1 year from diagnosis) were treated with vinblastine for 2 years and achieved a 5-year EFS rate of 81%. </li>

6996</ul>

6997</div>

6998</li>

6999<li>

7000Several additional studies suggest that allogeneic SCT may result in better outcome for refractory/relapsed anaplastic large cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_8.38">

700138</a>

7002,<a href="#cit/section_8.40">

700340</a>

7004,<a href="#cit/section_8.42">

700542</a>

7006,<a href="#cit/section_8.48">

700748</a>

7008]</li>

7009</ol>

7010</div>

7011</section>

7012<section id="_841">

7013<h3 id="_841_toc">

7014Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation for Anaplastic Large Cell Lymphoma</h3>

7015<p id="_899" tabindex="-1">

7016Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

7017NCI website</a>

7018. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

7019ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

7020.</p>

7021<p id="_1829" tabindex="-1">

7022The following are examples of national and/or institutional clinical trials that are currently being conducted:</p>

7023<div class="pdq-content-list">

7024<ul id="_487">

7025<li>

7026<strong>

7027<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03155620">

7028APEC1621 (NCT03155620)</a>

7029</strong>

7030 (Pediatric MATCH: Targeted Therapy Directed by Genetic Testing in Treating Pediatric Patients with Relapsed or Refractory Advanced Solid Tumors, Non-Hodgkin Lymphomas, or Histiocytic Disorders)<strong>

7031:</strong>

7032 NCI-COG Pediatric Molecular Analysis for Therapeutic Choice (MATCH), referred to as Pediatric MATCH, will match targeted agents with specific molecular changes identified in a patient's tumor (refractory or recurrent). Children and adolescents aged 1 to 21 years are eligible for the trial.<p id="_1823" tabindex="-1">

7033Patients with tumors that have molecular variants addressed by treatment arms included in the trial will be offered treatment on Pediatric MATCH. Additional information can be obtained on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match">

7034NCI website</a>

7035 and <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620">

7036ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

7037.</p>

7038

7039</li>

7040<li>

7041<strong>

7042<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03703050">

7043NCT03703050</a>

7044</strong>

7045 (Nivolumab for Pediatric and Adult Relapsing/Refractory <em class="gene-name">

7046ALK</em>

7047-Positive Anaplastic Large Cell Lymphoma, for Evaluation of Response in Patients With Progressive Disease [Cohort 1] or as Consolidative Immunotherapy in Patients in Complete Remission After Relapse [Cohort 2] [NIVO-ALCL])<strong>

7048:</strong>

7049 <em class="gene-name">

7050ALK</em>

7051-positive anaplastic large cell lymphoma commonly expresses PD-L1,[<a href="#cit/section_8.49">

705249</a>

7053,<a href="#cit/section_8.50">

705450</a>

7055] and two patients with <em class="gene-name">

7056ALK</em>

7057-positive anaplastic large cell lymphoma treated with the checkpoint inhibitor nivolumab achieved complete responses.[<a href="#cit/section_8.51">

705851</a>

7059,<a href="#cit/section_8.52">

706052</a>

7061] Further clinical evaluation is needed to define the role of checkpoint blockade for children with <em class="gene-name">

7062ALK</em>

7063-positive anaplastic large cell lymphoma.</li>

7064</ul>

7065</div>

7066<section id="_TrialSearch_841_sid_9">

7067<h4 id="_TrialSearch_841_sid_9_toc">

7068Current Clinical Trials</h4>

7069<p id="_TrialSearch_841_22" tabindex="-1">

7070Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

7071advanced clinical trial search</a>

7072 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

7073General information</a>

7074 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

N Engl J Med 374 (1): 95-6, 2016.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=26736010&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=26736010&amp;dopt=Abstract">

7258[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

7259</li>

7260<li id="section_8.46">

7261Peters S, Camidge DR, Shaw AT, et al.: Alectinib versus Crizotinib in Untreated ALK-Positive Non-Small-Cell Lung Cancer. N Engl J Med 377 (9): 829-838, 2017.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=28586279&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=28586279&amp;dopt=Abstract">

7262[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

7263</li>

7264<li id="section_8.47">

7265Pro B, Advani R, Brice P, et al.: Five-year results of brentuximab vedotin in patients with relapsed or refractory systemic anaplastic large cell lymphoma. Blood 130 (25): 2709-2717, 2017.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=28974506&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=28974506&amp;dopt=Abstract">

7266[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

7267</li>

7268<li id="section_8.48">

7269Fukano R, Mori T, Kobayashi R, et al.: Haematopoietic stem cell transplantation for relapsed or refractory anaplastic large cell lymphoma: a study of children and adolescents in Japan. Br J Haematol 168 (4): 557-63, 2015.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=25312752&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=25312752&amp;dopt=Abstract">

7270[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

7271</li>

7272<li id="section_8.49">

7273Panjwani PK, Charu V, DeLisser M, et al.: Programmed death-1 ligands PD-L1 and PD-L2 show distinctive and restricted patterns of expression in lymphoma subtypes. Hum Pathol 71: 91-99, 2018.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=29122656&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=29122656&amp;dopt=Abstract">

7274[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

7275</li>

7276<li id="section_8.50">

7277Shen J, Li S, Medeiros LJ, et al.: PD-L1 expression is associated with ALK positivity and STAT3 activation, but not outcome in patients with systemic anaplastic large cell lymphoma. Mod Pathol 33 (3): 324-333, 2020.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=31383967&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=31383967&amp;dopt=Abstract">

7278[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

7279</li>

7280<li id="section_8.51">

7281Hebart H, Lang P, Woessmann W: Nivolumab for Refractory Anaplastic Large Cell Lymphoma: A Case Report. Ann Intern Med 165 (8): 607-608, 2016.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27750310&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27750310&amp;dopt=Abstract">

7282[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

7283</li>

7284<li id="section_8.52">

7285Rigaud C, Abbou S, Minard-Colin V, et al.: Efficacy of nivolumab in a patient with systemic refractory ALK+ anaplastic large cell lymphoma. Pediatr Blood Cancer 65 (4): , 2018.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=29193772&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=29193772&amp;dopt=Abstract">

7286[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

7287</li>

7288</ol>

7289</div>

7290

7291 </section>

7297 <h2>

7298Lymphoproliferative Disease Associated With Immunodeficiency in Children</h2>

7299

7300 <div id="_section_343" class="pdq-sections">
