7363<section id="_843">

7364<h3 id="_843_toc">

7365Incidence</h3>

7366<p id="_299" tabindex="-1">

7367The incidence of lymphoproliferative disease or lymphoma is 100-fold higher in immunocompromised children than in the general population. The causes of such immune deficiencies include the following:</p>

7368<div class="pdq-content-list">

7369<ul id="_1046">

7370<li>

7371A genetically inherited defect (primary immunodeficiency).</li>

7372<li>

7373Secondary to HIV infection.</li>

7374<li>

7375Iatrogenic disorders after transplantation (solid organ transplant or allogeneic hematopoietic stem cell transplantation [HSCT]). Epstein-Barr virus (EBV) is associated with most of these tumors, but some tumors are not associated with any infectious agent.</li>

7376<li>

7377Iatrogenic disorders from chemotherapy.</li>

7378</ul>

7379</div>

7380</section>

7381<section id="_845">

7382<h3 id="_845_toc">

7383Clinical Presentation</h3>

7384<p id="_300" tabindex="-1">

7385Non-Hodgkin lymphoma (NHL) associated with immunodeficiency is usually aggressive, with most cases occurring in extralymphatic sites and a higher incidence of primary central nervous system (CNS) involvement.[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

73861</a>

7387-<a href="#cit/section_9.4">

73884</a>

7389] </p>

7390</section>

7391<section id="_1651">

7392<h3 id="_1651_toc">

7393Lymphoproliferative Disease Associated With Primary Immunodeficiency</h3>

7394<p id="_301" tabindex="-1">

7395Lymphoproliferative disease observed in primary immunodeficiency usually shows an aggressive mature B-cell phenotype and large cell histology.[<a href="#cit/section_9.2">

73962</a>

7397] Mature T-cell lymphoma and anaplastic large cell lymphoma have been observed.[<a href="#cit/section_9.2">

73982</a>

7399] Children with primary immunodeficiency and NHL are more likely to have high-stage disease and present with symptoms related to extranodal disease, particularly in the gastrointestinal tract and CNS.[<a href="#cit/section_9.2">

74002</a>

7401]</p>

7402<section id="_1652">

7403<h4 id="_1652_toc">

7404Treatment options for lymphoproliferative disease associated with primary immunodeficiency</h4>

7405<p id="_1693" tabindex="-1">

7406Treatment options for lymphoproliferative disease associated with primary immunodeficiency include the following:</p>

7407<div class="pdq-content-list">

7408<ol id="_1694">

7409<li>

7410Chemotherapy with or without rituximab.</li>

7411<li>

7412Allogeneic stem cell transplantation (SCT).</li>

7413</ol>

7414</div>

7415<p id="_1052" tabindex="-1">

7416Patients with primary immunodeficiency can achieve complete and durable remissions with standard chemotherapy regimens for NHL, although toxicity is increased.[<a href="#cit/section_9.2">

74172</a>

7418]; [<a href="#cit/section_9.5">

74195</a>

7420][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

7421Level of evidence C1</a>

7422] Recurrences in these patients are common and may not represent the same clonal disease.[<a href="#cit/section_9.6">

74236</a>

7424] Immunologic correction through allogeneic SCT is often required to prevent recurrences. </p>

7425</section>

7426</section>

7427<section id="_1851">

7428<h3 id="_1851_toc">

7429NHL Associated With DNA Repair Defect Syndromes</h3>

7430<p id="_1852" tabindex="-1">

7431The incidence of NHL is increased in patients with DNA repair syndromes, including ataxia-telangiectasia, Nijmegen breakage syndrome, and constitutional mismatch repair deficiency. Aggressive mature B-cell NHL accounts for the majority of NHL seen in patients with ataxia-telangiectasia (84%) and Nijmegen breakage syndrome (46%), while T-cell lymphoblastic lymphoma (81%) is observed in patients with constitutional mismatch repair deficiency.[<a href="#cit/section_9.5">

74325</a>

7433]</p>

7434<section id="_1853">

7435<h4 id="_1853_toc">

7436Treatment options for NHL associated with DNA repair defect syndromes</h4>

7437<p id="_1854" tabindex="-1">

7438 Patients with DNA repair defects are particularly difficult to treat.[<a href="#cit/section_9.7">

74397</a>

7440,<a href="#cit/section_9.8">

74418</a>

7442] Overall 5-year to 10-year survival rates are poor, at 40% to 60%.[<a href="#cit/section_9.5">

74435</a>

7444,<a href="#cit/section_9.9">

74459</a>

7446]</p>

7447<p id="_1855" tabindex="-1">

7448Treatment options for NHL associated with DNA repair defect syndromes include the following:</p>

7449<div class="pdq-content-list">

7450<ol id="_1856">

7451<li>

7452Chemotherapy.<div class="pdq-content-list">

7453<ul id="_2005">

7454<li>

7455Cytotoxic agents produce much more toxicity and greatly increase the risk of subsequent neoplasms in these patients. One review reported that dose reduction of chemotherapeutic drugs was effective and reduced toxic effects, but did not prevent subsequent neoplasms (10-year incidence, 25%).[<a href="#cit/section_9.9">

74569</a>

7457]</li>

7458</ul>

7459</div>

7460</li>

7461</ol>

7462</div>

7463</section>

7464</section>

7465<section id="_1654">

7466<h3 id="_1654_toc">

7467HIV-associated NHL</h3>

7468<p id="_1655" tabindex="-1">

7469NHL in children with HIV often presents with fever, weight loss, and symptoms related to extranodal disease, such as abdominal pain or CNS symptoms.[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

74701</a>

7471] Most childhood HIV-related NHL is of mature B-cell phenotype but with a spectrum, including primary effusion lymphoma, primary CNS lymphoma, mucosa-associated lymphoid tissue (MALT), Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia, and diffuse large B-cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_9.10">

747210</a>

7473,<a href="#cit/section_9.11">

747411</a>

7475]</p>

7476<p id="_1656" tabindex="-1">

7477HIV-associated NHL can be broadly grouped into the following three subcategories:</p>

7478<div class="pdq-content-list">

7479<ol id="_1048">

7480<li>

7481Systemic (nodal and extranodal). Approximately 80% of all NHL in HIV patients is considered to be systemic.[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

74821</a>

7483]</li>

7484<li>

7485Primary CNS lymphoma.</li>

7486<li>

7487Body cavity&#8211;based lymphoma, also referred to as primary effusion lymphoma. Primary effusion lymphoma, a unique lymphomatous effusion associated with human herpesvirus 8 (HHV-8) or Kaposi sarcoma herpesvirus infection, is primarily observed in adults infected with HIV but has been reported in HIV-infected children.[<a href="#cit/section_9.12">

748812</a>

7489]</li>

7490</ol>

7491</div>

7492<p id="_1049" tabindex="-1">

7493Highly active antiretroviral therapy has decreased the incidence of NHL in HIV-positive individuals, particularly for primary CNS lymphoma cases.[<a href="#cit/section_9.13">

749413</a>

7495,<a href="#cit/section_9.14">

749614</a>

7497] </p>

7498<section id="_1657">

7499<h4 id="_1657_toc">

7500Treatment options for HIV-associated NHL</h4>

7501<p id="_1695" tabindex="-1">

7502Treatment options for HIV-associated NHL include the following:</p>

7503<div class="pdq-content-list">

7504<ol id="_1696">

7505<li>

7506Chemotherapy with or without rituximab.</li>

7507</ol>

7508</div>

7509<p id="_760" tabindex="-1">

7510In the era of highly active antiretroviral therapy, children with HIV and NHL are treated with standard chemotherapy regimens for NHL; however, the prevention (using prophylaxis) and early detection of infection is warranted.[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

75111</a>

7512,<a href="#cit/section_9.13">

751313</a>

7514,<a href="#cit/section_9.14">

751514</a>

7516] Although the number of pediatric patients with HIV-associated NHL is too small to perform meaningful clinical trials, studies of adult patients support the addition of rituximab to standard treatment regimens.[<a href="#cit/section_9.15">

751715</a>

7518] Treatment of recurrent disease is based on histology using standard approaches.</p>

7519</section>

7520</section>

7521<section id="_1658">

7522<h3 id="_1658_toc">

7523Posttransplant Lymphoproliferative Disease (PTLD)</h3>

7524<p id="_302" tabindex="-1">

7525PTLD represents a spectrum of clinically and morphologically heterogeneous lymphoid proliferations. Essentially all PTLDs after HSCT are associated with EBV, but EBV-negative PTLD can be seen after solid organ transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_9.3">

75263</a>

7527] While most PTLDs are of B-cell phenotype, approximately 10% are mature (peripheral) T-cell lymphomas.[<a href="#cit/section_9.16">

752816</a>

7529] The B-cell stimulation by EBV may result in multiple clones of proliferating B cells, and both polymorphic and monomorphic histologies may be present in a patient, even within the same lesion of PTLD.[<a href="#cit/section_9.17">

753017</a>

7531] Thus, histology of a single biopsied site may not be representative of the entire disease process. </p>

7532<p id="_1051" tabindex="-1">

7533The World Health Organization (WHO) has classified PTLD into the following three subtypes:[<a href="#cit/section_9.16">

753416</a>

7535] </p>

7536<div class="pdq-content-list">

7537<ul id="_548">

7538<li>

7539Early lesion: Early lesions show germinal center expansion, but tissue architecture remains normal. </li>

7540<li>

7541Polymorphic PTLD: Presence of infiltrating T cells, disruption of nodal architecture, and necrosis distinguish polymorphic PTLD from early lesions. </li>

7542<li>

7543Monomorphic PTLD: Histologies observed in patients with the monomorphic subtype are like those observed in NHL, with diffuse large B-cell lymphoma being the most common histology, followed by Burkitt lymphoma/leukemia myeloma, plasmacytoma, and Hodgkin-like PTLD rarely occur in patients with this subtype. T-cell PTLD is seen in about 10% of PTLD cases, may be EBV positive or EBV negative, and is usually of the mature T-cell subtype.[<a href="#cit/section_9.16">

754416</a>

7545]</li>

7546</ul>

7547</div>

7548<p id="_549" tabindex="-1">

7549EBV lymphoproliferative disease posttransplant may manifest as isolated hepatitis, lymphoid interstitial pneumonitis, meningoencephalitis, or an infectious mononucleosis-like syndrome. The definition of PTLD is frequently limited to lymphomatous lesions (low stage or high stage), which are often extranodal (frequently in the allograft).[<a href="#cit/section_9.3">

75503</a>

7551] PTLD may less commonly present as a rapidly progressive, high-stage disease that clinically resembles septic shock, and these patients have a poor prognosis; however, the use of rituximab and low-dose chemotherapy may improve outcomes in these patients.[<a href="#cit/section_9.18">

755218</a>

7553,<a href="#cit/section_9.19">

755419</a>

7555] U.S. transplant and cancer registries show that PTLD accounts for about 3% of all pediatric NHL diagnoses; 65% of PTLDs have diffuse large B-cell lymphoma histology, and 9% of PTLDs have Burkitt histology.[<a href="#cit/section_9.20">

755620</a>

7557]</p>

7558<section id="_1659">

7559<h4 id="_1659_toc">

7560Treatment options for PTLD</h4>

7561<p id="_1660" tabindex="-1">

7562Treatment options for PTLD include the following:</p>

7563<div class="pdq-content-list">

7564<ol id="_909">

7565<li>

7566For localized resectable disease, surgical resection and, if possible, reduction of immunosuppressive therapy.</li>

7567<li>

7568Rituximab therapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_9.21">

756921</a>

7570]</li>

7571<li>

7572Standard or slightly modified lymphoma-specific chemotherapy regimens for the specific histology, with or without rituximab for B-cell PTLD.[<a href="#cit/section_9.22">

757322</a>

7574-<a href="#cit/section_9.24">

757524</a>

7576]</li>

7577<li>

7578For EBV-positive B-cell PTLD, low-dose chemotherapy with or without rituximab.[<a href="#cit/section_9.19">

757919</a>

7580]; [<a href="#cit/section_9.25">

758125</a>

7582][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

7583Level of evidence C2</a>

7584]</li>

7585</ol>

7586</div>

7587<p id="_597" tabindex="-1">

7588First-line therapy for patients with PTLD is to reduce immunosuppressive therapy as much as possible.[<a href="#cit/section_9.25">

758925</a>

7590,<a href="#cit/section_9.26">

759126</a>

7592] However, this may not be possible because of the increased risk of organ rejection or graft-versus-host disease (GVHD).</p>

7593<p id="_1661" tabindex="-1">

7594Rituximab, an anti-CD20 antibody, has been used in the posttransplant setting. Rituximab as a single agent to treat PTLD after organ transplant has demonstrated efficacy in adult patients, but data are lacking in pediatric patients. </p>

7595<p id="_2398" tabindex="-1">

7596Evidence (rituximab):</p>

7597<div class="pdq-content-list">

7598<ol id="_2399">

7599<li>

7600A study of 144 children and adults who developed post-HSCT PTLD reported the following:[<a href="#cit/section_9.21">

760121</a>

7602][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

7603Level of evidence C1</a>

7604] <div class="pdq-content-list">

7605<ul id="_2400">

7606<li>

7607Approximately 70% of the patients who received rituximab survived.</li>

7608<li>

7609 Survival was also associated with reduction of immunosuppression, but older age, extranodal disease, and acute GVHD were predictors of poor

7610outcome.</li>

7611</ul>

7612</div>

7613</li>

7614</ol>

7615</div>

7616<p id="_2401" tabindex="-1">

7617For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/b-cell-lymphoma-treatment-pdq#_1946">

7618Polymorphic Posttransplant Lymphoproliferative Disorder</a>

7619 section in B-Cell Non-Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment.</p>

7620<p id="_1662" tabindex="-1">

7621Low-intensity chemotherapy has been effective in patients with EBV-positive, CD20-positive B-lineage PTLD.[<a href="#cit/section_9.19">

762219</a>

7623,<a href="#cit/section_9.27">

762427</a>

7625] An event-free survival rate of 67% was demonstrated in a Children's Oncology Group study using rituximab plus cyclophosphamide and prednisone in children with PTLD after solid organ transplant in whom immune suppression was reduced.[<a href="#cit/section_9.19">

762619</a>

7627][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

7628Level of evidence B4</a>

7629] Other studies suggest that modified conventional lymphoma therapy is effective for patients who have PTLD with <em class="gene-name">

7630MYC</em>

7631 translocations and Burkitt histology.[<a href="#cit/section_9.23">

763223</a>

7633,<a href="#cit/section_9.24">

763424</a>

7635][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

7636Level of evidence C2</a>

7637] Patients with T-cell or Hodgkin-like PTLD are usually treated with standard lymphoma-specific chemotherapy regimens.[<a href="#cit/section_9.28">

763828</a>

7639-<a href="#cit/section_9.31">

764031</a>

7641]</p>

7642<p id="_1697" tabindex="-1">

7643 Antirejection therapy is usually decreased or discontinued when chemotherapy is given to avoid excessive toxicity. There are no data to guide the re-initiation of immunosuppressive therapy after chemotherapy treatment. There is little evidence of benefit for chemotherapy after SCT.</p>

7644<p id="_1926" tabindex="-1">

7645Adoptive immunotherapy with either donor lymphocytes or <em>

7646ex vivo</em>

7647&#8211;generated EBV-specific cytotoxic T-lymphocytes (EBV-CTLs) has been effective in treating patients with PTLD after blood or bone marrow transplantation.[<a href="#cit/section_9.32">

764832</a>

7649,<a href="#cit/section_9.33">

765033</a>

7651] To make this approach more broadly applicable, banks of <em>

7652off-the-shelf</em>

7653, third-party, allogeneic EBV-CTLs derived from healthy donors have been developed.[<a href="#cit/section_9.34">

765434</a>

7655,<a href="#cit/section_9.35">

765635</a>

7657] EBV-CTLs were evaluated in 46 patients with PTLD who had either progressed during rituximab treatment, not fully responded to rituximab treatment, or had a recurrence after a previous response. The following results were observed:[<a href="#cit/section_9.36">

765836</a>

7659] </p>

7660<div class="pdq-content-list">

7661<ul id="_2010">

7662<li>

7663The lymphomas were monomorphic diffuse large B-cell lymphomas in 24 of 33 patients who underwent HSCT and in 8 of 13 patients who underwent solid organ transplants.</li>

7664<li>

7665EBV-CTLs were selected for having at least one HLA allele shared between the donor EBV-CTLs and the lymphoma.</li>

7666<li>

7667 The objective response rate after one cycle of EBV-CTLs was 39% (9 complete responses [CRs] and 9 partial responses [PRs] among 46 patients). With additional cycles of therapy, 29 of 45 evaluable patients (64%) achieved CRs or sustained PRs.</li>

7668<li>

7669 Three of five recipients with progressive disease after their first cycle of EBV-CTLs achieved CRs or durable PRs after receiving EBV-CTLs from a different donor. </li>

7670<li>

7671Of 11 patients with CNS involvement, 5 achieved CRs and 4 achieved durable PRs. </li>

7672<li>

7673Factors associated with favorable response included previous treatment with rituximab only (i.e., no prior chemotherapy or radiation therapy), absence of extranodal disease, and more extensive <em>

7674in vivo</em>

7675 expansion of the EBV-CTLs.</li>

7676</ul>

7677</div>

7678</section>

7679<section id="_851">

7680<h4 id="_851_toc">

7681Treatment options under clinical evaluation for PTLD</h4>

7682<p id="_1830" tabindex="-1">

7683Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

7684NCI website</a>

7685. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

7686ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

7687.</p>

7688<p id="_1832" tabindex="-1">

7689The following is an example of a national and/or institutional clinical trial that is currently being conducted:</p>

7690<div class="pdq-content-list">

7691<ul id="_349">

7692<li>

7693<strong>

7694<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03394365">

7695NCT03394365</a>

7696</strong>

7697 (Tabelecleucel for Solid Organ or Allogeneic HSCT Participants With EBV-Positive PTLD After Failure of Rituximab or Rituximab and Chemotherapy [ALLELE])<strong>

7698:</strong>

7699 The purpose of this study is to determine the clinical benefit and characterize the safety profile of tabelecleucel for the treatment of EBV-positive PTLD in the setting of: (1) solid organ transplant after failure of rituximab and rituximab plus chemotherapy; or (2) allogeneic HSCT after failure of rituximab.</li>

7700</ul>

7701</div>

7702</section>

7703</section>

7704<section id="_2463">

7705<h3 id="_2463_toc">

7706Immunodeficiency Associated With ALL Therapy</h3>

7707<p id="_2455" tabindex="-1">

7708An international collaboration identified 95 cases of lymphoid neoplasms after the diagnosis of ALL.[<a href="#cit/section_9.37">

770937</a>

7710] Of these cases, 52 were characteristic of EBV-associated lymphoproliferative disease in the setting of immunodeficiency. These 52 cases were analyzed, along with 14 additional cases identified from the literature (n = 66). All cases occurred in the maintenance phase or within 6 months of completing maintenance (median, 14 months into maintenance therapy). Treatment strategies varied, but two-thirds of the patients were event-free survivors at 5 years. </p>

7711</section>

7868 <h2>

7869Rare NHL Occurring in Children</h2>

7870

7871 <div id="_section_853" class="pdq-sections">

7872<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

7873<h6>

7939</nav>

7940<p id="_304" tabindex="-1">

7941Low-grade or intermediate-grade mature B-cell lymphomas&#8212;such as small lymphocytic lymphoma, mucosa-associated lymphoid tissue (MALT) lymphoma, mantle cell lymphoma, myeloma, or follicular cell lymphoma&#8212;are rarely seen in children. The most recent World Health Organization (WHO) classification has identified pediatric-type follicular lymphoma and pediatric nodal marginal zone lymphoma as entities separate from their adult counterparts.[<a href="#cit/section_10.1">

79421</a>

7943] </p>

7944<p id="_352" tabindex="-1">

7945The Children's Oncology Group (COG) opened a registry study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01000753">

7946COG-ANHL04B1</a>

7947) to learn more about the clinical and pathologic features of these rare types of pediatric non-Hodgkin lymphoma (NHL). This study banks tissue for pathobiology studies and collects limited data on clinical presentation and outcome of therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_10.2">

79482</a>

7949]</p>

7950<section id="_1994">

7951<h3 id="_1994_toc">

7952Pediatric Gray Zone Lymphoma</h3>

7953<p id="_1995" tabindex="-1">

7954Gray zone lymphomas represent a hybrid malignancy, with an unclassifiable B-cell lymphoma and classical Hodgkin lymphoma, which may present together in an initial biopsy or sequentially as a relapse.[<a href="#cit/section_10.3">

79553</a>

7956]</p>

7957<p id="_2402" tabindex="-1">

7958 A retrospective case series study assessed the clinical characteristics and outcomes of six patients with gray zone lymphomas from Austria. The three male and three female patients ranged in age from 15 to 17 years. Two of the six patients had B symptoms and high lactate dehydrogenase (LDH) levels. All patients had mediastinal masses, and five of six patients had positive cervical/supraclavicular lymph nodes. Extranodal involvement of the pleura and lung was common. Initial therapy with B-cell NHL treatments in five patients led to a complete response (CR) in one patient and progressive disease and death in one patient. The other three patients relapsed with primarily classical Hodgkin lymphoma histology and required treatment with salvage therapy. All of these patients survived after high-dose therapies and stem cell transplantations. One patient who initially received Hodgkin lymphoma therapy achieved a CR and survived.[<a href="#cit/section_10.4">

79594</a>

7960]</p>

7961</section>

7962<section id="_855">

7963<h3 id="_855_toc">

7964Pediatric-Type Follicular Lymphoma</h3>

7965<p id="_506" tabindex="-1">

7966Pediatric-type follicular lymphoma is a disease that genetically and clinically differs from its adult counterpart and is recognized by the WHO classification as a separate entity from follicular lymphoma observed commonly in adults.[<a href="#cit/section_10.1">

79671</a>

7968] The genetic hallmark of adult follicular lymphoma is t(14;18)(q32;q21) involving <em class="gene-name">

7969BCL2</em>

7970; however, this translocation must be excluded to make the diagnosis of pediatric-type follicular lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_10.1">

79711</a>

7972,<a href="#cit/section_10.5">

79735</a>

7974-<a href="#cit/section_10.7">

79757</a>

7976] </p>

7977<p id="_2403" tabindex="-1">

7978 Pediatric-type follicular lymphoma predominantly occurs in males, is associated with a high proliferation rate, and is more likely to be localized disease.[<a href="#cit/section_10.5">

79795</a>

7980,<a href="#cit/section_10.8">

79818</a>

7982,<a href="#cit/section_10.9">

79839</a>

7984] In pediatric-type follicular lymphoma, a high-grade component (i.e., grade 3 with high proliferative index such as Ki-67 expression of &gt;30%) resembling diffuse large B-cell lymphoma can frequently be detected at initial diagnosis but does not indicate a more aggressive clinical course in children. Unlike follicular lymphoma in adults, pediatric-type follicular lymphoma does not transform to diffuse large B-cell lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_10.1">

79851</a>

7986,<a href="#cit/section_10.5">

79875</a>

7988,<a href="#cit/section_10.7">

79897</a>

7990,<a href="#cit/section_10.9">

79919</a>

7992,<a href="#cit/section_10.10">

799310</a>

7994] Limited-stage disease is observed with pediatric-type follicular lymphoma, with cervical lymph nodes and tonsils as common sites, but disease has also occurred in extranodal sites such as the testis, kidney, gastrointestinal tract, and parotid gland.[<a href="#cit/section_10.5">

79955</a>

7996-<a href="#cit/section_10.7">

79977</a>

7998,<a href="#cit/section_10.10">

799910</a>

8000-<a href="#cit/section_10.12">

800112</a>

8002]</p>

8003<section id="_1911">

8004<h4 id="_1911_toc">

8005Tumor biology</h4>

8006<section id="_sm_CDR0000794337_1760">

8007<h5 id="_sm_CDR0000794337_1760_toc">

8008Genomics of pediatric-type follicular lymphoma</h5>

8009<p id="_sm_CDR0000794337_1762" tabindex="-1">

8010Pediatric-type follicular lymphoma and nodal marginal zone lymphoma are rare indolent B-cell lymphomas that are clinically and molecularly distinct from these tumor types in adults. </p>

8011<div class="pdq-content-list">

8012<ul id="_sm_CDR0000794337_1767">

8013<li>

8014The pediatric types lack <em class="gene-name">

8015BCL2</em>

8016 and <em class="gene-name">

8017IRF4</em>

8018 rearrangements, resulting in IRF4 expression.[<a href="#cit/section_10.13">

801913</a>

8020] </li>

8021<li>

8022<em class="gene-name">

8023BCL6</em>

8024 and <em class="gene-name">

8025MYC</em>

8026 rearrangements are also not present.[<a href="#cit/section_10.13">

802713</a>

8028] </li>

8029<li>

8030The <em class="gene-name">

8031TNFSFR14</em>

8032 mutations are common in pediatric-type follicular lymphoma, and they appear to occur with similar frequency in adult follicular lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_10.9">

80339</a>

8034,<a href="#cit/section_10.14">

803514</a>

8036] </li>

8037<li>

8038<em class="gene-name">

8039MAP2K1</em>

8040 mutations, which are uncommon in adults, are observed in as many as 43% of pediatric-type follicular lymphoma cases. Other genes (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

8041MAPK1</em>

8042 and <em class="gene-name">

8043RRAS</em>

8044) have been found to be mutated in cases without <em class="gene-name">

8045MAP2K1</em>

8046 mutations, suggesting that the MAP kinase pathway is important in the pathogenesis of pediatric-type follicular lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_10.15">

804715</a>

8048,<a href="#cit/section_10.16">

804916</a>

8050] </li>

8051<li>

8052Mutations in <em>

8053IRF8</em>

8054, mutations in <em class="gene-name">

8055KMT2C</em>

8056, and abnormalities in chromosome 1p have also been observed in pediatric-type follicular lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_10.14">

805714</a>

8058,<a href="#cit/section_10.17">

805917</a>

8060-<a href="#cit/section_10.19">

806119</a>

8062]</li>

8063</ul>

8064</div>

8065</section>

8066</section>

8067<section id="_857">

8068<h4 id="_857_toc">

8069Treatment options for pediatric-type follicular lymphoma</h4>

8070<p id="_596" tabindex="-1">

8071Pediatric-type follicular lymphoma is rare in children, with only case reports and small case series to guide therapy. The outcomes of patients with pediatric-type follicular lymphoma are excellent, with an event-free survival (EFS) rate of about 95%.[<a href="#cit/section_10.5">

80725</a>

8073,<a href="#cit/section_10.7">

80747</a>

8075-<a href="#cit/section_10.10">

807610</a>

8077,<a href="#cit/section_10.12">

807812</a>

8079] Unlike in adult follicular lymphoma, the clinical course in pediatric patients is not dominated by relapses.[<a href="#cit/section_10.5">

80805</a>

8081,<a href="#cit/section_10.7">

80827</a>

8083,<a href="#cit/section_10.10">

808410</a>

8085,<a href="#cit/section_10.11">

808611</a>

8087] </p>

8088<p id="_1057" tabindex="-1">

8089Treatment options for pediatric-type follicular lymphoma include the following:</p>

8090<div class="pdq-content-list">

8091<ol id="_1058">

8092<li>

8093Surgery only.</li>

8094<li>

8095Multiagent chemotherapy with or without rituximab.</li>

8096</ol>

8097</div>

8098<p id="_858" tabindex="-1">

8099Studies suggest that for children with stage I disease who had a complete resection, a watch-and-wait approach without chemotherapy may be indicated. Patients with higher-stage disease also have a favorable outcome with low-intensity and intermediate-intensity chemotherapy, with an EFS rate of 94% and an overall survival (OS) rate of 100% (2-year median follow-up).[<a href="#cit/section_10.2">

81002</a>

8101,<a href="#cit/section_10.5">

81025</a>

8103,<a href="#cit/section_10.8">

81048</a>

8105,<a href="#cit/section_10.9">

81069</a>

8107] Although the number of pediatric patients with pediatric follicular-type lymphoma is too small to perform meaningful clinical trials, studies of adult patients with follicular lymphoma support the addition of rituximab to standard treatment regimens. </p>

8108<p id="_1760" tabindex="-1">

8109For patients with <em class="gene-name">

8110BCL2</em>

8111-rearranged tumors, treatment similar to that of adult patients with follicular lymphoma is administered.</p>

8112<p id="_2446" tabindex="-1">

8113For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/b-cell-lymphoma-treatment-pdq#_14">

8114Follicular Lymphoma (Grades 1&#8211;3a)</a>

8115 section in B-Cell Non-Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment.</p>

8116</section>

8117</section>

8118<section id="_859">

8119<h3 id="_859_toc">

8120Marginal Zone Lymphoma (Including MALT Lymphoma)</h3>

8121<p id="_468" tabindex="-1">

8122Marginal zone lymphoma is a type of indolent lymphoma that is rare in pediatric patients. Marginal zone lymphoma can present as nodal or extranodal disease and almost always as low-stage (stage I or stage II) disease. It is unclear whether the marginal zone lymphoma that is observed in pediatric patients is clinicopathologically different from the disease that is observed in adults. Most extranodal marginal zone lymphoma in pediatrics presents as MALT lymphoma and may be associated with <em>

8123Helicobacter pylori</em>

8124 (gastrointestinal) or <em>

8125Chlamydophila psittaci</em>

8126 (conjunctival), previously called <em>

8127Chlamydia psittaci</em>

8128.[<a href="#cit/section_10.20">

812920</a>

8130,<a href="#cit/section_10.21">

813121</a>

8132] </p>

8133<section id="_861">

8134<h4 id="_861_toc">

8135Treatment options for marginal zone lymphoma (including MALT lymphoma)</h4>

8136<p id="_1705" tabindex="-1">

8137Treatment options for marginal zone lymphoma (including MALT lymphoma) include the following:</p>

8138<div class="pdq-content-list">

8139<ol id="_1706">

8140<li>

8141Surgery only.</li>

8142<li>

8143Radiation therapy.</li>

8144<li>

8145Rituximab with or without chemotherapy.</li>

8146<li>

8147Antibiotic therapy, for MALT lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_10.21">

814821</a>

8149,<a href="#cit/section_10.22">

815022</a>

8151]</li>

8152</ol>

8153</div>

8154<p id="_862" tabindex="-1">

8155Most pediatric patients with marginal zone lymphomas require no more than local therapy involving curative surgery and/or radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_10.20">

815620</a>

8157,<a href="#cit/section_10.23">

815823</a>

8159] Treatment of patients with MALT lymphoma of the gastric mucosa may also include antibiotic therapy, which is considered standard treatment in adults. However, the use of antibiotic therapy in children has not been well studied because there are so few cases. </p>

8160<p id="_1878" tabindex="-1">

8161Evidence (treatment of marginal zone lymphoma):</p>

8162<div class="pdq-content-list">

8163<ol id="_1879">

8164<li>

8165In the largest retrospective study of pediatric patients (aged 18 years or younger) with marginal zone lymphoma (N = 66), the following was reported:[<a href="#cit/section_10.24">

816624</a>

8167][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

8168Level of evidence C1</a>

8169]<div class="pdq-content-list">

8170<ul id="_1880">

8171<li>

8172The overall 5-year EFS rate was 70%.</li>

8173<li>

8174 The OS rate was 98%.</li>

8175<li>

8176 Patients primarily fell into the following two WHO-defined groups:<div class="pdq-content-list">

8177<ul id="_2404">

8178<li>

8179Nodal (32%): Nearly all patients were male, with localized primary tumors in the head and neck. The treatment for all patients was resection (complete or incomplete) followed by observation. The EFS rate was 94%, and the OS rate was 100%.</li>

8180<li>

8181Extranodal (67%): 57% of patients were male, and 27% of patients had a preexisting condition, which was immune compromising in most patients. The treatment options included chemotherapy, radiation, rituximab, resection, and observation. The EFS rate was 64%, and the OS rate was 97%. The only two deaths resulted from treatment-related complications of stem cell transplantation; both patients had an underlying immunodeficiency. Of note, 9 of 12 patients with extranodal marginal zone lymphoma who were managed with resection only remained in a first continuous complete remission with no further therapy; the other 3 patients who relapsed had their disease successfully salvaged.</li>

8182</ul>

8183</div>

8184</li>

8185</ul>

8186</div>

8187</li>

8188</ol>

8189</div>

8190<p id="_1881" tabindex="-1">

8191Although the number of pediatric patients with MALT lymphoma is too small to perform meaningful clinical trials, studies of adult patients support the use of rituximab with or without chemotherapy. For more information, see the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/b-cell-lymphoma-treatment-pdq#_40">

8192Marginal Zone Lymphoma</a>

8193 section in B-Cell Non-Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment.</p>

8194<p id="_1060" tabindex="-1">

8195Intralesional interferon-alpha for conjunctival MALT lymphoma has been studied in trials.[<a href="#cit/section_10.25">

819625</a>

8197]</p>

8198</section>

8199</section>

8200<section id="_863">

8201<h3 id="_863_toc">

8202Primary Central Nervous System (CNS) Lymphoma</h3>

8203<p id="_384" tabindex="-1">

8204Other types of NHL that are rare in adults and are exceedingly rare in pediatric patients include primary CNS lymphoma. Because of the small numbers of patients, it is difficult to ascertain whether the disease observed in children is the same as the disease observed in adults. </p>

8205<p id="_1061" tabindex="-1">

8206Reports suggest that the outcome of pediatric patients with primary CNS lymphoma (OS rate, 70%&#8211;80%) may be superior to that of adults with primary CNS lymphoma.[<a href="#cit/section_10.26">

820726</a>

8208-<a href="#cit/section_10.29">

820929</a>

8210] </p>

8211<p id="_1698" tabindex="-1">

8212Most children have diffuse large B-cell lymphoma, although other histologies have been observed. </p>

8213<section id="_865">

8214<h4 id="_865_toc">

8215Treatment options for primary CNS lymphoma</h4>

8216<p id="_1699" tabindex="-1">

8217Treatment options for primary CNS lymphoma include the following:</p>

8218<div class="pdq-content-list">

8219<ol id="_1700">

8220<li>

8221Chemotherapy.</li>

8222<li>

8223Radiation therapy.</li>

8224</ol>

8225</div>

8226<p id="_866" tabindex="-1">

8227Therapy with high-dose intravenous methotrexate and cytosine arabinoside is the most successful, and intrathecal chemotherapy may be needed only when malignant cells are present in the cerebrospinal fluid.[<a href="#cit/section_10.30">

822830</a>

8229] </p>

8230<p id="_1701" tabindex="-1">

8231There are case reports describing the administration of repeated doses of intraventricular rituximab in patients with refractory primary CNS lymphoma, with excellent

8232results reported.[<a href="#cit/section_10.31">

823331</a>

8234,<a href="#cit/section_10.32">

823532</a>

8236] This apparently good outcome needs to be confirmed, and similar results have not been observed in adults. It is generally believed that rituximab does not cross the blood-brain barrier. </p>

8237<p id="_2021" tabindex="-1">

8238Among patients who have a partial response to induction therapy, and particularly those who are not eligible for transplant, reduced-dose whole-brain radiation therapy with a boost to residual disease may be a viable treatment approach that merits further investigation.[<a href="#cit/section_10.33">

823933</a>

8240,<a href="#cit/section_10.34">

824134</a>

8242]</p>

8243<p id="_1062" tabindex="-1">

8244For more information about treatment options for non&#8211;AIDS-related primary CNS lymphoma, see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/primary-cns-lymphoma-treatment-pdq">

8245Primary CNS Lymphoma Treatment</a>

8246.</p>

8247</section>

8248</section>

8249<section id="_867">

8250<h3 id="_867_toc">

8251Peripheral T-cell Lymphoma</h3>

8252<p id="_437" tabindex="-1">

8253Peripheral T-cell lymphoma, excluding anaplastic large cell lymphoma, is rare in children.</p>

8254<p id="_1063" tabindex="-1">

8255Mature T-cell/natural killer (NK)&#8211;cell lymphoma or peripheral T-cell lymphoma has a postthymic phenotype (e.g., terminal deoxynucleotidyl transferase negative), usually expresses CD4 or CD8, and has rearrangement of T-cell receptor genes, either alpha-beta and/or gamma-delta chains. The most common phenotype observed in children is peripheral T-cell lymphoma&#8211;not otherwise specified, although angioimmunoblastic lymphoma, enteropathy-associated lymphoma (associated with celiac disease), subcutaneous panniculitis-like lymphoma, angiocentric lymphoma, and extranodal NK/T-cell peripheral T-cell lymphoma have been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_10.35">

825635</a>

8257-<a href="#cit/section_10.39">

825839</a>

8259]</p>

8260<p id="_2022" tabindex="-1">

8261Extranodal NK/T-cell lymphoma is a rare subtype of NHL, constitutes between 0.2% and 1.6% of newly diagnosed cases of NHL in children and adolescents, and is closely associated with the Epstein-Barr virus (EBV).[<a href="#cit/section_10.40">

826240</a>

8263] The incidence varies by region; the incidence is between 3% and 10% in Asian countries and 1% in western countries.[<a href="#cit/section_10.41">

826441</a>

8265] The common primary tumor sites are the nasal cavity and paranasal sinuses.[<a href="#cit/section_10.42">

826642</a>

8267] A standard treatment for pediatric patients has not been established. A series of 34 patients were treated with chemotherapy with or without asparaginase. At a median follow-up of 54 months, patients with lower-stage (I/II) disease had 5-year EFS and OS rates of 66.2% and 94.7%, respectively, compared with 26.0% and 42.3% for patients with stage III/IV disease. For all patients, there was no statistically significant difference in outcomes between patients who received asparaginase-containing regimens and those who did not. All patients with stage I/II disease received radiation therapy, whereas only 4 of 13 patients with higher-stage disease received radiation therapy. The 5-year EFS rate was 66.7% for stage III/IV patients who received hematopoietic stem cell transplantation (HSCT) and 11.1% for patients who did not receive HSCT (<em>

8268P</em>

8269 = .054).[<a href="#cit/section_10.43">

827043</a>

8271][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

8272Level of evidence C1</a>

8273]</p>

8274<p id="_986" tabindex="-1">

8275Although very rare, gamma-delta hepatosplenic T-cell lymphoma may be seen in children.[<a href="#cit/section_10.38">

827638</a>

8277] This tumor has also been associated with children and adolescents who have Crohn disease and have been treated with immunosuppressive therapy; this lymphoma has been fatal in all cases.[<a href="#cit/section_10.44">

827844</a>

8279]</p>

8280<section id="_869">

8281<h4 id="_869_toc">

8282Treatment options for peripheral T-cell lymphoma</h4>

8283<p id="_620" tabindex="-1">

8284 Optimal therapy for peripheral T-cell lymphoma is unclear for both pediatric and adult patients. </p>

8285<p id="_1702" tabindex="-1">

8286Treatment options for peripheral T-cell lymphoma include the following:</p>

8287<div class="pdq-content-list">

8288<ol id="_1703">

8289<li>

8290Chemotherapy.</li>

8291<li>

8292Radiation therapy.</li>

8293<li>

8294Allogeneic or autologous HSCT.</li>

8295</ol>

8296</div>

8297<p id="_1064" tabindex="-1">

8298There have been four retrospective analyses of treatment and outcome for pediatric patients with peripheral T-cell lymphoma.</p>

8299<p id="_2407" tabindex="-1">

8300Evidence (treatment of peripheral T-cell lymphoma):</p>

8301<div class="pdq-content-list">

8302<ol id="_2408">

8303<li>

8304The United Kingdom Children's Cancer Study Group (UKCCSG) examined 25 children diagnosed with peripheral T-cell lymphoma over a 20-year period and reported the following:[<a href="#cit/section_10.35">

830535</a>

8306] <div class="pdq-content-list">

8307<ul id="_2409">

8308<li>

8309A 5-year survival rate of approximately 50%.</li>

8310<li>

8311The UKCCSG also observed that the use of acute lymphoblastic leukemia&#8211;like therapy, instead of NHL therapy, produced a superior outcome.</li>

8312</ul>

8313</div>

8314</li>

8315<li>

8316The COG reported on 20 patients older than 8 years who were treated on Pediatric Oncology Group NHL trials.[<a href="#cit/section_10.36">

831736</a>

8318] <div class="pdq-content-list">

8319<ul id="_2410">

8320<li>

8321Eight of ten patients with low-stage disease achieved long-term disease-free survival compared with only four of ten patients with high-stage disease.</li>

8322</ul>

8323</div>

8324</li>

8325<li>

8326In a study of Japanese children with peripheral T-cell lymphoma (N = 21), treatment included chemotherapy (n = 18), radiation therapy (n = 2), and autologous (n = 2) and allogeneic (n = 9) HSCT.[<a href="#cit/section_10.45">

832745</a>

8328]<div class="pdq-content-list">

8329<ul id="_2411">

8330<li>

8331The 5-year OS rate was 85.2%</li>

8332</ul>

8333</div>

8334</li>

8335<li>

8336The Berlin-Frankfurt-M&#252;nster study group reported 38 cases of peripheral T-cell lymphoma acquired over a 26-year period.[<a href="#cit/section_10.38">

833738</a>

8338][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

8339Level of evidence C2</a>

8340] <div class="pdq-content-list">

8341<ul id="_2412">

8342<li>

8343Patients with peripheral T-cell lymphoma&#8211;not otherwise specified (n = 18), most with advanced disease (stage III [n = 10] and stage IV [n = 5]), were usually treated with anaplastic large cell lymphoma protocols and had a 10-year EFS rate of 61%.</li>

8344<li>

8345Patients with NK/T-cell lymphoma (n = 9) fared poorly, with a 10-year EFS rate of 17%. </li>

8346<li>

8347This series also included five patients with hepatosplenic T-cell lymphoma and five patients with subcutaneous panniculitis-like T-cell lymphoma.</li>

8348</ul>

8349</div>

8350</li>

8351</ol>

8352</div>

8353<p id="_2487" tabindex="-1">

8354For more information about the treatment of adults, see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/peripheral-t-cell-lymphoma-pdq">

8355Peripheral T-Cell Non-Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment</a>

8356.</p>

8357</section>

8358</section>

8359<section id="_873">

8360<h3 id="_873_toc">

8361Cutaneous T-cell Lymphoma/Mycosis Fungoides</h3>

8362<section id="_2430">

8363<h4 id="_2430_toc">

8364Cutaneous T-cell lymphoma</h4>

8365<section id="_2478">

8366<h5 id="_2478_toc">

8367General information about cutaneous T-cell lymphoma</h5>

8368<p id="_722" tabindex="-1">

8369Primary cutaneous lymphomas are very rare in pediatric patients (1 case per 1 million person-years), but the incidence increases in adolescents and young adults. All histologies of NHL have been observed to involve the skin. More than 80% of cutaneous lymphomas are the T-cell or NK-cell phenotype.[<a href="#cit/section_10.46">

837046</a>

8371] </p>

8372<p id="_1754" tabindex="-1">

8373Subcutaneous panniculitic T-cell lymphomas (SPTCL) are very rare lymphomas with panniculitis-like infiltration of subcutaneous tissue by cytotoxic T-cells. SPTCL account for less than 1% of all peripheral T-cell lymphomas.[<a href="#cit/section_10.47">

837447</a>

8375-<a href="#cit/section_10.49">

837649</a>

8377] SPTCL can be observed with malignant T cells, expressing alpha-beta chain T-cell receptor or gamma-delta T-cell receptor rearrangements.</p>

8378<p id="_1756" tabindex="-1">

8379In adults, the gamma-delta subtype of SPTCL is associated with a more aggressive course and a worse prognosis than the alpha-beta subtype of SPTCL.[<a href="#cit/section_10.50">

838050</a>

8381] Morbidity and mortality are frequently related to the development of hemophagocytic syndrome, which was reported in one series in adults to occur in 17% of patients with alpha-beta SPTCL and in 45% of patients with gamma-delta SPTCL. The 5-year OS rate is 82% for patients with alpha-beta SPTCL and 11% for patients with gamma-delta SPTCL.[<a href="#cit/section_10.50">

838250</a>

8383] </p>

8384<p id="_2464" tabindex="-1">

8385SPTCL is heterogeneous in the pediatric age group and does not necessarily follow the course observed in adults. In a retrospective series of 18 children (median age, 11.1 years; range, 0.52&#8211;14.7 years, with 3 children aged &lt;1 year), most presented with single or multiple subcutaneous nodules or patchy skin lesions on the limbs and/or trunk. Most of the patients also had fever, asthenia, and weight loss. Four out of five patients screened were positive for the <em class="gene-name">

8386HAVCR2</em>

8387 gene mutation in the T-cell immunoglobulin domain and mucin domain 3 (TIM-3) lineages.[<a href="#cit/section_10.51">

838851</a>

8389][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

8390Level of evidence C3</a>

8391] Seven cases were associated with hemophagocytic syndrome, similar to 7 of 11 pediatric cases in another series.[<a href="#cit/section_10.52">

839252</a>

8393]; [<a href="#cit/section_10.51">

839451</a>

8395][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

8396Level of evidence C3</a>

8397]</p>

8398<p id="_987" tabindex="-1">

8399The diagnosis of primary cutaneous anaplastic large cell lymphoma can be difficult to distinguish pathologically from more benign diseases such as lymphomatoid papulosis.[<a href="#cit/section_10.53">

840053</a>

8401] Primary cutaneous lymphomas are now thought to represent a spectrum of disorders, distinguished by clinical presentation. </p>

8402</section>

8403<section id="_875">

8404<h5 id="_875_toc">

8405Treatment options for cutaneous T-cell lymphoma</h5>

8406<p id="_988" tabindex="-1">

8407Because of the rarity of cutaneous T-cell lymphoma, no standard treatments have been established. Management and treatment of patients with cutaneous T-cell lymphoma should be individualized and, in some cases, watchful waiting may be appropriate. Treatment may only be necessary if hemophagocytic syndrome develops.[<a href="#cit/section_10.54">

840854</a>

8409]</p>

8410<p id="_2462" tabindex="-1">

8411There is no standard treatment regimen for SPTCL. Spontaneous remissions have been observed, particularly in younger children. Older children, however, may have a course of disease that is complicated by hemophagocytic syndrome. First-line treatment consists of either chemotherapy or immunomodulatory drugs. Chemotherapy was the mainstay of treatment before 2019. Immunomodulatory therapy or observational follow-up became the mainstay of treatment after this time. Immunomodulatory agents include steroids combined with cyclosporine A or ruxolitinib. In a series of 18 patients, the CR rate was 71.4% for patients treated with these immunomodulatory agents.[<a href="#cit/section_10.51">

841251</a>

8413][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

8414Level of evidence C3</a>

8415]</p>

8416<p id="_665" tabindex="-1">

8417An oral retinoid (bexarotene) has been reported to be active against SPTCL in a series of 15 patients from three institutions.[<a href="#cit/section_10.55">

841855</a>

8419] In a series of 11 pediatric patients, aggressive polychemotherapy was used in all patients. Nine of 11 patients sustained clinical remission, with a median follow-up of 3.5 years.[<a href="#cit/section_10.52">

842052</a>

8421] Additional treatment options include high-dose steroids, bexarotene, denileukin diftitox, multiagent chemotherapy, and HSCT.[<a href="#cit/section_10.49">

842249</a>

8423,<a href="#cit/section_10.54">

842454</a>

8425-<a href="#cit/section_10.59">

842659</a>

8427]</p>

8428<p id="_1704" tabindex="-1">

8429Primary cutaneous anaplastic large cell lymphoma usually does not express <em class="gene-name">

8430ALK</em>

8431 and may be treated successfully with surgical resection and/or local radiation therapy without systemic chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_10.60">

843260</a>

8433] There are reports of surgery alone also being curative for patients with <em class="gene-name">

8434ALK</em>

8435-positive cutaneous anaplastic large cell lymphoma, but extensive staging and vigilant follow-up is required.[<a href="#cit/section_10.61">

843661</a>

8437,<a href="#cit/section_10.62">

843862</a>

8439]</p>

8440</section>

8441</section>

8442<section id="_2429">

8443<h4 id="_2429_toc">

8444Mycosis fungoides</h4>

8445<section id="_2479">

8446<h5 id="_2479_toc">

8447General information about mycosis fungoides</h5>

8448<p id="_1708" tabindex="-1">

8449Mycosis fungoides is rarely reported in children and adolescents,[<a href="#cit/section_10.63">

845063</a>

8451-<a href="#cit/section_10.66">

845266</a>

8453] accounting for about 0.5% to 7% of all cases. In a systematic review of 571 children and adolescents with mycosis fungoides, the mean age of diagnosis was 12.2 years, and the mean age at onset was 8.6 years.</p>

8454<p id="_2447" tabindex="-1">

8455Compared with adults, pediatric patients are diagnosed with an earlier stage of mycosis fungoides and have a higher rate of atypical presentations, specifically the hypopigmented variant.[<a href="#cit/section_10.65">

845665</a>

8457] One of the largest series of pediatric patients with mycosis fungoides (n = 71; diagnosed aged &lt;18 years) was followed for a mean of 9.2 years (range, 1&#8211;24 years).[<a href="#cit/section_10.65">

845865</a>

8459]</p>

8460<div class="pdq-content-list">

8461<ul id="_2448">

8462<li>

8463Sixty-nine of 71 patients had early-stage disease. The mean age of symptom onset was 8 years, and the mean age at diagnosis was 11 years. There was a mean diagnostic delay of 3 years.</li>

8464<li>

8465The most common presentation was hypopigmented lesions (55%), followed by folliculotropic lesions (42%) and classical mycosis fungoides (39%), alone or in combination.</li>

8466<li>

8467The head and neck region was more frequently involved in early-stage folliculotropic mycosis fungoides (43%), compared with non-folliculotropic mycosis fungoides (12%) (<em>

8468P</em>

8469 = .004). Pruritis was more common in folliculotropic mycosis fungoides than in non-folliculotropic mycosis fungoides.</li>

8470<li>

8471CD4 predominance was seen in 73% of patients with early-stage folliculotropic mycosis fungoides, whereas CD8 predominance was seen in 49% of patients with non-folliculotropic mycosis fungoides.</li>

8472<li>

8473Bacterial infection was rare in pediatric patients, in contrast to the frequency in adults.</li>

8474</ul>

8475</div>

8476<p id="_2449" tabindex="-1">

8477Factors associated with worse overall 10-year survival were delay in establishing the correct diagnosis, granulomatous slack skin, granulomatous mycosis fungoides, history of organ transplant, and stage 2 disease at the time of diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_10.67">

847867</a>

8479][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

8480Level of evidence C3</a>

8481] For information about the treatment of adults, see <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/mycosis-fungoides-treatment-pdq">

8482Mycosis Fungoides (Including S&#233;zary Syndrome) Treatment</a>

8483.</p>

8484</section>

8485<section id="_2431">

8486<h5 id="_2431_toc">

8487Treatment options for mycosis fungoides</h5>

8488<p id="_2432" tabindex="-1">

8489Mycosis fungoides in pediatric patients may respond to various therapies, including topical steroids, retinoids, radiation therapy, or phototherapy (e.g., narrowband UVB treatment [NBUVB]), but remission may not be durable.[<a href="#cit/section_10.68">

849068</a>

8491-<a href="#cit/section_10.71">

849271</a>

8493] In a retrospective series of 71 pediatric patients with mycosis fungoides, the overall response rate (CR + partial remission) was 88%. However, CR was achieved in only 40% of patients initially.[<a href="#cit/section_10.65">

849465</a>

8495][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

8496Level of evidence C3</a>

8497] </p>

8498<div class="pdq-content-list">

8499<ul id="_2450">

8500<li>

8501NBUVB monotherapy was the most commonly administered treatment to pediatric patients with early-stage nonfolliculotropic mycosis fungoides. The CR rate was 63% for these patients. In contrast, pediatric patients with early-stage folliculotropic mycosis fungoides had a CR rate of 29% with NBUVB (<em>

8502P</em>

8503 = .04).</li>

8504<li>

8505UVA-based phototherapy, such as systemic psoralen plus UVA (PUVA), bath PUVA, or UVA combined with NBUVB, were the most commonly administered treatments to patients with folliculotropic mycosis fungoides, with CR rates of 60% versus 81% for patients with nonfolliculotropic mycosis fungoides (<em>

8506P</em>

8507 = .17).</li>

8508<li>

8509During a mean follow-up of 9.2 years, four of the patients with early-stage disease (6%) experienced progression of their disease stage. Two of these patients (both with folliculotropic mycosis fungoides) progressed to advanced-stage disease.</li>

8510</ul>

8511</div>

8512</section>

8513</section>

Haematologica 98 (1): 50-6, 2013.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=22773605&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=22773605&amp;dopt=Abstract">

8803

8804

8805 <section id="_217">

8806

8807 <h2>

8808Changes to This Summary (08/18/2023)</h2>

8809

8810 <div id="_section_217" class="pdq-sections">

8811<p id="_222" tabindex="-1">

8812The PDQ cancer information summaries are reviewed regularly and updated as

8813new information becomes available. This section describes the latest

8814changes made to this summary as of the date above.</p>

8815<p id="_2489" tabindex="-1">

8816<strong>

8817<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1">

8818General Information About Childhood Non-Hodgkin Lymphoma (NHL)</a>

8819</strong>

8820</p>

8821<p id="_2490" tabindex="-1">

8822Added <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_2006">

8823text</a>

8824 to state that the ANHL12P1 study evaluated the addition of either brentuximab vedotin or crizotinib to ALCL99 trial chemotherapy. The study confirmed the poor prognosis associated with minimal disseminated disease (MDD) in the peripheral blood at diagnosis. In each arm, approximately 40% of patients had MDD, and their 2-year event-free survival (EFS) rate was about 60%. The remaining patients without detectable MDD had a 2-year EFS rate of approximately 85% (cited 2023 Lowe et al. and 2021 Lowe et al. as references 46 and 47, respectively).</p>

8825<p id="_2491" tabindex="-1">

8826<strong>

8827<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_785">

8828Aggressive Mature B-cell NHL</a>

8829</strong>

8830</p>

8831<p id="_2492" tabindex="-1">

8832Added <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_979">

8833Lymphomes Malins B (LMB)&#8211;based chemotherapy plus rituximab</a>

8834 as a treatment option for primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma.</p>

8835<p id="_2493" tabindex="-1">

8836Added <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_2482">

8837text</a>

8838 about the results of the French prospective LMB2001 study that reported the outcomes of patients with primary mediastinal B-cell lymphoma who were treated with LMB-based chemotherapy without radiation therapy (cited Dourthe et al. as reference 86).</p>

8839<p id="_2494" tabindex="-1">

8840<strong>

8841<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_322">

8842Lymphoblastic Lymphoma</a>

8843</strong>

8844</p>

8845<p id="_2495" tabindex="-1">

8846Revised <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1029">

8847text</a>

8848 to state that three small series have treated patients with recurrent or refractory T-cell leukemia/lymphoma using nelarabine, cyclophosphamide, and etoposide (cited Whitlock et al. as reference 37).</p>

8849<p id="_2496" tabindex="-1">

8850Added <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1912">

8851text</a>

8852 about the results of a study that treated ten patients with nelarabine, cyclophosphamide, and etoposide.</p>

8853<p id="_2497" tabindex="-1">

8854<strong>

8855<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_330">

8856Anaplastic Large Cell Lymphoma</a>

8857</strong>

8858</p>

8859<p id="_2498" tabindex="-1">

8860Revised <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_2427">

8861text</a>

8862 to update the results of one arm of the Children's Oncology Group ANHL12P1 study that added brentuximab vedotin to the ALCL99 trial chemotherapy backbone.</p>

8863<p id="_2499" tabindex="-1">

8864Added <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_2488">

8865text</a>

8866 about the results of the second arm of the ANHL12P1 study that added crizotinib to the ALCL99 trial chemotherapy backbone (cited Lowe et al. as reference 25).</p>

8867<p id="_2500" tabindex="-1">

8868<strong>

8869<a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_853">

8870Rare NHL Occurring in Children</a>

8871</strong>

8872</p>

8873<p id="_2503" tabindex="-1">

8874Revised <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1911">

8875text</a>

8876 to state that pediatric-type follicular lymphoma and nodal marginal zone lymphoma are rare indolent B-cell lymphomas that are clinically and molecularly distinct from these tumor types in adults.</p>

8877<p id="_2501" tabindex="-1">

8878Revised <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1911">

8879text</a>

8880 to state that mutations in <em class="gene-name">

8881IRF8</em>

8882, mutations in <em class="gene-name">

8883KMT2C</em>

8884, and abnormalities in chromosome 1p have also been observed in pediatric-type follicular lymphoma (cited Lim et al. as reference 19).</p>

8885<p id="_2502" tabindex="-1">

8886Added Welfringer-Morin et al. as <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_1708">

8887reference 66</a>

8888.</p>

8889<p id="_disclaimerHP_3" tabindex="-1">

8890This summary is written and maintained by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment">

8891PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board</a>

8892, which is

8893editorially independent of NCI. The summary reflects an independent review of

8894the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or NIH. More

8895information about summary policies and the role of the PDQ Editorial Boards in

8896maintaining the PDQ summaries can be found on the <a href="/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq#_AboutThis_1">

8897About This PDQ Summary</a>

8898 and <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq">

8899PDQ&#174; Cancer Information for Health Professionals</a>

8900 pages.

8901</p>

8902</div>

8908 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

8909

8910 <h2>

8911About This PDQ Summary</h2>

8912

8913 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

8914<section id="_AboutThis_2">

8915<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

8916Purpose of This Summary</h3>

8917<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

8918This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about the treatment of childhood non-Hodgkin lymphoma. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

8919</section>

8920<section id="_AboutThis_4">

8921<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

8922Reviewers and Updates</h3>

8923<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

8924This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment">

8925PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board</a>

8926, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

8927<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

8928 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

8929<div class="pdq-content-list">

8930<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

8931<li>

8932be discussed at a meeting,</li>

8933<li>

8934be cited with text, or</li>

8935<li>

8936replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

8937</ul>

8938</div>

8939<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

8940Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

8941<p id="" tabindex="-1">

8942The lead reviewers for Childhood Non-Hodgkin Lymphoma Treatment are:</p>

8943<div class="pdq-content-list">

8944<ul id="">

8945<li>

8946William L. Carroll, MD (Laura and Isaac Perlmutter Cancer Center at NYU Langone)</li>

8947<li>

8948Louis S. Constine, MD (James P. Wilmot Cancer Center at University of Rochester Medical Center)</li>

8949<li>

8950Alan Scott Gamis, MD, MPH (Children's Mercy Hospital)</li>

8951<li>

8952Thomas G. Gross, MD, PhD (National Cancer Institute)</li>

8953<li>

8954Michelle Hermiston, MD, PhD (University of California, San Francisco)</li>

8955<li>

8956Megan S. Lim, MD, PhD (University of Pennsylvania)</li>

8957<li>

8958Kenneth L. McClain, MD, PhD (Texas Children's Cancer Center and Hematology Service at Texas Children's Hospital)</li>

8959<li>

8960Arthur Kim Ritchey, MD (Children's Hospital of Pittsburgh of UPMC)</li>

8961<li>

8962Lisa Giulino Roth, MD (Weil Cornell Medical College)</li>

8963<li>

8964Nita Louise Seibel, MD (National Cancer Institute)</li>

8965<li>

8966Malcolm A. Smith, MD, PhD (National Cancer Institute)</li>

8967</ul>

8968</div>

8969<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

8970Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

8971Email Us</a>

8972. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

8973</section>

8974<section id="_AboutThis_10">

8975<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

8976Levels of Evidence</h3>

8977<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

8978Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/treatment">

8979formal evidence ranking system</a>

8980 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

8981</section>

8982<section id="_AboutThis_12">

8983<h3 id="_AboutThis_12_toc">

8984Permission to Use This Summary</h3>

8985<p id="_AboutThis_13" tabindex="-1">

8986PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as &#8220;NCI&#8217;s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].&#8221;</p>

8987<p id="_AboutThis_14" tabindex="-1">

8988The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:</p>

8989<p id="_AboutThis_15" tabindex="-1">

Available at: <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/types/lymphoma/hp/child-nhl-treatment-pdq">
